Then pour a small amount of hot water into the cannula, hold your hand, and revolve it with the pressure of your hand for a short while so that the edge of the cannula presses against the root of the corn and the hot water finds its way to the depth, to the root of the corn, and let the sick person wait for a while upon the soaking of the hot water for an hour.
Then release him, for the corn is eradicated with its roots.
Do this with each of them one by one, until you have treated all that are on the body.
Then treat them after they are eradicated, with that which generates flesh from ointments.
Concerning the method of cauterization in Tremor.
When tremor occurs in someone due to cold in the nerve, or from quartan fever, or otherwise: then it is necessary to cauterize him with four cauteries, or five, on the vertebrae of the back, and between each vertebra with a cautery, and on his chest with a cautery, and on his stomach with a cautery.
For tremor ceases with the olive cautery, and the health of the cold ailment is hastened.
Concerning the method of cauterizing Albathir.
Chapter LVII.
Sometimes foul things, that is, pustules, are expelled in the body of Albathir, which are formed from corrupted gross matter.
Therefore, when they appear, it is necessary first to cauterize every pustule on the head with a fine cautery made of myrtle wood, whose tip has been heated with fire. Or with the root of long aristolochia. Or with a lenticular cautery.
And sometimes they are cauterized at the beginning of their expulsion in this way, and they are not increased; and the superfluity causing them is broken up, and they are healed from them.
However, it is necessary for this to be done after evacuation with phlebotomy.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in the flow of blood from an artery in a wound.
Often a flow of blood from an artery occurs, which is cut in a wound, that happens externally, or in the incision of an abscess, or in the cauterization of a limb, and similar to them: and it becomes difficult to cut it off.
Therefore, when this happens to someone, then quickly apply your index finger to the opening of the artery, and press it firmly until the blood is obstructed beneath your finger, and nothing escapes from it.
Then place small, olive-shaped cauteries, and many larger ones, in the fire, and blow over them until they become very hot.
Then take one of them, either small or large, according to the wound and the place where the artery has been ruptured, and place the cautery on the vein itself, after you quickly remove your finger, and hold the cautery until the blood is cut off.
If it is expelled when you lift your finger from the opening of the artery, and you extinguish the cautery, then quickly take another cautery from the cauteries prepared in the fire.
Therefore do not cease to do this with one after another, until the blood is cut off.
And beware, lest you burn the nerve which is there, and another harm befall the sick person.
And know that when blood flows from the artery, then it is not possible to cut it off: especially when the artery is large, unless by one of four methods. Or with a cautery, as we have said. Or with extraction, when it is not extracted. For when it is extracted, its extremities contract, and the blood is cut off. Or it is tied with a strong ligature. Or let medicines of a nature to cut off the blood be placed upon it, and let it be tightened with a fitting constriction.
However, whoever performs its excision with a ligature and constriction with cloth, or by applying burning things, and similar methods, does not truly benefit it, except rarely.
If, however, this happens to someone and a physician is not present, nor medicine: then let him begin with the placement of the index finger on the opening of the vein itself, as we have recounted, and with a strong constriction of it, until the blood is blocked from the upper part of the wound and over the artery. And the finger is not to be removed, but constantly kept over it with very cold water, until the blood is congealed and thickened, and cut off.
And meanwhile, let him consider what it needs from a cautery, or from medicine.
Concerning incision, perforation, and phlebotomy in wounds, and similar matters.
We have already said in the first chapter, concerning every illness in which it is fitting to use cautery with fire, and burning medicine, and its occasions, its causes, and its instruments, and the forms of cauteries.
And I have placed there chapters from the head down to the foot.
And I will proceed in this chapter along the same path: so that the seeker might have his question alleviated.
And before I begin that, it is necessary for you, children, to learn that in this chapter there is fear from what is in the first chapter concerning cauterization.
And because of that, it is necessary that there be greater caution in it, since in the operation which is in this chapter, often evacuation occurs with blood, with which life stands at the opening of a vein, or incision on an abscess, or opening of an exit, or treatment of a wound, or extraction of an arrow, or incision on a stone, and similar things: from all of which trembling, and fear, and death in many of them fall.
And I warn you, lest that which is hesitation fall upon you, for sometimes the ways of men with the ways of illnesses fall upon you in this art. For among them is he who is angry with his illness, and death is light to him; because of the intensity that he finds from his illness and the length of its malice. And with illness is fear that signifies death.
And among them is he who gives his money, and enriches you with it, hoping for health, and his illness is harmful.
Therefore, you should not agree with him who comes to you from among them, whose manner is such. And let your caution be stronger than your greed and your rapacity. And do not presume upon anything of it, unless after certain knowledge, which verifies to you that to which praiseworthy success has come.
And use in the treatment of all your sick people, prognostication and indication of that to which health may properly be.
For in that is help for you in the acquisition of fame and glory, and of memory and praise.
May God, O children, inspire you with his understanding, and may he not take from you direction and inspiration, for it is in his hand.
And I have indeed ordered this Chapter, distinguishing it by chapters, in accordance with what preceded in the Chapter on Cauteries, from the neck down to the feet: so that your search for what you want from it, and what you need, might be eased.
Of the treatment of Water that gathers in the heads of children.
Chapter 1.
Know that this ailment frequently happens to children at birth, or when the midwife tightens the child's head without ease.
And sometimes it also happens from a hidden cause that is not known.
And I myself have not seen this sickness in others than in children, and death has hastened to all whom I have seen of them: for that reason it seemed to me to omit treatment in it.
And I myself saw one of them, a child whose head was filled with water, and the head grew larger every day, until he could not sit on himself because of the size of his head: and the moisture increased until he died.
And this moisture, indeed, either collects between the skin and the bone, or collects under the bone above the skin.
And if there is moisture between the skin and the bone, and the abscess is small, then it is necessary to make a single incision in the middle of the head, with a width of approximately two fingerbreadths, until the moisture flows out.
And this is the form of a lancet.
If therefore the moisture is more increased, and the abscess is larger, then place these two incisions, according to this form.
And if the moisture is under the bone, and its sign is that you see the sutures of the head open from every side, and water is depressed when you press it with your hand inward, and it is not hidden from you, then it is necessary to make three incisions in the middle of the head, according to this method.
And after the incision, extract all the moisture. Then bind the incisions with cloths and bandages. Then apply a fomentation of wine and oil to it until the fifth day.
Then loosen the bandage, and treat the wound with tents and ointments.
And do not allow the head to be bound with equality. And feed the weak person with all dry food of little moisture, until the limb is strengthened and healed.
And another method of incision is to consider where the size of the abscess and the collection of water appear.
Because sometimes it is more in the occiput, or in the forehead, or in the right, or in the left.
Therefore, ascend with the incision where the abscess and collection of water appear to you: and cut it according to what is possible for you.
And observe, lest you cut an artery, and a flow of blood occur, and the sick person die from that flow with the evacuation of moisture.
Of the incision of Arteries which are behind the ears.
Chapter II.
When cataracts occur to someone in the eyes, or in the chest, and it is prolonged, and treatment with medicines is not effective, then the final treatment for that is the incision of these two arteries.
Therefore, when you wish to cut them, the sick person should shave their head. Then rub the area with a rough cloth, so that the arteries appear. Then the sick person should tighten their neck with any cloth they have. Then consider where the vein beats. And the locations of both are the locations which are depressed behind the ears; and it is rarely hidden, except in certain people.
Then mark with ink on both; and cut both with a scalpel on both sides, the incision reaching the bone; and let that be across the width of the head.
And if you wish, insert the scalpel under the artery, and cut it upwards with the incision.
And let the length of the incision be like two joined fingers.
For when a vein is cut, blood emerges with a pulsating outflow, which leaps inwards with frequent jumps.
If, however, the artery is not apparent to the senses, then it is necessary to measure from the ear the amount of space of three fingers. Then mark with ink, and cut all the way to the bone.
And that which it is necessary to remove from the blood is six ounces, and perhaps you remove from it more, or less. All of that according to the quantity that appears to you from the weakness of the infirm and the fullness of his artery.
Then consider the wound. Then if anything remains on the bone from the sifac, then cut it, so that a hot abscess does not occur; then bind the wound with strips of linen cloth.
Then treat it with ointment, until it is healed.
Concerning the extraction of arteries, which are in the temples.
Chapter III.
When a person suffers from a chronic headache, or acute catarrhs due to acrid humors, and heat in the temple's tissues, or a severe, longstanding inflammation, and similar ailments: and is treated with the methods of medical treatment, and it does not help: then we have experience in these illnesses with the extraction of the arteries from the temples, or their cauterization, as we have described.
And the method of operation in extracting them is that the patient should shave the hairs that are in the temples, and direct himself towards the visible artery in the temple. For it indeed shows itself to you by its pulse, and rarely is hidden except in a few people, or by the intensity of cold.
If it is hidden from you, then the patient should tighten his head with any cloth of his: then rub the area with a rough cloth. Or warm the area with hot water, until it appears to you by clear appearance. Then take the spatula, which I have formed a little earlier. Then scrape the skin with it easily, until you reach the artery. Then insert a hook into it, and pull it upward, until you pull it out of the skin, and cause it to escape from the tunics that are beneath it on every side.
If however the artery is delicate: then wrap it with the end of the hook. Then cut a part of it according to the amount by which its extremities will be elongated and contracted, and so that no flow of blood occurs. For if it is not cut, and if it is not severed, the blood does not ascend completely.
Then evacuate from the blood six ounces, up to three.
If the artery is large: then it is necessary to tie it in two places with a strong, double thread, of silk, or of braided cord, so that putrefaction does not quickly occur in the wound before it consolidates, and that a flow of blood may not occur.
Then cut the excess that is between the two ligatures. Do it at that hour, or after another hour.
And if you wish, cauterize it with a cautery to the bone, with a surgical cautery, until its extremities are cut, and it will stand in place of the same work.
And it is a better work, unless if the sick person has a fever, or is of a hot disposition. Because cautery is from those things that help to end humidities. Therefore it is a better aid.
And after the extraction of the arteries, the place should be filled with old cotton, and pads made properly should be placed on it.
And after the solution, treat it with dry medicines that cause flesh to grow, and with bandages, until it is healed.
If, meanwhile, while you are working, a flow of blood from an artery occurs, then hurry to cut it off with cautery, or to fill the place with zegi, and bind your hand tightly until the blood is cut off. If none of those are present for you, then place your finger on the area, until the blood coagulates. And embrocate the area with water of intense cold, until the sharpness subsides, and it is bound as required.
And because it is easier and less troublesome than extracting the artery, it is to cauterize the vein with this cautery having two blades: and the sharpness of the two blades should be like an almond. However, they are of much less sharpness than two blades. Because if they are sharp, like two blades, the cold rushes to both [blades]: and they do not cut the flesh with speed. Therefore, since there is some thickness in them, the heat of the fire is retained in them, and the flesh is cut quickly: with this, therefore, the work is better than any other work, and easier, and more readily done.
And this is the form of cautery.
And let the space between the two blades be the thickness of a finger, after you have marked two places with ink: and place both [blades] intensely hot, until they reach the bone, and cut the vein in two places, so that what is between its extremities may be elongated. For they are not completely consolidated with this cautery.
Chapter Four.
Concerning the treatment of persistent acute lacrimal discharge from the eyes.
When the tears are persistent, and their flow is from veins situated above the external skull: and no medicinal treatment with medicines avails them, and you see the face of the sick person already flushed, and he feels a crawling sensation on his forehead, like the crawling of ants: and his eyes are weakened, moist, so that their eyelashes are already corroded with the corrosion: and his eyelids are heated from the intensity of the tears: then the ancients required this operation for their treatment, and they named that treatment, with the Sword.
And it is, that you should instruct the sick person to shave the hairs that are on his forehead. Then make three equally spaced incisions along the length of the forehead: and let the length of the incision be about two fingers, and one incision be equidistant from the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, and the second along the length a little from the movement of the temples, and the third on the other side: and beware of cutting the arteries that are on the two sides: and move your hand away from the joining of the two jawbones, and let the space of each incision be the amount of three joined fingers: and let you have a piece of prepared sponge, or many dried cloths, with which you dry the blood.
Then insert a probe with two sharp ends, whose following form is this. From the incision that is on the temple side, up to the second incision: and scrape off with it all the skin, which is between the two incisions, with the sifac, which is above the bone. Then do that also from the middle incision up to the second incision. Then remove this probe, and insert into the first incision again another instrument, called a sharp scalpel, with one side smooth, not sharp on the other side. And this is its form.
And place its sharp side towards the flesh attached with the skin, and its smooth side towards the bone: and drive it, until it reaches the middle fissure: and cut with it all the vessels, which descend from the head to the eyes: except that the incision continues up to that which appears of the skin.
Then do that in the middle fissure up to another fissure.
And after a quantity of blood has run, express the places from the clots of congealed blood.
Then place in every fissure a linen cloth from old cotton, and place over them cushions soaked in wine, and oil, or vinegar. And see that a hot abscess does not occur.
And on the third day, loosen the bandage, and administer much infusion with lukewarm water. Then treat it with basil ointment, and the other things with which wounds are treated: until it is healed.
Chapter V.
Concerning the treatment of acute tears and cataracts in the eyes from interior parts of the head.
When acute, sharp, persistent catarrhs befall someone; and you see his eyes weakened, small, and their appearance already impaired; and the eyelids ulcerated, and hairs fall from them, and there is a sharp, painful, injuring pain deep within the head, and a consequent sneezing: then from these occurrences, know that those matters and catarrhs do not come, except from many veins.
And the better treatment for them is this. And it is that you should order the sick person to shave his forehead. Then make one incision in the middle of the forehead, or another slightly across its width, and begin with the incision from the left temple to the right temple: and let the incision be right up to the bone: and you must pull your hand away from the temporal muscles, which move during chewing: until when the bone is exposed: and you shall dry up all the blood with a sponge.
Then separate the two lips of the incision with old cloth, or with linen strips. Then bind above with poultices: and soak the poultices with wine and oil, so that a hot abscess does not occur.
And when you loosen it, and see that the hot abscess is already lessening: then it is necessary that you rub the bone, until flesh begins to grow on it.
Then treat it with a drying regimen that causes flesh to grow: such as if you take two parts of wheat flour, and four parts of colophony: and prepare a plaster from them, and administer it for the growth of flesh in these wounds.
Chapter VI.
Concerning the treatment of that which falls into the Ear.
Everything that falls into the ear is one of four kinds: either a mineral stone, or something like a stone, such as iron and glass; or it is from a plant, such as chickpea and beans; or it is a liquid substance, such as water and vinegar, and things like them; or some animal.
Therefore, when a stone, or a type of stone from those which are not added or grow in the ear, falls into the ear, expose the ear to the sun.  If you see a stone, then instill into the ear something of violet oil, or sesame oil. Then administer its extraction, and cause sneezing with condiments, and close the nostrils at the time of the sneeze, after you place in a circle around the ear pieces of cloth or wool, and extend the ear upwards.
For often it departs with this treatment.
If however it does not depart, then administer its extraction with instruments, that is, with small tweezers; whose form is this.
If therefore it departs with the instruments, it is good.
And if not, then administer its extraction with a fine, single-hooked, thin instrument.
If therefore it does not depart through that, then make a tube of bronze, and insert the end of the tube into the ear hole very far, and seal that which is around the tube with softened wax mixed with oil, so that there is no passage to the wind except through the tube; then extract with your wind by a strong suction.
For often it departs with that which we have narrated. If however it does not depart in this way, then take a small amount of helkalimbat, or of viscous, that is, prepared glue, with which birds are caught, and place it on the end of the probe after you have carefully wrapped a bandage around it: then introduce it into the ear hole with ease, after you have dried the ear from moisture.
If therefore it does not depart with all that we have narrated, then hasten to incision, before a hot abscess or spasm occurs.
And the method of incision is, that a leeches be applied first to the cephalic vein: and extract from it blood according to the measure of his strength. Then make the sick person sit between your hands, and turn his ear upward, and make a small incision at the root of the ear, near his fat in the depressed area there: and let the incision be in the shape of a crescent, until you reach the stone: then remove it with whatever instruments you can.
Then stitch the incision from your hour with speed, and care for it until it heals.
If the thing falling into the ear is from one of the grains that grow and swell, then administer its extraction.
If, however, it does not yield to you to exit, then take a thin, slender spatula, according to this form.
And administer it with that to cut out that type of grain that falls into the ear, and do not do it when you think that grain has already been moistened by the vapor of the ear, until you put it into many fragments: then extract them with hooks, or with a fine tweezers, or with suction, as we said, for its extraction is thus relieved.
However, in the water that enters the ear, it is necessary that the weak person use sneezing with condiments first; and now he has filled his ear with some old cotton; and he lies on that ear in which the water is. If therefore it exits through that, it is good.
And if not, then take many stones, according to the length of a finger, thin, smooth, and heat them with fire for a short time, and let the weak person insert one of them into the hole of his ear: and let him jump on one foot on that side, and strike with another stone on the stone that is in the ear. Therefore, let him not cease doing that with stone and stone, until all the water exits.
And now the water exits in such a way that you take one from papyrus, or from reed, and insert one end of it into the ear; and light the other end with fire, until most of it is burned. Then repeat with another reed, and do this many times, until all the water exits: and extract it with a cannula according to what was stated before concerning the stone.
In extracting an animal entering it, if it is of small body, like a flea, and similar to it, treat it as I said in the division.
But if it is of large body, which appears to the senses, then administer its extraction with forceps and hooks; and its removal is easier than all those which are held in the ear.
In the extraction of a worm generated in the ear, when you treat it with what we said in the division, and in the treatise on distillations, and your treatment does not help, then it is necessary to examine the ear in the sun. If therefore anything of the worms appears there, then extract it with forceps, or with fine hooks.
If, however, nothing appears there, then take a cannula, of which this is the shape, narrow at the bottom, wide at the top; and introduce the slender end into the ear according to the amount that the patient tolerates it; then suck with a strong suction. Do this many times, until all the worms emerge.
If, however, they do not obey you for exit, then coat the surrounding area of the cannula with wax, as I said in the stone.
If, however, they do not emerge with what we have said, then administer the distillations which the ancients have already used in the killing of worms, and you will find it in the treatise on distillations.
And let your pouring of oils and medicines into the ear be with this instrument, the form of which is depicted here. As you see, make it from silver or bronze, narrowed at the bottom, in which there is a small hole, wider at the top.
Let the impelling thing that is in the concavity of the cannula be made of bronze, properly made. And if you wish, take a rod, and wrap linen around its end with a proper wrapping; then pour oil, or juice, or whatever medicine you desire into the cannula; and let it be in the ear; then introduce the rod with the linen from above, and squeeze it with your hand with moderate pressure, until the oil is driven into the cavity of the hearing, and the sick person feels it inside; and whatever is poured into the ear should be warmed with fire for a short time; and beware lest the distilled substance be excessively cold, for medicines do not tolerate that.
Chapter VII.
Concerning the treatment of sudden ear blockage.
Sometimes infants emerge from their mothers' wombs unperforated.
And it happens also to some people that blockage in the ear canal comes from a wound or flesh growing in it.
And this blockage sometimes occurs deep within the ear cavity, where sight does not reach it; and sometimes it happens near the outer part of the cavity, and sight follows it.
Therefore, that which occurs deep within the ear, and which the sight does not encompass, is in many cases difficult to cure.
And in that which is followed by sight, you must place the patient's ear in the sun and look at it.  Then if you see the blockage appearing, open it with a thin probe, whose form is as follows, having a small, slightly pointed width at one end, and the rest of the probe smooth-sided, so as not to injure the ear.
If therefore the blockage is from flesh grown in the ear canal, then grasp it with a thin hook and cut it away forcefully, until all the flesh is removed.
If the blockage is deep within the ear, then take a thin, smooth rod, and heat it slightly with fire; then insert it into the ear canal.
If, therefore, you sense a blockage with the patient experiencing hearing difficulty, attempt to open it gently, and beware of injuring the nerves of the ear.
Then place a bandage, according to the size of the opening you have made, and wrap it in Egyptian ointment.  Do this for several days, until you are certain the wound is healing.
Or take a bandage, soak it in rosewater, and sprinkle ground zegi powder on it, and use it.
If you see that the ear is already suppurating, then you must remove the bandage and replace it with another bandage wrapped in a caerota made from rose oil, until the hot abscess subsides; then treat it until it heals.
If, however, bleeding occurs, then immerse a sponge or cloth in cold water, and place it on the ear; and use the remaining treatment, which stops the bleeding, as described in the treatise on distillations.
Concerning the treatment of ulcers of the eyelids.
Chapter VIII.
These ulcers that occur on the eyelids are sometimes moist, and sometimes dry.
Therefore it is necessary to grasp them with forceps, or with a hook, and to cut them with a scalpel from their roots. Then apply a crushed lapis to the affected area.
If blood flows from them, then cauterize them with a fine, lenticular cautery.
And to cauterize them is better after the incision. For they often return when something remains from their roots. And when they are cauterized with fire, those roots are burned, and they do not return.
Concerning the treatment of granulations of the eyelids of the eye.
Chapter IX.
Often something happens in the eyelids that is similar to granulation in its strength and hardness, and because of this it is called granulation: and it is an aggregation of thick moisture in the upper eyelid and the lower eyelid.
Therefore, the operation on them is that you should consider then, if it is granulation proceeding from that which appears of the eyelid, and moves to every part, and its matter is easy.
Therefore, make a fissure upon it across its width. Then scrape it from every side with ease, until it escapes. Then suspend it with a hook, and cut it off.
However, if its excision is not prepared for you unless after you penetrate the eyelid with an incision for a short time, then that harms the sick person in some way.
If the fissure is large, then gather it with sutures, and treat it until it is healed.
And if it is small, no harm will come to you from it. For ointment restores it and makes it flesh again.
And if the granulation is sloping towards the inner surface of the ear, then invert the eyelid, and suspend the granulation with a hook; besides which, you need an incision, and press it from every side.
If the eyelid is prolonged by incision, that will not harm it in any way.
Then wash the eye after the incision of the granulation with salt water, and treat the place with that which makes flesh, until the sick person is healed.
Concerning the treatment of the ailment of the eyelids.
Chapter X.
Axirnath is a fat that is generated in the tunics of the upper eyelid, and it happens most often in children.  It weighs down their eyes, and they suffer from catarrhs in the mornings, and cannot look at the sunlight, because tears flow quickly to them.  Because of this, you see them always lying on their faces, and they sleep on them. Their eyelids, now covered by swelling, are moist beneath their eyebrows.  And the prominent Axirnath is visible to the eye, and when you press the area with your fingers, you feel the Axirnath between them.
And the method of its operation is that the sick person should place their head in your bosom.
Then take a linen cloth and prepare a bandage on it, and make a circle from that bandage according to the size that encompasses the Axirnath from every side. Then place it over it, and press with your fingers from every side, so that the Axirnath is gathered in the middle of the circle.
Then make a slit in the middle of that moisture with a lancet, going through the width, and let the slit not be larger than that made in phlebotomy in depth.
Or it is necessary that you pierce the entire skin, until you reach the Axirnath, for in many arrangements the Axirnath proceeds from the slit according to the shape of a piece of white fat.
Therefore, pull it out with the cloth, which you have inserted between your fingers to the outside; and you will turn your hand to the right and left, until it is dispersed; and beware, lest you add to the slit, so that you do not reach the eye with the lancet, and injure it.
However, if Axirnath does not appear to you at the beginning of the slit, you will add a little to the slit with ease, until Axirnath proceeds. Then extract it, as we said.
Then immerse the cloths in vinegar and water, and place them on the area, and bind it with bandages.
And there are men who rub salt, and put it in the concavity of the fissure, so that the moisture remaining from it may liquefy.
Then treat it until it heals.
If however a hot abscess occurs in the place, then treat it with sedative plasters until it heals.
Concerning the methods of Ataxmir of the eye, that is, when the eyelid of the eye softens, causing it to wrinkle improperly.
Chapter XI.
When extra hairs grow in the eyelids, beyond the natural course of hairs, and they multiply, they harm the eye and cause ailments, such as constant weeping, softening of the eyelids, and clouding, until it becomes a cause of the eye's destruction.
And Ataxmir of the eye is indeed cured according to four methods. Or by cautery with fire. Or with sharp medicine, according to what was said previously in the chapter on cautery. Or Ataxmir is cured by incision and suturing. Or by canes, according to what I will say.
It is necessary that you place the head of the sick person in your bosom. Then turn the eyelid of the eye with your left hand.
If therefore it is turned, it is good. And if not: introduce a needle into it, in which there is thread from the lower part of the eyelid, and cause the needle with the thread to penetrate from above, and let it be near the hairs themselves, that is, near the edge of the eyelid where the hairs grow: and pull the thread upwards with the eyelid, and turn it with a radius.
Then find in the inner part of the eyelid beneath the hairs, adding a spatula incident from the larger tear duct to the smaller tear duct.
Then pull out the thread, and place under the eyelid a small pad of cotton, or cloth: then mark on the eyelid with ink, which should be like the shape of a myrtle leaf.
However, it is necessary that it be shaped according to the size and elevation of the eyelid, since it is already diversified in humans.
For among them is one who needs a large amount to be cut from the eyelid, according to the amount softened by the eyelid.
Then, with the spatula, make an incision along the two lines that you marked, beginning at the larger tear duct and continuing to the smaller tear duct.  Let the incision be close to the natural hairs, as thick as a ray.
Then insert a hook into one of the two angles of the skin. Then scrape it all away. Then bring together the two lips with a needle and fine wool thread; and wipe away the blood, and fasten any excess thread to the two eyebrows, with certain disguising substances, if you wish to do so; and if not, do not worry.
Then leave the suture and threads for up to three or four days; afterwards, treat it.
And if you wish, leave the wound without sutures, and treat it with something that dries and constricts it. For the eyelid is raised by sealing the wound and bringing it together; but it will be better.
The ancients taught this method of treatment.
However, there is care for the weak person in it, and it is from good operation, and there is no fear in it.
And another method is in Ataxmir also: and it is, that you mark on the eyelid a figure which is like the figure of a myrtle leaf, as we have narrated.
Then lift the eyelid with three separate hooks, or gathered together, according to this following form, then cut what is superfluous with small shears, according to this form, with equal incision.
If however it is not possible for you to hold the hook, and it is not equal to it: then take a needle in which there is a thread, and insert it in the middle of the figure; and insert another thread near the larger tear duct, and a second thread near the smaller tear duct; and gather together the threads between your fingers with equality.
Then raise your hand with equal elevation; and raise the skin upon which the marked area is, as we have narrated.
Then bring together the two lips of the wound with suture; and treat it until it is healed.
If however an abscess occurs at your incision, or your fissure: soothe that abscess with a cauter, and similar things, from soothing ointments.
And sometimes it happens to the eyelids also, that its hairs are turned.
Therefore it is necessary that you use in it what we said about the incision: but suture is more artificial for both. Concerning the treatment of Ataxmir with canes.
And it is, that you turn the eyelid, and cut the fissure that is within, according to what I have described. Then, skillfully make two canes, or two slender pieces of wood, whose length is according to the length of the eyelid, and their width is less than the width of a spatula: and you have already incised at the extremities of each, from both sides, where you hold the threads.
Then carefully attach each to the excess of the eyelid, and tightly tie the two canes from both sides with a strong ligature, and leave it for several days. For the tightened skin will die, and turn black, and putrefy, until it falls off by itself.
If therefore it is delayed, then cut it with scissors. Then treat it until it heals.
When therefore it is consolidated, the eyelid is raised: and the hairs do not prick.
And this is the form of two canes, which you shall make according to this figure, and quantity in length and breadth.
Chapter XII.
Concerning the raising of pricking hairs in the eye with a needle.
The operation in that is, that you take a thin needle, and introduce into it a fine, soft silk thread. Then gather its ends, and tie each end with a very small knot: and let the length of the threads be one palm.
Then place another fine thread beneath it in the Alinxotati, and tie its ends if you wish.
Then let the sick person place his head in your lap, and be close to the light so that the operation is clear: because it is a delicate, or small, operation.
Then introduce the needle with the alinxotati into the root of the eyelid, and make it penetrate the eyelid quickly, until you draw it out above the natural hair.
Then pull the hair up above the alinxotati: and introduce into the alinxotati that hair: if it is one, or two, or three, not more.
Then pull your hand with it, until the hair comes out with the alinxotati from the eyelid along with the natural hair.
However, if the alinxotati is pulled out, and the hair does not come out with it: pull the alinxotati downwards, with the thread which you have placed in it, until the alinxotati comes out from the upper opening, and you see it with the eye's vision: then you shall pull the alinxotati and the thread: and remove both, and close the eye: and leave it closed for two days, or three, until the hair adheres to its place, and flesh grows over it.
If however the hair is short, then either associate a long hair from the natural hairs with it, and lift it with it: or leave it, until it lengthens after a time, and then lift it.
Chapter XIII.
When this Alxatrati occurs in the eye, it is called Leporina.
And this Alxatrati is either natural or accidental.
Accidental therefore results from the consolidation of a wound, or incision, or cautery, and things similar to them.
And the method of treatment in it is that you find that consolidation, and that you separate its lips, and place in that which is between them a linen bandage, and tie it, until it is healed.
And you should not use in its treatment things that dry and constrict: for if you do that, the Alxatrati returns worse than it was: rather, use in it things that soften, such as fenugreek: and embrocate with water, in which has been cooked marshmallow, flaxseed: and with the unguent dyaquilon, which has been liquefied with some of the oils: and wrap with it a bandage, and treat with it.
And the treasure of your treatment is that you should strive with all your ingenuity to prevent its consolidation, according to the form it had in the first place.
And this operation which we have described is, in fact, merely a rectification of the Alxatrati, so that the shape of the area does not return to what it was entirely.
Concerning the treatment of Alxatrati which occurs in the lower eyelid, and is a wrinkling or contraction upwards to the right and left.
Chapter XIV.
This Alxatrati which occurs below, is the one truly called Alxatrati. And it is natural, and it is accidental.
And the accidental one is caused by a wound, or a fissure, or a cautery, and similar things.
And the method of operation in it is to take a needle, in which there is a double thread, and to dip it into the flesh, and to make it penetrate from the left tear duct to the right tear duct, until the thread reaches the flesh at both ends.
Then extend the flesh upwards with the needle, and cut it with a wide spatula. If therefore the eyelid's shape returns as it should, it is good.
And if not, take a ray, and place it in the fissure, and turn the eyelid with it: and make two fissures on the inner side of the eyelid, and let the ends of the two fissures be from the two angles of the incision, until they meet and a corner is made from them: until when they come together, their shape becomes this shape, and it is the Greek letter lambda.
Then remove that flesh by the amount that is its sharp lower side, from what follows the eyelid. Then join the separated parts: sew it with wool thread.
Then treat them with what we have said, of softening medicines, and bandages, until they are healed.
And if a cataract occurs from a fissure, and suture, or cautery: then it is necessary that you make a flat fissure under the hairs of the eyelids, also according to what preceded. Then separate between the two lips with a bandage, according to what we have said.
And the summary of the treatment of a cataract, when it is above or below, is that the operation should proceed according to the form of the cataract.
For indeed there are many varieties in form; and a skillful craftsman prepares a method in whatever way is possible for him, until he restores the shape to the natural form, or nearly so, and himself, the affected part, according to what is appropriate to it from the operation and instruments in many arrangements.
Chapter XV.
Concerning the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva, or cornea.
Sometimes this adhesion occurs in many people, so that the upper eyelid adheres to the white of the eye, and prevents it from its natural functions.
Therefore, you should insert the end of a probe under the eyelid and lift it up; or extend it with a hook; then cut the adhesion with a thin spatula, which should not be sharp, but slightly blunt, so that the patient is not disturbed during the operation, and the spatula is removed and damages the eye with its sharpness; and your incision should be like scraping away the adhesion with ease, until when the eyelid returns to its natural form, and the entire adhesion is healed.
Then, pour salty water into the eye, washing it with water; or distil it from a vessel made of lead, dissolved with water.
Then, separate the eyelid and eye with linen; and place on the eye wool soaked in egg white; and after three days use lead and consolidants, until healed.
Concerning the incision of the toenail and the fleshy prominence of the tear ducts.
Chapter XVI.
Know that the toenail is according to two ways. Or it is nervous: and it is similar to hard, subtle sifac. Or it is not nervous, similar to frozen white moisture: which when it touches iron, or you try to grasp it with a hook, is severed, and the hook does not fix itself to it.
And both types do not begin, unless from the larger tear duct, until it goes gradually, until it covers the sight, and hinders the light, and the movement of the eye.
And the method of operation in its incision is: that the sick person should place his head in your bosom. Then open his eyes, and lift the eyelid with your hand. Then gather the nail with a hook of few foldings, and extend it upwards. Then take a needle, and insert into it a hair from the hairs of a horse, or ox, or cow: and let the thread be strong, and double the end of the needle a little, and fix it in the middle of the nail: and pierce it with the needle, and tie the nail with the thread, and extend it upwards: and scrape with the hair the side of the nail that follows the pupil, as if you were sawing it with the hair, until its last part.
Then cut the remainder at the root of the tear duct with a fine spatula, or with small scissors: and leave the natural tear-duct flesh, so that no constant flow of tears may occur from its incision.
And sometimes the nail is extended with a hook alone, or with a thread, as we have said. Then it is scraped with a gentle spatula, which has this form.
And be careful that the spatula does not touch the horny tunic, and that a rupture occurs in it, and the pupil is pierced.
Then, after the incision, put some crushed salt into the eye, or a preparation made from ziniar, and leave it until the next day. Then treat it with what is necessary until it is healed.
If the nail is not nervous, and you cannot insert a needle into it, nor is a hook fixed in it, then this type of nail is treated many times with medicines, and is healed as I have described in the division.
If you wish to cut it, then open the patient's eye, and take a thin, gentle, and weakly pointed spatula, according to this form. And shave the nail from above with precision. If you see that it thins and breaks, and the shaving imprints on it, then distil from your hour glass a solution made from ziniar, or from red sief, or crushed salt: and poultice the eye until the next day.
Then repeat the operation on it until the whole of it is removed. Unless there is a hot abscess in the eye, in which case leave it, and treat the abscess until it heals.
Then repeat the operation on it with a razor, until it heals.
If, however, a fleshy prominence of the tear duct is harmful to the eye with great injury, it should be suspended with a hook and a portion of it cut away: and do not make the incision too deep, so that the flow of tears is not obstructed.
Then instill into the tear duct a red ointment, or one made from ziniari, until it heals.
Concerning the incision of Alguardenic, and flesh grown in the eye.
Chapter XVII.
Sometimes red flesh grows in some people's eyes, either composed or superimposed, until it covers the pupil or comes close to it or presses on the eyelids, and perhaps turns the eyelids outward; and it resembles a rose-hip.
And the treatment in it is also that the sick person should place their head in your lap. Then open their eyes, and gather all that flesh with suitable hooks: and hold it with a single forceps, or tongs.
Then cut the flesh in the first place, until the whole of it is finished with the scalpel. And be careful of the eye, so that you do not harm it during the operation; and let your incision, with which you cut it, be either with a scalpel, as we described in the incision of the hoof; or with small scissors, with which the asebel is gathered, according to its form, as it will appear after this chapter.
When therefore your incision is complete, and the entire alguardenici is removed, then fill the eye with crushed salt; or distill into it red powder, and similar corrosives from the medicines; and place over the eye externally a cloth with egg white; so that it is protected from a hot abscess.
If anything of the alguardenici remains, and hot blood dominates you, and you fear a hot abscess, then leave the eye, and treat it with that which quiets the abscess.
Then repeat the operation on it until it is healed.
And do likewise with the added flesh that occurs in the eye from this type.
However, it is necessary to avoid treating any weak eye by a natural or accidental way, operating with iron; and do not put your hand in any of these operations until you see that that member is tolerant of that operation.
Concerning the collection of Sebel from the eye.
Chapter XVIII.
Sebel are red veins woven over the eye, and they hinder the eye's function of sight; and they weaken the eye with the length of the days.
Therefore, it is necessary for you first to consider whether the eye in which the Sebel is present is strong, and there is no other illness in it besides the Sebel; then collect its Sebel.
And it is, that you should instruct the sick person to place his head in your bosom.
Then suspend those veins with one hook, or two according to your prudence: and let the hook be a thin double hook, according to this form, or let there be two hooks graded on one body, according to this form.
Then collect those veins with fine scissors with care, and wipe away the blood hour after hour, until you see those veins removed from the eye and are dissolved; and beware lest you injure the eye with the extremities of the forceps.
And let your operation be in the midday sun, and be very firm in your operation, so that you do not cut anything but those veins.
And when you have finished, then instill into the eye a green or red sief, so that its sharpness may corrode what remains of the sebil.
However, if it is not possible for you to collect it all in that hour, then plaster the eye with that which soothes the eye's pain, and leave it for a few days until its pain subsides and is secure from a hot abscess; then repeat upon it according to the same method until it is healed.
And this is the form of the forceps.
Concerning the reduction of a fractured nose.
Chapter XIX.
Physicians call a fractured nose a fistula, which you treat with a cautery, or with a sharp burning medicine, according to the preceding account of it.
And if it does not heal, then there is no remedy for it, except to pierce above the abscess at its origin, and to extract all the moisture and pus from it, until the bone is exposed.
When the bone is exposed, and you see corruption or blackness on it, then scrape it with an instrument, whose shape is this.
And it is called a rough head.
Let it be made from tempered iron, and let its head be round like a hazelnut, pointed with a file, or with a fine aliskilfer point; and place it upon the corrupted spot of bone.
Then revolve it between your fingers, and press your hand a little, until you know that the corruption has been scraped away; and do this several times.
Then restore the place with drying astringent medicines.
If therefore the place is consolidated, and flesh grows in it, and the flow of pus is cut off; and it remains for forty days, and is not renewed, and does not become an abscess, and no accident occurs in it; then know that it is now healed.
And if not, then there is no skill in it, except to reduce the fistula to the nostril's opening, according to this form.
And it is, so that you expose the second bone with an iron, or with a sharp medicine; and when the bone is exposed, then take a drill according to this form, let its iron tip be triangular; and its wood be a pyramid tapering to the tip, as you see.
Then place it on the bone itself; and let it be near the tear duct; and move your hand slightly away from the eye. Then rotate your hand with the drill, until it penetrates the bone, and the sick person feels it; through this that he finds air coming out from it, when he holds his hand over his nose.
Therefore then restore the place with astringent drying medicines, as I taught you.
When therefore flesh grows, and the place hardens, then the matter that was discharged to the exterior returns to the nose; therefore it is less burdensome upon the sick person.
Of the reduction of the prominent eye.
Chapter XX.
When the eye protrudes completely, and no harm occurs to the sight, nor any diminution, then you should release the patient; then bleed him; afterwards place cupping glasses on the neck without scarification, but with suction.
Then place on the eye a liniment made from acacia, aloe, olibanum, and sarcocolla. Then bind the eye from above with a plaster and many bandages.
And the method of the bandages is: that you take soft cloths, many, and each cloth is doubled over four times; make many of these; then place them one over the other, according to the amount needed for the eye; then bind over them with a hard cloth with strong constriction.
And again use cupping glasses with suction without scarification.
Then remove the cupping glasses, and leave the eye bound day and night.
If however the bandage softens again, then it is necessary that it be bound tightly; then remove the bandage after one day and night, as we said.
If therefore you see the eye already restored to the integrity of the tunic, it is good; and if not, then again apply the plaster, and bandages, and constriction, and cupping glasses, until it is healed.
Concerning the incision of the tunic.
Chapter XXI.
When a rupture occurs in the eye's tunic, then it protrudes and comes forth outside the eyelids like a grain of the eye, and the man's appearance is disfigured because of it, and one wishes to cut it; then the operation is as follows, as I relate: to insert a needle into the root of the eye from the lower part to the upper.
Then introduce another needle, on which is a double thread from the tear duct side, and let it penetrate; and leave the first needle in its position.
Then cut the place of the double thread, and bind with it the part of the eye around the needle; and tightly tighten it.
Then withdraw the needle, and place on the eye wool soaked in the white of an egg; and leave it until the thread comes away with the eye.
Then treat the eye with whatever strengthens it, until the wound heals.
And sometimes the eye is tightened on all sides with a bandage of cloth, until the eye gains great prominence.
Then it is pierced with a fine probe that reaches the depth of the eye, and the white fluid runs out, and the eye descends, and immediately enters: then tighten it until it heals.
Concerning the treatment of Alcomnat.
Chapter XXII.
This disease, called Alcomnat, is nothing but pus that collects in the eye, similar to descending water, and is not the eye itself.
And the method of treatment in it is that you make the sick person sit on a prepared seat, or upright.
Then take his head in your hands from both sides, and move it until you see the pus descend to the lower part of your eyes. Then let it be fixed, and do not let it descend, and let the light be released: and let the sick person see things as he saw them before: and if it does not descend lower, know that it is water.
If, however, the descent of the pus is not prepared with what we have said, then make the sick person sit between your hands. Then take a fine spatula and split with it above the horny membrane a little at the junction of the conjunctiva with the cornea, at the crown, until pus comes out.
When it comes out, then instill into the eye warm water in which you have mixed honey; or water in which you have already boiled fenugreek and honey; then treat with the rest of the treatment until it is healed.
Concerning the treatment of water that descends into the eye, or cataract.
Chapter XXIII.
We have already discussed the kinds of water in division, and what is suitable for piercing, the speech declaring and explaining; then take that same one according to health.
Then therefore it is necessary that you make the sick person sit between your hands, square, opposite the light, near the sun; and bind his healthy eye, and tie it tightly; then with your right hand, the eyelid of his eye in which the water is, if the eye in which there is water is the left eye; or with your left hand, if the eye is the right.
Then take with your right hand an almond, if the eye is the left; or with your left hand, if the eye is the right.
Then place the tip of the almond near the crown, by the thickness of a ray, in the apparent white of the lesser tearful eye; then drive the almond with force; and you revolve your hand with it, until it penetrates the white of the eye; and you feel that the almond has now reached the empty thing.
And it is necessary that the measure of the almond's journey to the depth be the measure of the space that is from the pupil to the end of the blackness; and it is the crown of the eye. For you will see it in the very pupil of the eye's vision, because of the clarity of the cornea.
Then place the almond to the place where the water is. Then press it lower, one time after another.
If therefore the water descends immediately, then the sick person sees that on which he opens his sight immediately, and the almond in his eye.
Then rest a little. If therefore the water descends, then place it down a second time, except that the almond emerges.
When therefore it is fixed, and does not ascend, then extract the almond with ease, and you will turn your hand gradually.
Then dissolve some clear gem salt in water, and wash the inside of the eye with it. Then place the entire wad of lint, or wool soaked in rose oil and egg white, over the eye, and bandage the sound eye with it.
We, and those of our time, place crushed cumin with egg white on it.
If the almond does not yield to you for entry into the eye because of its hardness, since it is from humans whose eye is very hard, then it is necessary to take a spatula, which is called Alberid, of which this is the form.
Therefore, scrape it with it only the conjunctiva, and do not insert your hand into the aperture; and there is nothing but to make a subtle path for the almond's place.
Then introduce the almond, according to what we have said.
And at the completion of your work, prepare for the patient a suitably constructed sleeping place, in which he shall sleep on his back in a darkened room; and prohibit him from all movement, and from coughing; and place before him food that will soothe his nature; and do not allow him to move his head to the right, nor to the left at all.
And let the bandage remain according to its arrangement until the third day.
Then loosen it in that darkened room, and test his sight; and have him look at objects. Then replace the bandage until the seventh day.
And it is not necessary that he do it at the time of treatment, or immediately after the incision; rather, it is necessary to delay it; because water ascends quickly through violent sight.
If, however, a hot abscess occurs, then it is necessary to loosen the eye before the seventh day, and to treat it with that which assuages the abscess, until it is calmed; then unbind his sight and place upon his face an oral remedy that controls his sight for a few days, having him remain in that darkened room.
Then let him gradually leave the house, and go about his affairs.
And know that in a zalia a student is not excused from a sight several times; then indeed he may dare on the operation.
And now indeed one of those from Alayrach has come to us, who said that in Alayrach a perforated magdan was made, from which water is drawn.
However, I have not seen anyone in your land who has done that, nor have I read of it in any of the ancient books; and it is possible that it is new.
And these are the forms of the species from the magdan, so that one who is ignorant of them may stand upon it.
Let them be made properly of bronze; and let their extremities be triangular with the same subtlety, and pointed.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
Concerning the treatment of flesh born in the nose.
Sometimes diverse growths of flesh are born in the nose, some of which resemble a scorpion with many feet; and from this there is cancerous, stony flesh, of a dark colour; and from this there is soft flesh, not of a dark colour.
Therefore, in the case of the soft flesh among these growths, not deceitful nor cancerous, you should have the sick person sit between your hands facing the ground, and open their nostrils, and insert a hook into the flesh; then draw it outward, and cut what you have grasped from it with a fine sharp scalpel on one side, until you know that all the flesh has been removed.
If anything remains from it, whose removal is not possible, then scrape it with one of the fine instruments with ease, until nothing remains of it. If, however, blood prevails, or a hot abscess occurs, then apply to it what is appropriate; or if it is from malignant abscesses, then hasten to cauterize it until the blood is severed and all the flesh is removed.
Then, after the incision, vinegar, and water, or wine, should be placed into the nose. If, however, the nose opens, and moisture flows from it to the palate, then know that it is now healthy.
If, however, the moisture does not penetrate, as it should, then know that within it is flesh grown on the upper nasal bones, to which the instrument did not reach during the incision.
Then, therefore, it is necessary that you insert a linen thread of some thickness, and tie many knots in it; and place a finger's width, or less, between each knot.  And let the sick man, with a rod or whatever he can, bring one end of the thread into his nose, having made it like a [certain] shape. And pull on his thread until it reaches the [affected] mass, and emerges at his throat.
And many times children in schools make something similar to this [using] atoms; and it is an easy thing for him who wishes.
Then place the two ends of the thread, one end that emerges from the nose, and the other that remains in the nose. Then cut away the flesh with the knots that are on the thread. Do that until you know that the flesh is now with the knots of the thread.
Then pull out the thread; and after cleansing the blood, place in the nose a compress soaked in Egyptian ointment: do this for three days, or more, until the ointment has corroded all that remains of the flesh.
Then place a lead cannula at the end of the object in the nose, for several days, until it heals.
If however it needs a treatment that will dry it, use that.
To make a nasal douching, with which oils and other medicines are distilled into the nose, make it of silver or bronze: that it should be like a small lamp, open like a shell: and its passage similarly.
And if you wish, make a closed cannula, like a pipe.
And the shell of the instrument for nasal douching should be full, uncovered; with a handle at its end, with which you hold it when you heat oil in it, or whatever you wish from juices and fluid substances.
Chapter XXV.
Often a wart is born on the tip of the nose, and it grows larger and increases with the days until its appearance becomes unsightly.
And therefore it is necessary, that in the beginning of its appearance, you should cauterize it and eradicate all of it. Then place over the site either a cautery or a burning medicine that stands in its place.
If, therefore, its incision is delayed until it becomes enlarged, then consider, if it is stony, hard, of a dark colour, with little sensation: then do not touch it with iron, for it is a cancerous abscess.
For many times I have seen that whoever incises such an abscess, and great harm comes upon the one who had it.
And if the abscess is soft to the touch, not dark-colored, and you see that incision is possible throughout it, then evacuate the diseased part, and incise it without fear, without anxiety: and treat the site with that which dries and constricts it, until it is healed.
Chapter XXVI.
Concerning the suture of the nose, and lip, and ear.
Know that when separation of continuity occurs in one of these cartilages, little benefit comes from treatment, except in some people.
Therefore, when something of that sort happens to someone, you should consider if it is a fresh wound with its own blood; and then assemble the wound's edges with sutures; then treat it until it is healed.
And if the separated lips of the discontinuity are now each part whole, then you must scrape away every part from its apparent skin until it bleeds. Then, assemble the two edges with sutures, and tighten them, and powder the blood of a dragon, and crushed frankincense, over it; and place a piece of a diafinicon plaster, and some other of the consolidating plasters, upon the powders; and leave it bound for two or three days. Then unbind it, and change the medicine; and leave it until the threads separate by themselves. Then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
The method of suture is, that you assemble the discontinuity either with needles, as we have described in the suture of the belly; or assemble the discontinuity with thread, as I have caused you to know.
Chapter XXVII.
Concerning the extraction of lip nodules.
Many men frequently have hard abscesses resembling grains of herbs on the interior of their lips; and some of them are smaller, and some larger.
Therefore it is necessary that you turn the lip, and cut over every nodule on it, and suspend it with a hook, and cut it away from every side.
Then you should fill the place after the incision with bruised zegi until the blood is cut off.
Then it should be washed with vinegar, and the place, where there is astringency, should be treated with it until the wounds are healed.
Chapter XXVIII.
Concerning the incision of fleshy growth on the gums.
Often fleshy growth grows on the gums, which the ancients call abuluss.
Therefore, you should suspend it with a hook; or hold it with forceps, and cut it near its root, and allow the pus, or blood, to run.
Then place on the area ground zegi, or other astringent, drying powders.
If that flesh returns after treatment, for it often does, then cut it a second time and cauterize it; for it will not return after cauterization.
Chapter XXIX.
Concerning the extraction of teeth with iron.
Sometimes rough, unsightly, and sometimes blackened, yellowish, or greenish scabs gather on the surfaces of the teeth from the inner and outer sides, and between the gums, until that corruption reaches the gum, and the teeth become unsightly because of it.
Therefore, you should have the patient sit between your hands, with their head in your lap, and scrape the molar and tooth, where the scabs, like sand, appear, until none of them remain; and similarly deal with blackness, greenness, yellowness, and other such things until they are cleaned.
If, therefore, that which is on them from the beginning of the scraping is removed, it is good; and if not, then repeat the scraping on them another, second, and third day, until finally the desired result is achieved.
And know that a tooth requires scrapers of various shapes and forms, according to what is prepared for your operation; therefore, the scraper with which a tooth is scraped from the lower part is different from the scraper used for scraping between the teeth, according to other shapes.
And let these shapes of scrapers be prepared by you, as you see them.
Chapter Thirty.
Concerning the extraction of toothache.
You ought to treat the tooth from its pain with all ingenuity, and delay in its eradication. For there is nothing to replace it when it is eradicated; because it is a superior substance.  Until, when there is no excuse for its eradication, then it is necessary when the infirm is compelled to eradicate it, that it may be strengthened, and that the aching tooth may be certified by you.
For pain often deceives the infirm, and they think that it is in the tooth's substance and eradicate it; afterwards the pain does not recede, until the infirm's tooth is eradicated.
For we have already seen that from the operation of phlebotomists.
Therefore, when the aching tooth itself is certified by you, then it is necessary that you scarify around the tooth with a spatula, in which there is something of virtue, until the gum is loosened on every side; then move it with your fingers, or with delicate forceps at first gently, until you break it.
Then place large forceps in a good position on it, and place the patient's head between your knees: which you have already held firmly, so that it does not move; then pull the tooth straight, so that you do not break it.
If therefore it does not emerge, then take one of those instruments, and insert it under it from every side with ease, and try its movement, as you did at first.
If however the tooth is perforated or corroded, then it is necessary that you fill that hole with cloth; and bind it with the end of a thin radius, so that it does not break in the moment of your constriction upon it with the forceps.
And it is necessary that you act skillfully with the scarification around the gum from every side very much.
And maintain your strength so that you do not break it, and a part of it remains, and returns upon the sick person from that malice which is greater pain than his first pain.
And beware, lest you do that which ignorant phlebotomists do in their haste, and their boldness over its eradication, beyond what we have described.
For very often they cause great illnesses upon men, which are more difficult to break, and the roots of it all remain, or some of them; or to extract it with some of the jawbones; as we have testified many times.
Then it should be washed after its eradication with wine, or with vinegar and salt.
If a flow of blood occurs from those places (for it often does), then rub some of the zegi and fill the place with it; and if not, cauterize it if zegi does not help you.
The forms of the fine forceps with which the tooth is initially moved should have long extremities, a short handle, and be thick, so that they do not double when pressed upon the tooth.  They should be like those with thick handles, such that when you tighten them, they do not give way, nor double; with short extremities, and made of tempered iron, or well-made steel, with straight extremities.  And on their extremities be teeth, some entering into others, and tightening with a proper, strong constriction.
And sometimes the extremities are made like the shape of the beak of a bird called a stork, and they are of strong constriction.
Chapter XXXI.
Of the eradication of teeth, and the extraction of fractured mandibular bones.
When, during the extraction of a tooth, a piece remains, then it is necessary to apply a poultice with butter for one or two days, until the area softens.  Then introduce the forceps, or pliers, whose extremities resemble the beak of the bird called a stork. The form of these pliers is...
Their extremities should be made like a file within; or like an aliskilfegi.
If, therefore, it does not yield to removal with these pliers, then it is necessary to carefully expose the root and remove all flesh, and introduce the instrument called a small lever, whose form is this.
Let it be short, somewhat thick: and let it not be soaked, lest it break.
If, therefore, the root emerges through it, it is good; and if not, it is necessary to assist with other instruments, whose forms are these.
The form of the first is triangular with a certain thickness at the extremity.
The form of a triangular, slender extremity.
And with other instruments and tools which preceded in the treatment of teeth.
Also, there is assistance with this instrument, which resembles a large hook, of this form: triangular extremity twisted, where there is some thickness so that it does not break; and it should not be soaked.
And know that there are many instruments for teeth, and likewise other instruments are not to be counted, and a skillful craftsman, subtle in his art, finds for himself the instrument according to which the operation and the illnesses themselves indicate to him.
Because there are illnesses for which the ancients did not describe an instrument, due to the diversity of their types.
If, however, a bone breaks from the jaw or from other bones of the mouth, or if it rots, then probe over it in its place with what is appropriate for it from the instruments and forceps that I have mentioned in the extraction of roots, and assist with forceps, whose shape is this.
Let there be some gradual thickness in it, so that the bone is gripped or held with it, and does not escape and move away until the bone comes out; and restore the place with suitable medicines for it.
If, however, there is a small rot in the bone, then scrape it of its rot, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter XXXII.
Of the filing of teeth that project over others.
When teeth project not according to their natural course, an ugly shape is made by it; and especially when this happens in women and girls, namely those who are available.
It is therefore necessary that you consider. For if a tooth is born after another tooth and is not positioned properly, or its filing or grinding is not possible, then extract it; and if it is attached to another tooth, then cut it with this instrument, whose shape is this.
It is similar to a small beak, and is made of tempered iron, with a very sharp tip.
And let your incision, by which you cut it, be in many days, and because of the tooth's hardness, and so that another tooth is not moved.
If however it is prominent, exposed to its grinding: then grind it with a file of tempered steel, whose shape is this.
Let the whole be of tempered steel, and its handle of small, fine points, like the file with which needles are made: with which the tooth is ground gradually over many days with ease, so that the tooth is not moved and falls out; then smooth it, finally scrape it with some scrapers.
If, however, it is a tooth from which a piece has broken off, and it injures the tongue: then it is necessary to file it also, until the roughness of that break is removed, and it is made even: and flattened, and it does not injure the tongue, nor impair speech.
Chapter XXXIII.
Concerning the retention of teeth with gold wires.
When the front teeth suffer concussion and displacement from a blow or fall, and the patient cannot bite on it without it falling out, and you have treated them with astringent remedies, and the treatment has not been successful, then the remedy is to bind them with a gold wire, because silver turns green and rots after days, but gold never changes.
And let the wire be medium in thinness and thickness according to the amount that it will take between the teeth.
And the method of retention is to take a wire, and insert twice its length between two healthy teeth; then weave the two ends of the wire between the loosened teeth, whether one or more, until you reach with the weaving to a healthy tooth on the other side. Then repeat the weaving to the part from which you began; and bind your hand with care and skill until it is completely fixed; and let your tightening of the wire be near the roots of the teeth, so that it does not come loose.
Then cut the excess ends of the wire with scissors; and bring both together, and shape them with pliers; and conceal them between the healthy tooth and the loosened tooth, so that they do not injure the tongue; then let it be so tight that it remains.
If however it loosens or cuts, tighten them with another wire, and it will stay with it throughout this time.
And this is the method for teeth, and the manner of retaining two healthy teeth, and two loosened teeth, as you see.
And sometimes it happens that one tooth, or two, returns after the fall of each to its place, and are retained as we have described, and remain; and only a skillful, prudent, and experienced craftsman can accomplish this.
And sometimes some of the vaccini bones are adapted; and it is made like the form of a tooth, and placed in the place from which the tooth is strengthened and tightened, as we said; and it remains, and is preserved with it for a long time.
Chapter Thirty-four.
Sometimes this ligament which occurs under the tongue is either natural, with which a human being is born, or accidental, from a wound which has already become consolidated.
And the operation on it is that you open the patient's mouth at his head in your lap, and raise his tongue; then cut that nervous ligament across, until the tongue is released from its restraint.
If there is anything hard and knotted in it, and it is from the consolidation of a wound, then insert a hook into it, and split it across, until you cut the ligament, and the knot is released.
And be careful, lest there be a fissure deep in the flesh, and you cut an artery there, and a flow of blood occurs.
Then wash the area of the incision with rosewater, or vinegar, or cold water.
Then place a linen bandage under the tongue, which the patient must hold all night, so that it does not consolidate again.
If, however, a flow of blood occurs, then place ground zegi over the area. If, however, blood overcomes you, then cauterize the area with a lentil-shaped cautery, suitable for it; then treat it with the rest of the treatment, until it is healed.
Chapter XXXV.
Sometimes an abscess occurs under the tongue that resembles a small frog, hindering the tongue from its natural function, and perhaps it magnifies so much that it fills the mouth.
And the operation in it is that you open the mouth of the sick person before the sun, and consider the abscess.
If therefore you see it of a dark color, or black, hard, to which the sick person finds no sensation: then do not touch it, for it is cancer.
And if it is inclined to whiteness, in which there is moisture, then throw a hook into it, and cut it with a thin spatula, and free it from every side. If blood overcomes you during your operation, then place crushed zegi upon it until the blood is stemmed; then return to your operation until you extract it with its entirety.
Then it should be washed with vinegar and salt, and treat it with the appropriate further treatment, until it is healed.
Concerning the treatment of an abscess of the two tonsils.
Chapter XXXVI.
Sometimes there are occurrences between the throat and the glands, which resemble algodad, occurring externally, and are called the two tonsils.  When you treat them with what we have said in the division, and they do not heal, then consider.
If therefore it is a hard abscess, of a dark color, with little feeling, then do not touch it with iron.
And if it is of a reddish color, and its root is thick, then do not touch it with iron either, fearing a flow of blood, rather, leave it alone until it ripens. And then you will either perforate it, or it will burst on its own.
And if it is white in color, round, and its root is fine, then it is necessary to cut it.
And the operation on it is, that you consider before its operation, if its abscess has rested completely, cooled, or diminished by some diminution; then therefore make the infirm person sit in the sun, and his head be in your lap; and open his mouth, and let the servant take between his hands, and press his tongue downwards with an instrument, whose shape is this.
Let it be made of silver, or of bronze, which is as thin as a knife.
Therefore, when you press the tongue with it, and the abscess is shown to you, and your sight falls upon it, then take a hook, and fix it in the tonsil, and pull it outwards as much as you can, besides pulling out with it some of the membranes.
Then cut it with an instrument, whose form follows this. It is like shears; however, its ends should be curved, so that the opening of each is directly opposite the other sharply; therefore, take care that it is made of tempered iron, or imbued with alfuled.
If however this instrument is not present, then cut it with a spatula, whose form is this. One side should be sharp, the other side not sharp.
And after you cut one tonsil, cut the other according to this same type of incision.
Then, after the incision, let the sick person gargle with cold water, or with vinegar and water.
If however a flow of blood occurs, let him gargle with water in which the peels of pomegranates have been boiled; or the leaves of myrtle, and things similar to those from astringents, until the flow of blood is stopped; then treat him until he is healed.
And sometimes other abscesses arise in the throat, besides the two tonsils. Therefore, cut it according to what I said in the incision of the two tonsils equally.
And I myself have already treated a woman from an abscess which had arisen between her throat, which was drawing to blackness, few sensations, which had nearly closed her throat; and the woman was breathing from a constricted passage, and I had already forbidden her eating and drinking water; and she had already reached death, if it had remained for one day or two days.
And the abscess had risen so much that it had emitted two branches, until they had emerged above the two nostrils.
Therefore, I hastened quickly, and I fixed a hook in one of them. Then I pulled it, and a large portion came out from it.
Then I cut it, where I grasped it from the nostril.
Then I did the same to that which proceeded from the other nostril.
Then I opened its mouth, and pressed its tongue; and afterward I fixed a hook in the abscess; then I cut off a part of it, and only a little blood ran from it.  Therefore, the woman's throat was healed, and she immediately began to drink water, then ate food.
Therefore, I did not cease cutting from that abscess for a long time; and the abscess succeeded in the place of that which I was cutting, until it was excessively prolonged with it.
