Therefore, I devised and cauterized the abscess between the throat.
Therefore, it stood from the addition; then I departed from that place, and did not know what God did with it after me.
Concerning the incision of the abscess of the uvula.
Chapter XXXVII.
When the catarrh descends to the uvula, and it becomes abscessed, and is long, then it is called perpendicular; and if it is in the lower part thick, round, then it is called a grape.
When it is treated with what we have said in the division, and the treatment does not help; and you see that the abscess, hot, has ceased from it, and is subtle; then it is necessary that you incise it.
And that which is gathered from it, round, and has no length, and is bloody, or of a brown color, or black, and has no sensation: then it is necessary that you avoid its incision. For in it is fear over the sick person.
Therefore, when you see it according to the manner which I have said, from its whiteness and length, you should make the sick person sit facing the sun; and press his tongue with the instrument, of which the narration has preceded; then fix the hook into the uvula, and pull it downwards; and incise it with one of the two instruments that I have mentioned in the incision of the two tonsils.
And it is necessary that you do not incise from it, unless that which is added beyond the natural limit without addition.
For if you incise from it more, you will harm the voice and speech.
Then after the incision, use that which we have narrated in the incision of the two tonsils, and treat it until it heals.
If, however, the sick person is terrified by the incision, then it is necessary that you employ skill in cauterizing it without fear and trembling.
And the method of the cautery on it is not except with sharp water; and it is that you place the head of the sick person in your lap. Then press his tongue with the instrument that we mentioned. Then take from the water which I told you about in the chapter on cauterization, and make with it an unquenched lime, and place it neither thick nor thin, and fill with it the concavity of this instrument, which has this form.
Let the concavity in which the medicine is placed be, like the concavity of a spoon's radius.
And place the instrument with the medicine upon the uvula itself; and let the sick person lie on his side, so that saliva may run from his mouth from the internal medicine, so that nothing descends from it into his throat and harm him.
Then hold your hand with the medicine. And you shall keep it upon the uvula for a quantity of half an hour, until you see it already blackened and the biting of the medicine quieted.
And if you wish to take a poultice, and wrap it about the extremity of the radius, and infuse the poultice with the medicine, and introduce the radius with the poultice into the cannula from above, until the poultice adheres to the uvula.
Do it many times, until you achieve what you wish of cauterization of the uvula. Then leave it: for it will dry and fall after three, or four days.
If you need to repeat the medicine, repeat it: and after the cauterization, wipe the circumference of the uvula with a poultice soaked in butter, and cleanse with that which is around it from the medicine; and wash it with cold water.
And treat it externally with embrocations, and internally with gargles, until it is healed.
This kind of treatment is therefore healthier than incision and farther from danger.
And sometimes the uvula is treated even with something finer than cautery and incision: for it is treated with fumigation, according to this method. Take mint, hyssop, oregano, rue, sseah, chamomile, and wormwood, and similar herbs from them: and gather them all, or some of them, in a pot, and steep them in vinegar, and boil them: and let the pot be plastered with suitable clay: and there be a hole in the middle of the pot, over which the concave instrument is placed, in this way.
Let it be made of silver, or of bronze: and let the end, in which is the pomegranate, be inserted into the patient's mouth, until the vapor ascends to the uvula above the cannula, until the uvula is greatly darkened.
Then repeatedly over it, until it dries.
And beware, that you do not perform this treatment at the beginning of the abscess's arrival. For it usually adds to the abscess: and it is not necessary that you do that, except at the decline of its hot abscess.
If however this instrument is not present to you, then take a reed, and place on its end the rind of an egg: so that the patient's mouth is not burned: because the rind of an egg prevents the vapor from burning the mouth: and this is a good treatment with health.
Concerning the extraction of thorns and bones from the throat.
Chapter Thirty-Eight.
Often a bone, or a fishbone, or something else adheres in the throat.
Therefore, it is necessary to extract that which is apparent from them, upon which the sight falls, after pressing the tongue with an instrument near the sun, and it is made clear to you what is in the throat.
And that which is not apparent to you and is hidden in the throat, it is necessary to make the vomiting weak before the food is digested in the stomach. For perhaps the attached thing will come out by vomiting.
Or let the weak person swallow a piece of turnip, or a root of lettuce, or a small piece of dry bread; or let him take a piece of soft dry sea sponge, and tie it to a thread, then swallow it. When therefore it reaches the place where the spines or bone are, and it is drawn out, then it goes out.
If therefore it does not come out with what we have said: then use an instrument of lead according to this form.
Let it be thicker than a reed a little, and at its extremity let there be a curve, which he should insert into his throat with ease; and let him have his head raised up.
And let him beware of touching his epiglottis, lest a cough befall him, and let him push with it a bone or spine, or let the physician insert it with his hand.
But that which the sick person inserts is better, because of his knowledge of the place of the attached thing; and let him push downward, or pull his hand upward with the instrument, the whole of it according to the quantity which is prepared for him, until it emerges.
Concerning the extraction of a leech.
Chapter XXXIX.
When a leech is medicated with what I have said in the division on treatment with medicines, and it does not help, then consider in the throat of the sick person in front of the sun, or in the sun after you have pressed his tongue with the instrument that I have told you about.
If therefore your sight falls upon the leech, extract it with a small hook, or with delicate slender forceps.
If it is not possible with them, then take a concave cannula, and insert it into the throat of the sick person until near the throat; then insert into the concavity of the cannula a heated iron with fire. Do that many times; and let the sick person abstain from water the whole day.
Then let him take a vessel full of cold water, and open his mouth in it; and let him wash his mouth with it, and not swallow anything from it; and let him move the water one hour after another with his hand. For the leech immediately, when it feels the water, falls.
If, however, it does not exit with what we have described, then fumigate the sick person with lice, or with asa, with the instrument that I have described in the fumigation of the uvula.
Do that many times, for it will fall itself.
And the method of operation in fumigation is, that you take a pot, in which are live prunes with fire; and the pot is covered with a cover, in whose middle is a hole; and the extremity of the instrument is placed in that hole.
Then, project the fumigation, and let the sick person place his mouth at the end of the tube, and close his mouth so that the fumigation does not escape, until you know that the fumigation has reached the leech. For the leech will immediately fall.
If it does not fall, then repeat the fumigation many times.
And let the sick person endure thirst, and eat salty things, and garlic, and not drink water.
Therefore, it is necessary that it depart with this regimen.
And this is the form of the instrument with which the leech is drawn out from the throat, when it falls upon it as seen.
It is similar to forceps, as you see: unless because it has this curvature which is inserted into the throat. And its extremity is similar to the mouth of a bird called a stork, in which there is the roughness of a file: when it grasps something, it does not let it go at all.
Concerning the opening of abscesses, and their incision, and their general care.
Chapter XL.
There are many kinds of abscesses, of various types, according to the recollection of them, that is to say, one, and one in this book: and they are diverse in their perforation, and incision from two ways.
One of which is from the type of abscess in itself, and because it is comprised of humors.
And the second way is because of the places in which it occurs in the body.
Because an abscess occurring in the head is different from an abscess occurring in the anus; and an abscess occurring in a fleshy area is different from an abscess occurring in the joints; and each of them has its own judgment from the operation.
And of abscesses, there are some which should not be punctured, unless after the maturation of the pus in them, and its completion; and of them there are some which should be punctured while they are raw, and do not mature according to completion.
Such as abscesses which are near the joints.
Because an abscess, when it occurs near a joint, and its duration extends until that which is around it putrefies, perhaps the ligaments or nerves of that joint are corrupted; therefore, it is a more proximate cause of the contraction of that limb.
Or it is an abscess near a main limb; for if you delay its incision until it matures, it will harm either that limb or the main limb through it.
Or it is near the anus; therefore, incise it unripe; and it is not necessary to incise an unripe abscess, not of complete digestion, except for one near the anus, lest it putrefy deeply, and extend to the interior of the anus, and become a fistula, or become incurable at its end.
It is necessary to know the time of incision of a mature abscess, according to its completion: and it is through the calming of pain, the recession of fever, the diminishing of redness, and pulsation, and the sharpness of the abscess's head; and the other signs.
And it is necessary that you make the same incision in the lower place of the abscess, and of more prominent eminence: and let the incision be directed longitudinally along the body, if the abscesses are in the hands and feet, and the places of tendons, and chords, and nerves, and arteries, and finally in all equal places where there is no reflection.
In those that are reflected, proceed with the incision according to that limb.
However, when the abscess is in fleshy places, then it is better to leave its incision until its digestion is confirmed, as we have said according to its completion.
Because if you incise it before that, the course of the virus from it is prolonged, and there is much dirt and filth, and perhaps its edges and depth become hardened.
And some abscesses are incised across the body at need, or according to the limb that requires it.
And it is necessary that you administer a small incision in small abscesses, and a large incision in large abscesses, or multiple incisions similar in size to the abscess.
And there are some abscesses in which the skin must be hollowed out and incised when it has already become like a rag, and has reached a stage where it is already dead, like that which happens in many boils and buboes.
And some of them are incised with an incision having three angles. And from among them is that which is incised like the shape of a myrtle leaf.
Like an inguinal abscess.
And from among them are some in which a round incision, and a lunate incision are administered, and similar incisions to these.
That which does not have a head, however, such as mild superficial abscesses, should be pierced with a superficial or merely a flat perforation.
And when the abscess is large, and has already collected much pus, and you pierce it, you should not be hasty and extract all the pus at that hour; rather, extract a part of it. Then cover the abscess until another day. Then extract some more pus.
Do this repeatedly according to a gradual method, until it exits; especially if the patient is weak in strength, or a pregnant woman, or a young child, or an aged and decrepit person: for the animal spirit is often resolved by the exit of pus; and perhaps the patient dies, and you do not perceive it.
Therefore, beware of this chapter with great caution.
And after your perforation, with which you perforate these abscesses, it is necessary that you cleanse the wound: and consider. If therefore the abscess wound is small, or is a flat incision, then apply bandages of linen, or old tow. And if the abscess is large, and the perforations' fissures are numerous, it is then necessary that you insert a bandage into every fissure, until they meet each other.
And if in the abscess you have excised a part of the skin, or have hollowed it out around the edges, then it is necessary that you fill it with old tow, or as when cloth is woven, or the down of linen is shaved off without moisture, and bind it tightly until the third day.
Then remove it, and treat it with whatever ointment is appropriate, until it is healed.
If however, a flow of blood occurs during your operation, then apply cold water, and vinegar, after they are soaked into a cloth, and place it repeatedly on the site of the bleeding.
If, however, the bleeding persists, then it is necessary that you administer the regimen, and treatment, and powders, which I have described in many places in this book of ours, and by division.
If, however, it is during the winter season, and the area of the abscess is densely innervated, then it is necessary that you insert cushions with hot wine and oil, and place them over the affected areas.
And if it is in summer, and the areas are fleshy, then it is necessary that you place cushions soaked in water and oil, or in wine and oil, and make it all cold. Until when the third day shall come, as we have said, then it is necessary that you open the abscess, and cleanse it, and administer the treatment appropriate for it, until it is healed.
This is therefore what is necessary from the knowledge of the treatment of abscesses universally.
According to the path of distinction, however, I have already spoken of every abscess, how the way to its treatment is chaptered individually.
Chapter XLI.
Concerning the incision of abscesses that occur in the scalp.
Small abscesses occur in the scalp, and are of the type of Nodes, which are contained within tunics that are like sacs to them; as if they were the pouched crops of a hen.
And their types are many.
Some are fatty; and some contain moisture like mud; and some contain moisture like bran and gruel; and similar to these, and some are hard, stony.
And there is no fear in the incision and extraction of all of them, provided that their incision does not oppose an artery.
And the procedure in their incision is to examine them with an instrument, whose form comes after this, which is called the Intromissum, until you know what it contains.
For if it contains moisture, then cut it with a superficial longitudinal incision, according to this form.
And begin with an incision from line b to line g. When the moisture is evacuated, then scrape the container that holds the moisture, and do not leave anything from it at all.
For it often returns if anything remains from it.
Then immerse the cotum in Egyptian ointment, if it is present; and if not, in water and salt; and fill the wound with it, and leave it until another day. For it will corrode what remains from the container.
Then again apply the cotum with Egyptian ointment a second, and third time, and if you need it, until you are confident that nothing has remained from the container; then treat the wound with the ointment, until it is healed.
If the abscess contains a fatty lump, then cut it according to this shape, the incision having the shape of a cross; and insert hooks between the two sides of the incision, and scrape it from every side; and try your skill in extracting the tissue that contains it.
If, however, an artery resists you, then do that which I will tell you afterward.
And similarly do in the abscess, if it is stony, by the same incision and treatment, according to what we have said.
And the incision on a stony abscess is easier, because there is little blood and moisture. And sometimes there are some of these abscesses which are in the head in some people, in which there is not moisture at all.
That is: because I cut upon an abscess in the head of an old woman, and found the abscess like a solid and hard stone, rough, white, which could not be broken; and if someone struck it with something to throw, they wounded it.
In other abscesses, however, occurring in the head, apart from these abscesses which occur in the heads of children, and at the roots of the ears, the incision of all of them should be a superficial incision.
Or make a perforation of them always from their lower part, so that the flow of pus may be relieved downwards.
Then treat it with whatever is suitable for them from the treatment.
Concerning the incision of Whitlows on the neck.
Chapter XLII.
These abscesses often occur in the neck, and under the armpits, and in the groins: and sometimes they occur in the rest of the body.
And concerning those that occur in the neck, they are single, and multiple: and they are generated from one another: and every whitlow from them is within its own proper tunic, as is in the nodes and abscesses of the head, as we have narrated.
And the species of these whitlows are many. And from them are stony ones; and from them are those that contain humours; and from them are malignant ones, which do not obey treatment.
Therefore, in that one among them which you see of good disposition to the touch, whose proximity to the skin colour is evident, and which moves to every part, and is not joined to the neck's nerves, nor to the veins, nor to the artery, nor is deep: you should separate it by a superficial incision from the upper part to the lower part of the body along a line, b, to line, g, and scrape it from every side; and spread the two lips of the skin with a hook, or with many hooks, if you need that, as we said in the case of abscesses of the head, and pull it out gradually.
And it is a precaution that the vein or nerve not be cut.
And let the scalpel not be overly sharp, so that it does not injure your hand with the incision, or disturb the sick person, and you cut what is not necessary.
However, if you cut a vein or artery, or it hinders you from the operation, then apply pounded zegi to the wound, or certain powders that stop bleeding, and bind the wound, and leave it until the sharpness of the abscess subsides and the wound softens; or it starts and wants to become putrid.
For then the blood stops; therefore return to your operation until you complete it.
Then investigate with your index finger if anything remains of the other small scabs; therefore cut and cleanse them.
If there is a large vein in the root of the scab, then you must not cut that scab from its root; rather, you must tie it with a strong doubled thread, and leave it until it falls off by itself without harm.
Then fill the wound with old lint, which you have already soaked in Egyptian ointment. Then treat it.
If the entire scab is cut, then you must bring together the two edges of the wound and suture it immediately, after you see that there is no longer any excess.
However, if you see that excess remains because of the size of the scab, then you must strive to cut its root and remove it, and administer the suture which we have described.
And whatever scabs contain moisture, pierce it with a superficial puncture, so that the place of its maturity appears to you, and place the puncture from what follows below the body, as we have said.
Then after the puncture, apply liniments with ointment, and similar things, to corrode whatever remains of the corruption, until when you see the wound already cleansed, then treat it with a generative flesh ointment until it is healed.
Chapter XLIII.
Concerning the incision of the epiglottis from an abscess occurring within the throat.
The ancients said this incision on the epiglottis, and I have seen no one in our land who has done it, and this is the writing of their speech.
However, in those with synocha, the incision of the epiglottis should be avoided, as it does not provide benefit because all the veins and lungs are weak.
However, in those who have a hot abscess in the mouth, or throat, or both tonsils, when there is no illness in the canal, the incision of the epiglottis should be used, to avoid the harm that comes from suffocation.
Therefore, when we wish to do this, we should cut the epiglottis under three or four circles of the epiglottis, making a small incision across, in the area between two circles, an incision that is in the sifac, not in the cartilage. This place is indeed suitable for the incision, because it is devoid of flesh, and the blood vessels are distant from it.
If the physician is timid, then he should extend the skin of the throat with a hook. Then he should split the skin until he reaches the canal, avoiding the blood vessels if he sees any of them.
Then he should cut the sifac we mentioned, and the incision of the canal is indicated by the phlegm that comes out of it, with its rupture, and the severing of the voice.
And leave the wound open for a time.
When therefore the hour in which suffocation is feared has passed, bring together the two lips of the wound's skin, and sew it itself alone without the cartilage.
Then administer medicines that cause flesh to grow, until it is healed.
Says Albucasis. The summary of this account, which we have related, is not applicable unless they see that some of these abscesses have already blocked the patient's throat; and the patient has been raised above death, and his breath is about to cease: they proceed to cut the epiglottis, so that the patient may breathe over the wound with some respiration and be saved from death.  And on account of this, they prescribed to leave the wound open until the malice of the illness is consumed, and its malice is for three days, and as much as it is; then, therefore, they prescribed to sew the wound and to treat it until it is healed.
And that which I saw for myself is that a maid took a knife and thrust it into her throat, and cut a part of the lung channel with it.
And I was called to treat her, and I found her groaning, like the groan of a dead person.
Therefore, I examined the wound and found a small amount of blood that had come out of the wound. Therefore, I believed that she had not cut a vein, nor an artery; and wind was coming out of the wound.
I hastened therefore, and treated her wound, until it was healthy; and nothing happened to the maid except hoarseness in her voice; and she returned after days to her former state of health.
Therefore, I say that there is no fear in the incision of the epiglottis.
Concerning the incision of a hernia of the throat.
Chapter XLIV.
This abscess, called a hernia of the throat, is a large abscess, according to the color of the body; and it is very common in women.
And it is in two ways: either it is natural, or it is accidental.
Certainly, in the natural one there is no ingenuity.
Accidental, however, is in two ways: one of which is similar to fatty tumors. And the other way is similar to an abscess that forms from the knotting of an artery. And in it there is fear, and it is not appropriate that presumption be made toward it with iron, unless it is small; and you should test it and investigate it with an instrument. If, therefore, you find it similar to a fatty tumor, and it is not connected to any vein, then cut it, as tumors are cut, and extract it with what contains it from the cyst, if it is in a cyst; and if not, then scrutinize its entire area. Then treat the area with what is appropriate for treatment until it is healed.
Chapter XLV.
Concerning the incision upon the variety of Tumors.
There are many kinds of tumors, and I have already stated all their kinds in the division; and it is necessary for me to relate here the difference between a tumor and a fistula, since it is a cause of doubt.
Therefore, I say that when a fistula is present, there is heat, fever, and intense pain until the ebullition of the superfluity subsides and the putrefaction is complete; then the fever and heat cease.
However, with a tumor there is no heat, nor fever, nor pain, and it contains them in a sort of bag or cyst, peculiar to them, and it is according to the color of the body.  And its beginning is like a chickpea, and it grows larger or smaller.
And they are of two kinds: either fatty or containing moisture. And the colours of moisture are many, as I have said in the division.
Therefore, when you arrive at the treatment of a tumor, you must examine it and investigate it first with an instrument called an Intromissum, according to its form, which will be shown in the chapter after this one.
And the method of investigation of abscesses and all tumors is that you take this instrument and insert it into the most moist place you find in the abscess; and you roll your fingers with it slowly until you know that the instrument has already penetrated the skin. Then place your hand at the end according to the size of the abscess.
Then withdraw the Intromissum and consider what emerges in its wake.
If therefore a running moisture emerges, whatever colour it may be, then cut it with a superficial incision, as I said in the other abscesses.
And if no moisture emerges in the wake of the Intromissum, then know that it is fatty; therefore cut over it with an incision shaped like a cross, according to this form, as I taught you; and suspend it with hooks, and peel the skin from every side with ease; and keep the cyst if you can over it, so that you extract the healthy one with the tumor.
If however the cyst breaks open during the operation, and it is not possible to extract the healthy part, for this often happens, then extract it piece by piece until nothing remains of it.
For if a little or much remains of it, the tumor returns for the most part.
If it overcomes you, and a little remains of it: then fill the wound at your completion with certain sharp corrosive powders; and constrict the wound, and place upon it what sits as a warm abscess, and treat it with the rest of the treatment until it is healed.
If therefore the tumor is large, then suture its two lips, and treat it with that which causes it to heal.
If however there is a pulsating or non-pulsating vein there, then hurry and fill the place with pounded zegi, and leave it constricted for two or three days, until the wound putrefies and the boiling of the blood ceases; then return to cutting what remains of the tumor.
Chapter XLVI.
Concerning the instruments outside for section and perforation.
Among them are the forms of instruments for insertion, and they are of three kinds.
For among them is a large one, and among them is a medium one, and among them is a small one.
They should be made of iron, with square ends, well-finished, so that they quickly enter the abscess.
And these are forms of instruments, and, no, that is, piercing ones: and one of them is called a beris, that is, a piercer: and they are of three kinds.
For among them is a large one, and a medium one, and a small one.
They are suitable for investigating abscesses, and openings, and fistulae, and buboes from that which lies between the bones within them.
And others are made round, smooth, and gentle, like large needles with which saddles are sewn, made of gold, or brass, or iron, or silver.
And the best of them are those made of gold.
And there are also experiments made from black lead, which are suitable for testing fistulae, whose concavity has reflections, so that they may be reflected with their gentle reflections.
And they are again of three kinds. Because among them are long ones, among them medium ones, and among them short ones, according to the quantity of the fistula's breadth and its constriction.
The shape of hooks, and they are of many kinds.
Because among them are simple ones, namely those which have a single curve: and they are of three kinds, as you see, Large, Medium, and Small.
And among them are blind hooks: and they are of three kinds.
And among them are hooks having two curves, and they are of three kinds: and among them are hooks having three curves, and they are of three kinds: and all these are necessary in their place.
And these are the shapes of knives, with which knots and abscesses are cut and scraped: and they are of three kinds: because among them is a large, medium, and small one.
Their cutting edges are sharp, the other edges are not sharp: and they are not placed thus, unless so that they may help in the scraping of knots in fear of cutting a vein or nerve: and so that the sick person may be healed by them, and find a little peace from the burning sensation he finds in scraping an abscess.
They are made of bronze, and are similar to the instrument with which alcohol is made: and on the broad end a hidden spatula point is placed: and on it runs inward, and outward as you wish, as you see.
The shapes of spatulas that are hidden between the fingers when puncturing abscesses: and the sick do not perceive them: and they are of three kinds, large, medium, and small.
The shapes of cupping glasses with which the flow of blood is cut off: and they are of three kinds, large, medium, and small.
They are made of bronze, or brass, round, a little in length, as you see: and let them be of fine workmanship.
And it is necessary that these cupping glasses, large and small, be with you, so that you may cut off blood with speed when needed, and when medicine is not present.
However, do not use them to cut off blood on every part of the body, and use them only on fleshy parts, such as the thighs of the leg, and the hips, and the arms, and the breasts, and the belly, and the groins, and similar fleshy, moist parts.
And sometimes other small instruments are made from them, similar to hazelnuts, according to their shape, with which blood is cut off when it flows from a phlebotomy, or at the incision of a vein or artery: and sometimes they are made round in this form.
Chapter XLVII.
Concerning the care of men's breasts that resemble women's breasts.
Sometimes the breasts of some men become swollen at the completion of sleep (that is, when they begin to become soiled in sleep) until they resemble women's breasts, and remain foul-looking abscesses.
Therefore, whoever abhors that, ought to cut above the breasts with a crescent-shaped incision, according to this form from line b to line g. Then scrape off all the fat. Afterwards, fill the wound with a flesh-forming medicine. Then bring together the two edges of the wound with sutures, and treat it until it is healed.
If, however, the breast inclines downwards and softens because of its size, as happens in women, then it is necessary to cut it in two places on its upper sides with two incisions similar to a crescent shape, each of which is joined to the other at the ends of each, until there is a larger line containing a smaller one, according to this form from line b to line g. Afterwards, scrape off the skin that is between the two edges, and remove the fat, and administer what we have said about sutures and powders, which is necessary for treatment, until it is healed.
If, however, you do not complete the incision that you ought to make, because of the patient's distress or because of blood flow, then you ought to fill the wound with a cloth soaked in a corrosive ointment, and leave it until it corrodes what remains of the fat. Then treat it until it is healed.
Concerning the perforation of an abscess, which occurs under the nipple.
Chapter XLVIII.
These abscesses which occur under the nipple are of the nature of hard strophulae, some containing cysts, and some containing moist fluids.
Those of them which contain moist fluids should be incised with a crescent-shaped cut, according to this form, from line b to line g. When thus the entire matter from the abscess is evacuated, then fill it with old linen, and leave it until another day.
Then the linen should be removed, and treated with a suitable ointment for it, until it heals.
If the abscess is of the nature of knotted strophulae, then dissect upon it, as we said in the section on strophulae, equally.
If, however, the wound extends in this so as to emit pus, then apply a cautery to it, according to what we said in what has been stated before.
Chapter XLIX.
Of the incision of an abscess that occurs because of the artery and vein.
When an artery bursts, and the skin over it is consolidated, then often an abscess results from it: and similarly, it also happens to the vein, so that an abscess and swelling occur in it.
And the signs by which it is known that the abscess and swelling are due to the artery and vein are: because, when the abscess is due to the artery, it is long, gathered deep within the body; and when you press the abscess with your fingers, you feel as if there is a creaking sound to it.
And that which is due to the vein is round on the surface of the body.
And incision upon this abscess is an error, and especially that which is under the armpits, and the groin, and the neck, and in many places of the body: and it is very large. Therefore, its treatment with iron should be avoided, and that which is also from them in the extremities, or in the head, should be avoided.
Upon that which is from them, from the swelling of the opening of the Artery, find in the skin a cut in length. Then open the cut with hooks, afterwards the Artery: and free it from its sheaths, until it is exposed.
Then insert a needle beneath it, and make it penetrate to the other side, and tie the Artery with a double thread in two places, according to what I taught you in the extraction of the two Arteries which are in the temples.
Then puncture the place, which is between the two ligatures with a spatula, until the blood which is in it is all discharged, and the abscess is resolved.
Then administer a treatment that produces pus, until the ligatures fall off: afterwards treat it with suitable ointments, until it is healed.
If the abscess is because of a cut in a Vein, then you should hold with your hand what you can of the abscess with the skin. Then insert a needle under the place which you held with your hand, and make it penetrate, and have a double thread on it, until you extract it from the other side. Then tie the abscess with a good ligature; as I told you in the ligature of the grape, from two sides with two ends of the thread.
If however you fear that the thread will be pulled out, then insert another needle with another thread under the entire abscess at the incision of the needle; press, and tighten your threads in four places.
Then cut the abscess in the middle of itself, until what is inside it comes out; then cut off the excess skin, and leave what is bound from it.
Then place over it the poultice which you have soaked in wine and oil. Then administer the treatment, which is done with plasters and ointments, until it is healed.
Chapter L.
Concerning the abscess which occurs from the twisting of nerves.
Just as an abscess occurs in the artery and vein, so too an abscess occurs in the nerves: which occurs in them through a blow, or occurs from excessive exertion, and similar things: and it happens most often in those places of assistance, or the heel, and in every place where joints move: and it is a serious abscess.
Its color is similar to the rest of the body, and it is in many dispositions without pain: and when it is squeezed with force, the infirm person feels a sensation similar to numbness in it.
And it is not a deep-seated abscess within the body, rather it is beneath the skin, and it moves to every part, and does not go forward, or before, nor backward to the rear.
Therefore, those that are in the joints should not be treated with iron. For perhaps a contraction will result.
In that which is in the head or forehead, slit the skin with a lancet. If it is a small abscess, then hold it with scissors and cut it away from the root.
And if it is large, then suspend it with a hook, and scrape it: then remove it, and close the wound with sutures: and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter LI.
Concerning the incision of an abscess which occurs in the belly.
Many people frequently have, in their bellies and other parts of their bodies, growths called Fungalia, by resemblance to fungi.
Their root is slender, and their head is thick, and their edges are turned back. And some of them are small; and some of them greatly enlarge.
And I myself have seen a man in whose belly were two growths, which resembled fungi more than anything else; there was no difference between them—white, small root, and their edges turned back, and they were cleft, and moisture constantly flowed from them.
Therefore I cut them, and found in one the weight of approximately ten and eight ounces, and in the other approximately six ounces.  And the procedure for their incision is, if the patient is damp, and the color of the growth is white, moist, and slender-rooted; then cut it with a broad scalpel, and have cauteries ready in the fire.
For often much blood is expelled at its section; therefore hurry if blood overwhelms you, then cauterize it.
If you see a patient fearful and timid of incision with iron, then take a well-made lead thread and bind it tightly to the growth;  using the method we described, and leave it for two days.
Then add to the lead binding. Therefore do not cease binding the lead, whenever it presses on the growth, until it is severed, and falls off by itself without effort.
If however the growth is large and rooted, then in its incision there is something of fear and dread, especially that which is in the belly.
However, it is possible to cut it in half. Then cauterize it, so that it does not return.
Then treat it until the wound is healed.
And beware that you do not presume to cut a growth that is dark in color, of little feeling, and foul in appearance: for it is indeed a cancerous abscess, and I shall come back with a discussion of Cancer after this.
Chapter LII.
Concerning the treatment of the navel's prominence.
The navel's prominence arises from many causes. Or from fissures of the abdominal wall above, from which wind or intestines escape, as happens in other ruptures. Or from blood flowing from a vein or artery, as previously mentioned. Or from constrained wind within it.
If, however, it is due to a fissure of the abdominal wall, and an expulsion of pus, then its color resembles the color of the body, and it is smooth, without pain, and appears in various locations. And if it is due to the expulsion of an intestine, then its location is, with that which we have described, of a more diverse nature. When therefore you press it with your fingers, it disappears; then it returns, and perhaps there is a sound with it; and it is greatly magnified at the entrance of the bath and with strenuous exertion.
If, however, it is due to moisture, it is smooth, then it does not disappear when you press it with your hand, nor is it contracted, nor is it added to.
If, however, it is due to blood, then with these signs an abscess appears to be black on the side.
If, however, it is due to generated flesh, then the abscess is serious, large, hard, and fixed according to one amount.
If, however, it is due to windiness, then its touch is smooth.
And the operation on it is, that you should consider then, if the eminence of the navel is due to blood of the artery, or vein, or windiness: then you must abstain from its cure. For in it is fear and terror, as I taught you in the chapter, in which I said abscesses due to artery and vein.
If, however, the eminence of the navel is due to the intestine or zirb, then you must instruct the sick person to hold his breath and stand outstretched. Then mark with ink around the entire navel. Afterwards, instruct him to lie on his back between your hands. Then surround with a wide spatula the area around the navel, over the spot you marked with ink. Then extend the middle of the abscess upwards with a large hook. Then tie him around firmly with thread, or with silk cord, a strong ligament: and let the knot of the ligament be tied tight.
Then open the middle of the abscess extended above the ligament. If therefore you find it already grasped by the ligament, then extract the tied part, and introduce the index finger into it, and push the intestine towards the interior of the belly; and if you find the adhesion, then take it with a hook, and cut off its excess.
If however the artery or vein is contrary to you, then surround it greatly. Then return to your operation. And take two needles, and introduce into them two strong threads; and introduce the two needles into the circuit you have made around the abscess in the manner of a cross, making them penetrate.
Then tighten the abscess in four places over the needles; and if you wish, remove the needles, and leave the place until the extended flesh rots; and it falls away by itself; or cut it when it becomes thin and rots.
Then treat it with whatever ointment is appropriate, until it heals.
If the prominence of the navel is from flesh protruding in it, or from moisture, then it is necessary to hollow out the abscess, as we said. And extract the blood, or moisture, that you find in it.
Then treat it with whatever consolidates the place.
Chapter LIII.
Concerning the treatment of CANCER.
We have already discussed the division of the species of Cancer, and how there is a way to cure it with medicines, and caution to avoid causing an ulceration when treating it with iron; and we have discussed Cancer generated in the womb and the precautions for its treatment.
And the ancients said that sometimes Cancer is in a place where its eradication is possible, such as Cancer in the nipples or the thigh, and similar parts of the body, by removing the entire diseased part, especially when it is small and beginning.
However, when it becomes old and large, you should not approach it.
For I myself could not cure anyone from it, nor did I see anyone before me who had achieved this.
And the operation on it, when it is located as we have said, is to proceed and to relieve the weak from black cholera sometimes. Then phlebotomize it if you find it with manifest fullness.
Then prepare the patient with a preparation in which he is placed with the operation.
Then throw into the Cancer hooks that suit it. Afterwards, hollow it out from every side with the skin, according to scrutiny, until nothing remains of its roots; and let blood run, and do not sever it quickly, rather squeeze the places, and wipe away all the thick blood with your hand, or with whatever instruments are possible there.
If however the flow of thick blood from the incision of an artery or vein causes you opposition, then cauterize the vein until the blood is severed. Then care for it with the remaining treatment until it is healed.
Concerning the treatment of Dropsy.
Chapter LIII. We have already recounted in the division of the species of dropsy, and how the accumulation of water occurs; and the signs of each species, and its treatment with medicines.
And that which is cured with iron is only the species of ascites alone; and it is not applied with iron to the species of tympanitis; nor to the species of hyposarca at all; for that is mortal.
When you have already treated this species of tympanitis dropsy with medicines, and your treatment has not been successful, then consider. For if weakness has already overtaken the sick person, or there is another infirmity with him besides dropsy, such as if there is a cough with him, or looseness of the bowels, and things similar to these: then beware lest you treat him with iron, for there is fear of that.
If, however, you see the sick person of vigorous strength, and there is no illness with him besides dropsy alone; and he is neither a child nor an old man; then the method of operation in him is that you raise the sick person standing between your hands, and a servant behind him presses his belly with his hand, driving the water to the lower part of the chest.
Then take a sharp-pointed spatula, according to this form. Let it be of two sharp parts, long, and sharp at the extremity like a spatula. However, let there be something of shortness in it gradually, so that you do not proceed with it in the operation all the way to the intestines, and harm him.
Then consider. For if the generation of dropsy is from the intestines, then it is necessary that you extend the incision from the navel a distance of three fingers, downwards in a straight line above the pubic bone.
If however the generation of dropsy is because of disease of the liver, then let your incision be on the left side from the navel three fingers.
And if its generation is because of the spleen, then let the incision be on the right side; and it is also not necessary that the incision be on the side on which the sick person wishes to lie, so that superfluities do not flow to that weak place.
Then pierce the whole skin with an instrument. Then introduce it into that incision, and raise your hand with the spatula between the skin, and if you do, as if you were to scrape it; and let the amount scraped off be the quantity of a fingernail, or about that.
Then pierce, if so, until the spatula reaches the empty space; and it is the space of water. Then extract it, and introduce into the hole the instrument, which has this shape. And it is similar to a cannula; it is made of silver, or bronze, or brass, smooth, polished; to which at its lower part is a small hole, and on its sides are three holes, two on one side, and one on the other side; and the end of it is cut, according to the manner of cutting a reed, as you see; on its upper end is a ring.
For when the instrument reaches the water, then it itself descends immediately upon the instrument, therefore evacuate from the water in an hour a medium quantity.
Because if you evacuate from it more than is necessary, perhaps the sick person dies in an hour through the dissolution of his animal spirit; or a syncope occurs to him which is near death.
But evacuate from it according to the quantity of its virtue, and according to what the dispositions of the sick person signify to you, from the virtue of his pulse, and from his good condition.
Then extract the instrument, and retain the water.
Which is, because it is retained immediately on account of the skin that retains the opening which is over the sifac: of which I spoke, that you should pierce it according to that method.
Then again insert the instrument on the second day, if you see the sick person tolerating it; and also extract a small quantity from the water.
Do this for several days without fear, and guard yourself from error, until there remains little of the water.
However, if you fear for the sick person, and you remove much from the water, then care for him with burial in hot sand, and with much sweat in a bath and the sun, and let him endure thirst; and treat him with drying medicines, until he is healed; and cauterize above the stomach, and liver, and spleen, after the extraction of the water, according to what I said in what preceded.
Concerning the care of infants who are born in places where their urinary openings are not perforated.
Chapter LV.
When any infant emerges from its mother's womb, and the opening of its urethra is not perforated, or is a narrow opening, or is in the wrong place.
Therefore, it is necessary to assist with its perforation immediately when it is born, with a very fine Spatumili, according to this form.
Then place a fine leaden nail in the opening, and tie it; and hold it for three days, or four.
When, therefore, it wishes to urinate, it is removed, and it urinates. Then replace it.
And if you do not place lead, it is possible that the urine which flows over the place will not allow it to close.
However, he whose opening is constricted is treated with lead, as we said, for many days, until it is widened.
However, those in whom the opening is in the wrong place are harmed. This is because, in those who are born with it, the opening is at the end of the penis: therefore, they cannot urinate forward until they raise the penis with their hand to the higher place. Nor is there generation for him, because the semen cannot reach the womb properly: and it is a very foul illness.
And the method of operation in him is that the sick man be placed on his back. Then extend his penis with your left hand by forceful extension, and cut off the head of the penis from the place of the glans, with a pointed knife, or a sharp scalpel; as is the incision of a reed, or as you shave something, so that the middle of it comes to be similar to the head of the penis, and so that the opening falls in the middle as it should.
And beware of the flow of blood in your operation. For it often happens. Therefore, apply to it whatever stops the blood, and treat the wound until it is healed.
Concerning what happens in the foreskin and the head of the penis, and corruption, blackness, and adhesion.
Chapter LVI.
These affections of the penis often occur, and there is a prominence of foul flesh.
And from it, one is malignant and another is not malignant.
Therefore, the non-malignant one should be suspended with a fine hook and cut off, until you cleanse the whole thing. Then place upon it a poultice soaked in Egyptian ointment. Then treat it afterwards with the unguent diafinicon, until it heals.
If it is malignant, of foul colour, then you should apply a cautery to it after incising and scraping it.
If, however, the Albather is in the foreskin of an uncircumcised person, and part of it is inside the foreskin and part outside, then it is necessary to remove the Albather that is inside first, until it is consolidated, then treat the outside part; for when you treat them together, the foreskin is not safe from being pierced.
And they also occur in the testicles, and in the foreskin, blackness and corruption. Therefore, you must cauterize all that is already blackened and that is about to be corrupted, or is already corrupted.
Then apply over it afterwards with honey that has already been mixed with the flour of pomegranate bark and crushed and sifted herbs.
Then treat it with the remaining treatment until it is healed.
If however, a flow of blood occurs, then apply a cautery with a lunar cautery, according to this form.
For cautery is helpful for two conditions simultaneously, namely for the flow of blood, and a wound when it is corrupted.
If the head of the penis is corroded and completely destroyed within an hour, then it is necessary to insert a lead cannula into the urethra so that the patient can urinate over it.
However, in the adhesion of the foreskin to the head of the penis, and this adhesion does not occur unless the foreskin is healthy, and circumcision is not required upon it; and sometimes adhesion occurs due to a wound or abscess: therefore, it is necessary to scrape it with a blunt Spatumili, until the ligament is loosened, and the head of the penis is freed from every part.
If it is difficult to discern these things precisely, then it is necessary to scrape off something from the head of the penis and from the foreskin. Because when the foreskin is thin, it is perhaps perforated quickly due to its thinness.
Then separate the foreskin and the head of the penis with a thin linen cloth that has been moistened in cold water, so that they do not adhere again.
Then treat it with astringent wine until it is consolidated.
Chapter LVII.
Concerning the cleansing of infants and the treatment of errors that befall them.
Circumcision is not a solution of continuity, like other wounds; however, since it is from our operation, by our will, and its use is proper to children, it is necessary that we mark it with a better operation, and a smoother path leading to health.
Therefore, I say that the ancients did not mention circumcision in any of their books, because they did not use it in their laws, and it is only what we have acquired through experiment.
This is because I have found the majority of craftsmen and phlebotomists using cleansing, circumcising with a razor and scissors, and using leeches and ligating with thread and incision with a nail.
And I myself have already tested all these methods, and have not found a better cleansing than with scissors and ligation with thread.
Because in cleansing with a razor, the skin often escapes. Since the skin of the foreskin has two layers, perhaps the upper layer is cut, and the lower layer remains; therefore, it is necessary to make an incision to cut the other, and future pain will result. And with cleansing done by leeches, that is, digital, which the sewers use, the incision of the male member's extremity is not secured; because perhaps it enters into its opening.
In cleansing with a nail, perhaps the iron slips out and ruins your operation: and indeed the skin of a boy is of short nature, and often they are born in such a way that they do not need cleansing; and I have already seen that.
However, cleansing with shears, and tying with thread, has shown me a good test; because shears are proportional to the incision; because the sharpness that is above is like the sharpness that is below. Therefore, when you press your hand, the two sharpnesses are proportional, and they cut according to one operation at one time; and a compression is made in a similar figure, encompassing the skin of the penis on all sides, with which error does not occur at all.
And the proper method of the operation is, that you cause the boy, especially if he is one of those who understand a little, to realize that you do not do anything except tie a thread to his penis, and you leave him until another day.
Then cause him to rejoice and be glad in every way possible to you, from that which his understanding receives. Then make him stand between your prepared hands, and let him not be seated; and hide the shears in your sleeve, or under your foot, so that the boy sees neither them nor anything of the instruments at all.
Then insert your hand into his penis, and blow on the skin, and pull it upwards until the head of the penis emerges. Then cleanse it of the impurities that collect upon it.
Then tie the marked place with a double thread. Afterwards, tie a second ligature a little lower. Then hold the lower ligature site with your index finger and thumb, with good retention, and cut between the two ligatures. Then lift the skin quickly upward and pull out the rod's head. Then allow the blood to run slowly (for it is better) and place powder on top.  And over the powder, a cloth on which is spread the yolk of a boiled egg in rosewater, mixed with good fresh rose oil.  And leave it on top until another day.  And treat it with the remaining treatment until healed.
The form of the scissors for cleansing is this.
Let the two ends be blunt and meeting, so that the nail's branch does not come out of them; and let the length of the two sharp points be equal to the length of the two handles.
An error, however, occurring in cleansing is: perhaps the inner skin is entirely or partially escaped at the incision.
Therefore, you should stretch it from your hour with your fingernail before the abscess of the place, and cut it according to equality.
If you cannot hold it with your fingernail, then pull it out with a hook and cut it.
However, if three days have passed, and that which is under the rod remains swollen, abscessed: then leave it until the abscess subsides, and carefully remove it, and cut it according to what is prepared for you; and beware of the head of the rod. For that will not harm it.
Therefore, treat it with that which consolidates a wound from the powders, which we have described in the treatise on powders; and if it is cut from the skin beyond measure, and extends to the upper parts, that will not harm it either.
Therefore, treat it with what we have said, until it is healed.
Chapter LVIII.
Concerning the extraction of retained urine in the bladder.
Retention of urine in the bladder occurs from an obstruction caused by a stone, or congealed blood, or pus, or formed flesh, and similar things to those which we have mentioned from the methods of treatment in the division.
And if the urine is not discharged, and you see that its retention is due to a stone that is already in the neck of the bladder, then it is necessary for him to be weak, bent over his knees. Then let his feet be placed on his back, and let him extend his belly from the upper part of his back, and let the weak person restrain his breath as much as possible. Thus, he is forced, with the neck of the bladder, towards the expulsion of the stone to the rear, wherefore the urine is released.
If it is not released with what we have said, and the matter is severe on the sick person, then it is necessary for you to administer its extraction with an instrument, which is called a syringe, whose form is this: Let it be of silver, and let it be slender, smooth, concave, like the quill of a bird's feather in slenderness, long in the likeness of one and a half palms; to which let there be a slender tube at its head.
And the method of the urine's exit through it is, that you take a doubled thread, and tie to its extremity wool, or tow, a good ligature; and introduce the extremity of the thread into the lower part of the Syringe; and cut with shears if any excess of wool remains, but introduce it into the tube with force, as a lever.
Then anoint the syringe with oil, or with butter, and the white of an egg; and make the sick person sit on a seat, and embrocate the bladder, and its member with moist oils, and olive oil, and tepid water.
Then introduce the syringe into the member with ease, until it reaches the root of the member.
Then double it upwards from the navel.
Then propel the syringe within it, until it reaches the Bladder, and the sick person feels it has already arrived at the empty thing: and this is not done according to this order, except because the passage, in which it goes, is tortuous.
Then extend the thread with wool gradually. For urine will follow the wool.
Then pull it out, and cause the urine to run through the urinary pathway: and again syringe, and do not cease to do that, until the bladder is emptied, and the sick person finds relief.
Concerning the quality of bladder clysterization with a Clepsydra.
Chapter LIX.
When an ulcer occurs in the bladder, or blood congeals in it, or pus is collected in it, and force is required to distil water and medicines into it: then be done with the instrument called Clepsydra, and this is its form.
It is indeed deaf, that is, closed. And in it are three openings, two on one side, and one on the other side, as you see.
And the concave place, in which that which propels it is situated, should be according to the amount that is sufficient for it without addition, until, when something is pulled with it, moisture is drawn out; and when it is propelled, it is expelled far away, in accordance with how the clepsydra throws naphtha, that is, Greek fire in naval battles.
Therefore, when the force is to project the moisture into the bladder, introduce the extremity of the clepsydra into the moisture, and pull with that which propels it upwards, for the moisture is drawn into the concavity of the clepsydra.
Then introduce its extremity into the tube, as we described in the syringe. Then propel the moisture with that which propels it; for that moisture will reach the bladder at once, so that the sick person feels it.
A subtle form of clyster for injecting into the bladder.
Let it be made of silver, or of brass:  its head should be higher, like a small alcama, and below that a sinus into which a ligament should fall.
Then take a ram's bladder, and place in it the moisture with which you wish to inject the bladder. Then tie it between two sinuses with a strong ligature, using a doubled thread; and warm that moisture a little over the fire.
Then introduce the end of the clyster into the tube, afterwards, grip your hand over the bladder with the moisture with a suitable constriction, until the sick person feels that the moisture has already reached the bladder.
If, however, a bladder is not present, then take a piece of parchment, and make a circle from it, and pierce it all around its circumference. Then introduce a strong thread into those holes, as they revolve. Then assemble the circle, as an alembic is assembled; after you have placed in it what you want of moistures, and oils, and waters.
Then tie it to the instrument, and do with it as you did with the bladder by squeezing with your hand, until the moisture reaches the bladder.
Chapter LX.
Concerning the extraction of the Stone.
We have already spoken in the division of the species of Stone, and its treatment with medicines; and I have explained the difference between a stone generated in the kidneys and the bladder, and the signs of each; and I have spoken of the stone in which manual operation and incision are necessary; and this is the stone that is properly generated in the bladder; and that which adheres to the urethra; and I am narrating this with clear explanation and brevity.
Therefore, I say that a stone generated in the bladder most often occurs in children.
And among its signs is that the urine issues from the bladder like water in its fineness; and white sand appears in it, and the sick person rubs his urethra and suffers with it, and often hangs [himself] down. Then it rises and the anus protrudes in many of them.
And the cure is easy in children, as long as they reach fourteen years. And it is difficult in the elderly. But in young people it is intermediate, in that which is between those [two]; and that of whose stone is larger, the cure is easier; and if small, the cure is the opposite of that.
Therefore, when we come to the cure, it is necessary first to cleanse the sick person with an enema, which will expel all the stool that is in their intestines; for it sometimes hinders the finding of the stone in the examination.
Then let the sick person be taken with their feet, and shaken, and moved downwards, so that the stone may descend to the depth of the bladder, or leap out of a high place sometimes.
Then have him sit between your hands, prepared, and let his hands be under his thighs, so that the whole bladder may be sloping downwards.
Then examine him and touch him externally.
If therefore you feel a stone in the space, then quickly proceed with incision upon it.
But if it does not fall under your touch at all, then it is necessary to wipe your index finger with the oil of your left hand, if the patient is a boy; or the middle finger if he is a full-grown youth; and to insert it into his anus, and search for the stone until it is beneath your finger; and gradually turn it towards the neck of the bladder.
Then press upon it with your finger, and drive it outwards against the place whose incision you desire; and command a servant to press the bladder with his hand, and command another servant to raise the testicles with his right hand upwards; and with his other hand, to draw back the skin which is beneath the testicles, in the part adjacent to the place where the incision is.
Then insert the Spatumile, making an incision, whose shape is this. And make an incision in the area between the anus and testicles, and not in the middle, towards the left flank; and let the incision be over the stone, and your finger be in the anus; and let the incision be transverse, so that the incision is wide on the outside and narrow on the inside, according to the amount that it is possible for the stone to exit through it not larger.
For perhaps the finger, which is in the anus, compresses the stone near the incision, and it emerges without difficulty.
And know that if the stone has angles and edges, its exit becomes difficult because of that.
And if it is smooth, like a gland, and round, its exit becomes easy.
Therefore, to the one that has angles and edges, add in the fissure.
If it does not emerge thus, then it is necessary to devise something over it: either to string it over it with a carefully made instrument, whose tip is like the Moon, which strings over the stone and does not escape from it; or to introduce under it a subtle instrument with a curved tip.
If, however, you cannot over it, then enlarge the opening a little. If, however, anything from the blood overcomes you, cut it off with a zegi.
If, however, there is more than one stone, then drive the largest first into the bladder opening. Then cut over it. Afterwards, push the smaller one after that; and similarly do if there are more than two.
If it is very large, then it is ignorance to cut over it with a large incision. For since it happens to the patient one of two things, either that he dies, or that a constant dripping of urine occurs to him; because the place is not completely consolidated.
But administer its expulsion, until it emerges; or skillfully break it with scissors, until you extract it piece by piece.
When you have completed your operation, then fill the wound with frankincense, and aloe, and dragon's blood; and bind it, and place over it a cloth soaked in oil and wine, or rose oil, and cold water: that a hot abscess might settle.
Then lay the sick person on his back, and do not loosen the bandage until the third day. When you have therefore loosened it, embrocate the place with water and plenty of oil.
Then treat it with diafhinicon ointment and basilicon ointment, until it is healed.
If however in the wound a hot abscess has been added, and corrosive things, and similar things: such as if the blood should congeal in the bladder, and the urine should be prohibited from it; and it is recognized by the issue of blood with urine. Therefore introduce your finger into the wound, and extract that blood. For if it remains, it provokes the corruption of the bladder and its putrefaction.
Then wash the wound with vinegar, water, and salt, and apply whatever is suitable for treatment, until it is healed.
And it is necessary in the hours of the entire treatment, that you bind the two hips, and gather, for the reason of the medicines which are placed upon the place.
If a small stone is in the urethra, and is fixed in it, and the exit of urine is hindered, then treat it with what I described previously before you reach the stage of incision. For very often you will be spared incision through this treatment, for I have already proven it.
And it is so that you take a perforator of iron, whose form is this, a triangular tip, sharp, fixed in wood.
Then take thread, and tie the urethra with it under the stone so that it does not return to the bladder.
Then introduce the piercing iron into the urethra with ease, until the perforator reaches the stone itself: and turn the perforator with your hand on the stone gradually: and try to pierce it, until you make it penetrate from the other side.
For then urine is immediately discharged.
Then press your hand upon that which remains of the stone from the outer part of the urethra. For it will break, and exit with the urine, and the sick person will be healed.
If however this treatment is not prepared for you, due to something that prevents it: then tie a thread under the stone, and another thread above the stone. Then cut above the stone in the urethra itself between the two ligatures. Then extract it. Afterwards, loosen the ligature, and cleanse the congealed blood that forms in the wound.
And a ligature of thread beneath the stone is not necessary, unless so that it does not return to the bladder; and another ligature above it, unless so that when the thread is loosened after the stone's expulsion, the skin returns to its place and covers the wound.  And for that reason, it is necessary for you, when you tie the upper thread, to lift the skin upwards, so that it returns to its proper position and covers the wound, as we have said.
Chapter LXI.
Concerning the extraction of a stone in women.
Little stone is generated in women.
However, if a stone happens to any of them, then the cure is difficult, and it is prohibited due to many methods.
One of them is: that the woman is perhaps a virgin.
And the second is, because you do not find a woman who will reveal herself to a physician, when she is chaste, or having husbands.
The third is, because you do not find a woman well-knowing this art, especially the operation with the hand.
And the fourth is the distance from the stone's location: wherefore it needs deep incision, and in that is fear.
If necessity provokes it, then it is necessary that you take a woman physician, well-knowing: and little is found.
If truly you keep her private, then seek a chaste, subtle physician, and present a woman midwife well-taught in the matter of women, or a woman who indicates a part of the art in this skill: make her therefore present, and especially to her, that she do all that you command her from the inquiry about the stone.
First, that she look to see if the woman is a virgin: then it is necessary that she insert her finger into her anus, and seek the stone.
If, therefore, she finds it, she should constrict it under her finger, and then command the midwife to insert her finger into the vagina of the sick woman, and inquire about the stone, after placing her left hand upon the bladder, and compressing it with a good compression.
If, therefore, she finds the stone, then it is necessary that she gradually move it from the opening of the bladder downwards with all her strength, until she reaches it with the root of the thigh.
Then she should cut upon it at the opposing midpoint of the vulva, at the root of the thigh, from whichever side it is prepared for her, and she feels the stone in that part: and her finger should not be removed from the stone, constricting it underneath: and let the incision be small at first. Then insert a rod over that incision.
Then insert a rod over that incision. If, therefore, she feels the stone, then she should add to the incision according to the proportionate amount that the stone emerges from it.
And know that the types of stone are many. For among these there is a small one, and a large one, a smooth and a rough one, a long one, and a round one having branches.
Know therefore its types, so that this may signify to you concerning that which you desire.
If, therefore, blood conquers you, then add to the place ground zegi, and hold it for one hour, until the blood is cut off.
Then return to your operation until the stone emerges, and do so as to prepare yourself with the instruments, which I have said concerning the extraction of stone in men, so that you may be assisted by them in your operation.
If, however, a flow of blood conquers you, and you know that the expulsion of blood is from an artery that has been cut: then put powder upon the place, and bind it with bandages fittingly tight: and do not change it: and leave the stone, and do not extract it. For perhaps the weak woman will die. Then treat the wound.
When, therefore, the sharpness of the blood subsides after days, and the place becomes putrid, then return to your operation, until the stone emerges.
Chapter LXII.
On the sectioning of a watery hernia.
A watery hernia is not present unless there is an aggregation of moisture in a white sifac, which is beneath the skin containing the testicle and egg; and it is called a sifac.
And sometimes it is in its own membrane, which nature prepared for it in part of the egg, until it becomes rotten; which is another egg, and is the skin of the testicle between the white sifac, which we said: and that does not occur except rarely.
And indeed the generation of this hernia is from weakness that occurs in the testicles; wherefore this matter is discharged to them. And sometimes it occurs from a blow upon the testicles.
And this moisture has many colours. For either its colour inclines to yellowness, or it is blood-red, or foul, black, or watery white, and there is that which is more.
And the signs by which it is known where the collection of water is, are these. For if it is in the white sifac that we have mentioned, then the abscess is round to a small length like the shape of an egg: and the testicle does not appear, because the moisture surrounds it on every side.
And if the moisture is in the proper pannicle to itself, then the abscess is round with a portion of an egg, and on account of this a man estimates that it is another egg.
And if the moisture is between the skin of the testicles and the white sifac, then it falls under the senses.
Therefore, when you want knowledge of the color of the moisture, then experience the color of the moisture in the abscess with the inserted square, the form of which preceded. Therefore, according to that which emerges in the trace of the Intromission, judge of that which is between it.
Therefore, when we come to treatment with iron, then it is necessary that you instruct the sick person that he be bled if it is possible for him, and you see his body full.
Then he will be laid upon his back upon a slightly elevated object, and place many cloths beneath him.
Then sit you on his left, and instruct the minister to sit on his right, who will extend his rod to one of the two sides of the skin of the testicles, and to the part of the navel of the belly.
Then take a broad spatula, and cut the skin of the testicles from the middle lengthwise, up to near the pubis; and let the incision be according to the straightness of an equidistant line which divides the skin of the testicles, until it reaches the white sifac encompassing it; and scrape it and observe, or beware from this, that you dissect it; and let your scraping, with which you scrape it, be from the part that hangs more with the egg; and subtly examine it with the scraping according to the amount that is possible for you.
Then pierce the sifac full of water with a wide perforation, and extract all the water.
Then separate the two lips of the incision with hooks, and stretch the sifac upwards, and do not touch the skin of the testicles encompassing it; and cut the sifac however it is possible for you to cut it, either with its totality, or in fragments, especially its thin side. For if you do not diligently examine it, security is not achieved in its incision, lest the water return.
If, however, the egg proceeds outward from its skin during the time of your operation: then, when you have completed the incision of the sifac, return it. Then gather the two lips of the skin of the testicles with sutures.
Then treat it as the other wounds until it heals.
If you find a corrupted egg by reason of another blackness, then it is necessary that you tie the vessels, which are the suspensories, fearing the flow of blood.
Then cut off the testicle with the suspensory, and remove the egg; afterwards treat it with what we have said.
If, however, water is collected in two parts, then know that there are two branches; therefore cut the other part in the same manner as you did the other, equally.
And if it is equal to you, so that it is one operation, then do it; afterwards wipe away the blood. And introduce into the sections wool soaked in oil, or in rose oil; and place outside wool which you have soaked in wine and oil, and spread it over the two testicles and the middle of the belly; and place over that folded cloths, and they are cushions; and tie them over with a bandage, which has six according to this form.
Then take two cloths, and sew them together, and fill them with dyed wool, according to this likeness; and make the ends with which it is pressed of cotton, or damp wool; and let them be gathered with a constriction at the lumbar part of the breeches; and one end take over the anus up to the back, and the second take to the part of the pubic bone; and two ends over the two thighs below, and two other ends over the two groins, and the whole constriction be gathered at the lumbar part of the breeches.
And, sometimes, this branch is artificially treated with a cautery in the place of the incision with iron; and it is that you take a fine, knife-like cautery and cut with it the skin of the testicles; and it is heated, according to what we have described; until, when a white substance containing water is revealed, then take the cautery, according to this form.
It resembles the Greek letter.
Then pierce it with it, and it is heated, until all the moisture comes out.
Then extend the sifac with hooks, and scrape it with a heated fine cautery, and cut it as much as possible, until you eradicate the whole of it.
And beware of the egg, lest the fire touch it.
And know that this operation is safer, and further from blood flow, and better than an operation with a cut.
If, however, opposition occurs to you during the hour of the incision, or during the hour of the cautery, a hot abscess, or blood flow, or something else: then it is necessary to treat that which causes opposition to you from it with the cure that suits it, as has been stated.
If, however, the patient is timid and does not expect this operation, because of its horror: then administer a perforation, as I describe it: and it is.
When it is certified to you by what we have said about signs that the limb is watery, then it is necessary that you make the patient sit on a raised seat.
Then pierce the abscess in its lower part with a wide spatula: and place the perforation along the length of the body, and let the opening be wide, until all the water runs out.
Then place a poultice over the affected area, and bandage it, and allow it to drain the remaining water; then treat it, until the wound is healed.
If, however, there is resistance to the egress of water at the time you pierce it: and this is only because a white membrane causes resistance in the wound opening and prevents the escape of water: then it is necessary that you insert a pen into the wound, or an instrument with which the water of hydrops is extracted, whose form preceded: or add to the operation of the wound for a short time.
And know that water returns and collects after six months, or about that time. When it collects, then it should be pierced according to the same method, and the sick person will pass their days.
Chapter LXIII.
Concerning the treatment of fleshy tumors.
Know that in the section above this tumor, there is fear leading to harm in many dispositions. Therefore, it seems to me that its dismissal is better than that.
And I am recalling the operation on it, and the appearance of the abscess.
Therefore, I say that many abscesses occur in the bodies from which the testicles are composed; and that is made from many causes, namely.
Or from acute or acrid superfluity, which is poured out to the testicles. Or from a blow.
And the color of the abscess is according to the color of the body, and no pain is found in it; and it is a severe abscess.
And perhaps it is a stony abscess, whose color is dark, and there is no feeling in it.
