And sometimes it arises from the knotting of arteries and their inflation, as its prior mention has already shown, or from the inflation of veins.
Therefore, that which arises from the inflation of arteries is recognized by the disposition of the abscess, when you compress it with your fingers. And you need not touch it entirely.
But that which arises from the inflation of veins does not disperse anything from it upon compression with the fingers.
And now it is possible that a section be made upon this type, and upon the remaining flesh: and it is, as if you separate the skin of the testicles. Then extend the egg upward, and extract it with a white sifach: and free the suspensory from the vessels, and tie the vessels: and cut the suspensory after you have freed it from all parts of the egg.
If therefore the egg is consolidated with those born flesh, then it is necessary to pull the egg, and cut it.
And if there is an adhesion between some siphaket, or in that which is between the vessels: then it is necessary to free all that adhesion, and cut it with a round incision.
If however there is a nativity of flesh in the place of adhesion that is behind, then it is necessary to cut all of it, and extract the egg, as we said.
When therefore your operation is complete, then fill the wound with wool soaked in rose oil and wine; then treat it with the rest of the treatment, until it is healed.
Chapter LXIV.
On the treatment of Ramicis, which is with Vite.
Vitis is an abscess enveloped by a certain covering, similar to a bunch of grapes, with a softening of the testicles; and it makes difficult for the infirm movement and exercise, and walking.
And in the treatment of this illness there is fear, because of that which preceded it being remembered.
But it is necessary, that we say, the operation in it according to what the first ones did.
I say therefore, that it is necessary that you make the infirm person sit on a raised seat. Then push the suspensory of the testicles downwards. Then grasp the skin of the testicles with your fingers, with the vessels that are near the penis; and let another attendant hold them, and extend them with a violent extension.
Then cut with a broad, sharp spatule in a transverse section directly over the vessels, until the vessels are exposed.
Then scrape away from every side, as we said to you in the extraction of the arteries that are in the temples.
Afterwards, insert into them a needle with a double thread, and tie it at the first place where the veins occur; and tie it again at their last. Then cut them in the middle, in a straight section along the length of the body, and extract that which is collected from corrupt, foul humors.
Then treat the wound with the treatment of other wounds, in which the force generates pus, so that the vessels that nourish one testicle may fall. For there is no harm through it.
However, if it happens to all the vessels, then it is necessary that the testicles be extracted with the vessels, so that the egg is not deprived of nourishment due to the incision and decays or dries up, and there is no benefit with them.
Chapter LXV.
Concerning the treatment of intestinal worms.
The outcome of this worm is from a section, which happens in the extended sifach above the abdomen in the groin area from a wound of the belly.
Why do the intestines descend from that rupture to one testicle. And this rupture is either from a sifach, or an extension of it.
And these two types happen from many causes: either from a blow, or a jump, or a cry, or lifting a heavy object, and similar things.
And a sign, when it is from the extension of sifach. It is that which happens gradually over a long time, and does not happen suddenly: and it is an abscess equal to the depth: because the sifach compresses the intestine.
And a sign of it when it is from a section of sifach, because it causes a severe pain to occur suddenly at its beginning: and it is a different abscess, appearing under the skin nearby: and that is because of the exit of the intestine, and its course to the exterior of the sifach.
And sometimes it emerges with the intestine as a zirbum: and this is called a Ramix intestinalis, and zirbia: and sometimes it happens with flatulence.
And sometimes faeces run into the intestine, and are retained there: and there is harm with it for the sick person: because it causes a mild pain, and especially when pressed.
And in the treatment of these types of illness, there is fear with iron; therefore, it is necessary to avoid such an event.
And the method of operation is that you should instruct the sick person to draw back the intestine with his own hand to the inner cavity of his body, if it yields to being pulled back. Then he should be laid back on his neck between your hands, and his legs raised.
Then stretch the skin which follows the skin upwards, and cut the skin of the testicles completely lengthwise. Then place hooks in the two lips of the incision, according to what you need to open the incision with them, and let the incision be according to the amount that is possible, so that the egg may emerge from it.
Then scrape away the siphak and whatever is under the skin of the testicles, until when the white, hard siphak is uncovered on all sides; then insert your index finger into that which follows the testicle, into that which is between the white siphak, which is under the skin of the egg, and the second siphak, and detach with it the adhesion which is on the posterior part of the egg.
Then move with your right hand the skin of the testicles inwards; and with this, stretch the white siphak upwards with your left hand, and lift the egg with the siphak towards the incision; and command the servant to stretch the egg upwards, and you detach the adhesion, which is behind the complete separation.
And search with your fingers to see that there is nothing there of the intestine wrapped in the hard white siphak. If, however, you find anything from it, then push it towards the lower abdomen.
Then take a needle, on which is thick thread, made of linen from ten threads, and insert it at the last siphak, that is, the tunic, which is under the skin of the testicles that follows the incision; then cut the ends which protrude from the thread, until there are four threads. Then arrange some of them over some with a figure which is in the manner of a cross; and tie them with the alsifach, of which we spoke, because it is beneath the skin of the testicles with a strong ligature, until no vessel, which nourishes them, can send anything to them: so that no hot abscess may occur from this. And place again a ligature second, outside the first ligature, distant from it by less than two fingers. And after these two ligatures, leave from the siphak, which is beneath the skin of the testicles, a quantity the size of a finger; and cut the rest entirely around, and remove the egg from it.
Then cut in the lower part of the skin of the testicles the incision, from which runs blood and pus, as we have narrated in that which is said before.
Then apply wool soaked in oil, and place it in the wound; and apply the ligature that we have narrated. And sometimes the white sifach that we spoke of is cauterized, after its incision, fearing a flow of blood. For this often happens. And leave the ligature until it falls off by itself.
If, however, its falling is delayed, then it should be moistened with hot water; and place upon it that which we have mentioned, until it falls off.
Then treat the wound with the rest of the treatment for wounds, until it is healed.
Chapter LXVI.
Concerning the windy (or:  windy-disease) ram.
In this ram disease, I have not seen anyone who dared to use iron in its treatment.
And indeed the first ones said that it occurs as we have described in the ram disease that is with the vine. That is, tie the vessels after the incision over them easily from below; then cut in the middle, and finally treat with whatever opens it, until the vessels fall away; and treat the wound as we have said until it heals.
Chapter LXVII.
Concerning the rupture that occurs in the inguinal region.
When a rupture occurs in the groin, as we said, and the area protrudes, and nothing from the intestines descends into the scrotum: and if something does descend, it is little, and returns every hour: but if the time passes with this, a fissure is added to the sifach, until the intestines descend into the sifach.
And that happens from the stretching of the sifach, which is in the groin, as we said. This is because the sifach stretches; then the area softens, and the place becomes suppurated, and protrudes.
And its treatment is with a cautery, as its previous description stated.
And sometimes it is treated with iron, according to this method: and it is that the sick person lie on their back between your hands. Then cut the suppurated and protruding place of the rupture, cutting across the width, according to the amount of three fingers.
Then draw tight the alsifak and what is under the skin, until the white sifach is revealed, which is under the skin that follows it.
Then take a rod, and place it on the protruding place of the sifach, and press it into the depth of the belly.
Then sew the two protruding places on the end of the rod from the sifach, and connect one of them to the other with a suture; then pull out the end of the rod.
And do not cut the alsifach entirely, nor stretch the egg, nor anything else, as I have made you know in the treatment of the intestinal ramicis.
Then treat it with the treatment of wounds. When therefore you cut the threads, cleanse it, and restore the wound, until it is consolidated.
For the sisach contracts, and does not add to the prominence of the rupture.
And the cautery is better in this place, because it is nearer to health.
Chapter LXVIII.
On the softening of the skin of the testicles.
The skin of the testicles is often softened in some men, because of which the flesh between them is softened, and its appearance is marred.
Therefore, it is necessary for him who is asked to treat it, that he make the sick man lie on his back, and cut all the softened skin over the natural skin.
Then, he shall bring together the two folds with sutures: and if he wishes, he shall first suture the excess of softened skin with three or four sutures, and make the sutures firm.
Then, cut between the sutures.
Then, treat the treatment of the remaining wounds, until it is healed and the threads fall out.
Chapter LXIX.
Concerning Castration.
Know that castration is excommunicated in our law, and because of that it would be permissible for me not to recall it in this book of mine; and its mention is not necessary except for two ways.
One of which is that it should be in the physician's knowledge when questioned about it, and so that he may know its treatment for the one who has suffered it.
And another way is that we frequently require the castration of certain animals, for our benefit through it, such as rams, boars, and cats, and similar animals.
Therefore, I say that castration is done according to two methods. Or by attrition. Or by fissure and section.
In that which is done by attrition, the method of operation is: that we make the animal sit in warm water, until its testicles are softened and relaxed and hang down; then rub them with your hand until they are dissolved and do not appear under the touch.
In castration by fissure and section, it is necessary that you hold the animal and press the skin of its testicles with your left hand.
Then tie the suspensory ligament, and cut above each testicle with a single incision, until two testicles are exposed.  Then cut them after you have peeled off each, and do not leave on them any sifaket other than the thin sifac, which is over the vessels.
And this method of castration is better than that which is done by attrition, because attrition perhaps leaves something of the testicles, wherefore the animal desires intercourse.
Then treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter 70.
Concerning the treatment of Hermaphrodites, there are two methods.
A hermaphrodite is from men in two ways. One of them is because a space appears in it, or in the skin of the testicles, in the area between the two testicles, a figure like a woman's vulva, in which there are hairs; and sometimes urine runs from what is in the skin of the testicles.
In women, however, there is one species, and it is a woman's vulva above the pubis, like very small male testicles, protruding outward; of which one is like a man's penis, and two like two testicles.
And the treatment of three species, two from men, and one species from women, is: because it is necessary to cut away the added flesh, until the impression of them is gone. Then treat them with the treatment of the remaining wounds.
In the second species of men, from which urine emerges, which is in the skin of the testicles, there is no operation, nor is there any cure for it at all.
Chapter LXXI.
Concerning the incision of the Tentigo, and the protruding flesh in the vulvas of women.
Tentigo perhaps grows over the natural state, until its appearance becomes unsightly and foul: and sometimes in some women it magnifies so much, until it expands like in men, and reaches the point of intercourse.
Therefore, you should hold the excess Tentigo with your hand or a hook, and cut it; and do not stop cutting, especially at the deep root, so that a flow of blood does not occur.
Then treat it with the treatment for wounds until it heals.
However, the flesh that arises is flesh that grows in the orifice of the womb until it fills it: and perhaps it emerges to the exterior in the likeness of a tail; and because of this some earlier writers call this illness "tailed disease. Therefore, it is necessary to cut it like we cut Tentigo, and to treat it until it heals.
Concerning the treatment of Alratica, which is the unpierced vulva of a woman.
Chapter LXXII.
Alratica is, that the vulva of a woman is not perforated, or the opening is small. And it is either natural or accidental.
Accidental is it from an illness that preceded it: and indeed it is made either from added born flesh, or from thin or thick syrphach: and it is made either in the deep of the matrix, or in its sides, or in its upper or lower part: and it prevents intercourse and impregnation, and childbirth, and perhaps it prevents menstruation.
And its knowledge is made through the observation of the midwife, if an apparent blockage is nearby.
If however it is not apparent, then seek it with your fingers, or with a probe.
If however the blockage is from thin syrphach, and is near the two lips: then hurry, and break it.
And it is, that you place cushions on the two lips.
Then place your index fingers of both hands together, and let the woman lie on her back with her legs outstretched. Then spread the two lips with strength, until the thin syrphach is broken, and the blockage is opened.
Then take wool, and soak it in oil, and place it over the place: and let the woman apply it daily, so that the place will not consolidate again.
If however the sifach is thick, dense: then it is necessary to cut it with a spatula broad like a myrtle leaf.
If however the blockage is of flesh, then suspend it with hooks, and cut it: and let there be with you medicines that curb the flow of blood without causing a bite, such as acacia, and dragon's blood, and frankincense mixed with egg white.
Then administer a wide lead cannula, so that the wound does not heal quickly, hold it for a few days: and administer lint made from dried linen, then treat the remainder with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
And sometimes a birth of other flesh grows in the womb: therefore it is necessary that they be cut according to this method, as long as it is not a cancerous abscess; for a cancerous abscess that is in the womb, no opposition should be made with iron at all.
Chapter LXXIII.
Concerning the treatment of hemorrhoids, Athehelil, and red Bothor in women's vulvas.
Hemorrhoids indeed are an opening of the veins' orifices, until much blood flows from them continuously.
When hemorrhoids become chronic, they become thelil; and I have already stated in the division of their species and their signs; and I shall say here which of them receive treatment, and which do not.
Therefore I say that hemorrhoids which are in the orifice of the womb, and do not appear to the senses, then there is no treatment with iron for them.
However, those of them which are in the orifice of the womb over which sensation falls, are those which are treated.
Therefore it is necessary that you introduce the woman into a cold house. Then extend the thelil with scissors, or with a rough cloth, and cut them from their roots.
Then powder over them at the flow of blood from one of the powdered incisions without mastication: such as acacia, and dragon's blood, and frankincense, and things similar to them.
Then let him drive her feet to the wall for one hour. Afterwards, lay her down in cold water.
If the flow of blood persists, then make her sit in a decoction of sumach, and quince bark, and galls, and similar things.
Then take barley flour, and mix it with honey and vinegar, and make a plaster with it on her back.
If, therefore, blood is cut off with it, it is good; and if not, make cupping vessels adhere to the back and under the breasts, without incision.
Then take wool, and immerse it in the juice of tarathit, or the juice of arnogloss, or the juice of alaulat branches, that is, aglanter, or Pontic wine, and make it adhere to the wound.
When, therefore, the abscess rests, then treat the place with ointments, until it is healed.
Albarth, however, is red, similar to the heads of alhaasse, and is of harsh appearance.
Therefore, it is necessary that you cut what appears from it, according to what I said about the thelil equally: and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter LXXIV.
Concerning the perforation of the passage that occurs in the womb.
Many kinds of abscesses occur in the womb, such as cancer, and stony abscess, and ulcers, and inflamed swellings, and alekilati, that is, herpes, erysipelas, and fistulas, and hemorrhoids, and fissures, and thelil, and a hot abscess.
And indeed I have already mentioned all these illnesses and their types, and their signs, and their cures in the division: it is necessary that I say in this treatise about the hot abscess that occurs in the womb, when it is from the abscesses that collect pus, and you wish to know how its opening is made with iron.
Therefore, it is necessary to consider. Then, if the pain of the abscess in its inception is acute with its percussion, and inflammation, and fever: and you see it red: if feeling falls upon it, then do not hurry with its perforation: and treat it with that which aids in maturation, until when the acuteness of the occurrences subsides.
Then it is necessary that you make the woman sit on a seat that slopes down two seats, and that she reclines, or is supported on her back, and that she draws her feet to the lower part of her belly: and that her thighs be spread open, and that she place her arms under her knees, and that she bind each with a suitable bandage to it.
Then let the midwife sit on the right side, and administer the instrument with which the opening of the womb is opened.
And it is necessary, when you wish to administer this instrument, that you fix it with the rod deep in the woman's womb, so that what enters from the instrument is not more than the depth of the womb and harms the weak.
However, if the instrument is longer than the depth, then it is necessary that you place cushions over the depth of the womb: so that the entrance of the entire instrument into the womb's depth is prevented: and it is necessary that it place the pivot, which runs along the instrument, from the upper side; and that it hold the instrument, and that the attendant turn the pivot, until the depth of the womb is opened.
When therefore the exit appears, and is touched by the hand, and is smooth and soft, then it is necessary that you separate it at its head with a broad spatula.
When therefore the pus is evacuated, then it is necessary that you place on the wound a linen cloth soaked in rose oil, or green oil, in which there is some astringency; and place the cloth, and extract the section within the depth of the womb.
And place externally on the womb, and upon the vulva, clean wool soaked in water, in which mallows have been boiled.
Then treat it after two days with an ointment suitable for it, until it heals.
And sometimes the womb and wound are washed, so that it is injected with water and honey; then with water in which the lily root or long aristolochia has been boiled; then return to the ointment.
If the wound is hidden internally within the womb, then it is necessary that you refrain from treating it with iron; rather, treat it with what we said in the division.
Chapter LXX.
Doctrines of Midwives, how living embryos are treated, having emerged in a non-natural form.
It is necessary for the midwife to know first the form of natural birth.
Therefore, among its signs is this: when you see a woman pressing downwards, and she desires to smell the air, and she is relieved of what is upon her from the birth, and the exit of the embryo is hastened: then know that this birth is according to the natural form, and its exit is upon her head, and the afterbirth with it: or suspended, or hanging from the navel.
Therefore, when you see these signs, then it is necessary that her abdomen be compressed, so that the embryo descends quickly.
For when it descends upon her head, the afterbirth descends with it, and she is cleansed from those superfluities with good cleansing.
However, any birth other than this one is unnatural, blameworthy.
For sometimes the fetus emerges on its feet, and its body emerges before its head and its feet, and its hand or foot alone; or its head and hand emerge; or the belly emerges; and perhaps it turns on its neck, and similar forms are blameworthy.
Therefore, it is necessary for the midwife to be prudent, subtle in all these matters, and to beware of error and deception; and I am showing every form of them, and how the nature of it is, and how it is to be signified and to stand over it.
When the fetus emerges on its head in its natural emergence, and it becomes violent upon the woman, and her labor becomes difficult, and you see her strength already weakened, then make her sit on a seat, or let women hold her; and apply vapors to her vulva with decoction of fenugreek and with moist oils.
Then let the midwife insert a small spatula between her fingers, and with it split the afterbirth, and cut it with her fingernail until that which is in it from moisture runs out; and press the woman's belly until the fetus descends.
If it does not descend, then the woman must be given an enema of fenugreek mucilage with sesame oil.
Then command her, after the enema, to compress herself; and make her sneeze with seasonings, and hold her mouth and nose for one hour. The fetus will emerge immediately.
If the fetus's two hands emerge first, it is necessary to gently and gradually draw them back. If, however, it does not yield on entry, then place the woman on a seat, and raise her feet up; then shake the seat on the ground; and let the woman be held so that she does not fall during the shaking.
If, therefore, the hands do not emerge, and the fetus dies, then cut them and extract the remaining fetus; and bind its hand with cloth, and extract it with evenness; for it will emerge.
The exit of the embryo is above its feet. When the fetus emerges above its feet, then it is necessary to push them upwards; then turn the fetus gradually; afterwards, straighten it.
Then take one of its feet and extend it gradually.
When, therefore, they emerge up to the hips, then command him to be compressed, and make him sneeze with condiments; for it will emerge, if God wills.
And if it does not emerge with what we have recounted, then return the fetus gradually, until you place it in its natural form. For it will then emerge with ease.
If it is prevented with all that we have recounted, then take from marshmallow mucilage, and sesame oil, and dissolved gum: and pound all in a mortar with good pounding: then line with it the woman's vulva and the lower part of her abdomen. Then make her sit in lukewarm water, until she achieves hypocundria.
When therefore you see that her lower parts are now warmed, then make her a poultice of myrrh and apply it. When therefore the poultice has held for one hour, then make her sit on a seat. Then make her sneeze: and bind her mouth and nose: and press the lower part of her abdomen with gentle pressure. For the fever will depart immediately.
The birth of the fetus is on the knees and hands. When it emerges in this manner, then gently insert her hands. Then the woman should lie on her back, with her feet hanging down: and she should lie on the bed, and fold her hands. Then press gently on her abdomen. Then remove what is under her feet from the pillows, until it is prepared at the lower part. If the fetus does not emerge, then take her feet and move them with a forceful motion. Then press gently on what is above the hypochondria, until the fetus ascends to the upper part. Then let her introduce her hands, and gently adjust the fetus: and order the woman to compress, until the fetus emerges.
The exit of the fetus hanging laterally from the hands. Let his hand be inserted in the reduction. If however he cannot reduce it, then lift the woman, and make her walk.
If however she cannot walk, then make her lie down on a bed: and shake her legs with a violent shaking.
Then administer the liniment which you have prepared from mucilages.
If, however, childbirth takes it, then make her sit on a seat. Then strive in the reduction of the hand, and bring the fetus back to its natural form: and make the woman sneeze, and order her to press herself, until it emerges.
The exit of the fetus is upon her neck, with hands outstretched, and its face towards her mother's back. The midwife ought to hold the hand of the fetus, then gradually turn it over, and bring it into alignment with ease.
When, therefore, it is aligned, then place upon her vulva that which we have described from oil and mucilages.
Then order her to move gradually. When you have done that, then make her sit on a seat, and order her to lean to her right side. Then order her to press [herself] and make her sneeze with a condiment.
The exit of the prepared fetus on its side. When it descends to the opening of the womb in this manner with the afterbirth, then cut the afterbirth with your fingernail until it is evacuated from moisture.
If therefore it hangs over the face of the fetus, and its neck, then cut it from the navel, so that it is not retained, and dies.
Then push the fetus inward until you place it as it should be, and order the woman to press herself several times; for it will emerge through a light exit.
The exit of twins, or many fetuses. Know that twins are often born, and sometimes three or four are born. However, that is unusual and rarely happens.
However, five is a thing outside nature, and they do not live at all; and one afterbirth contains them, however numerous, and they are separated by a membrane, distinguishing between them, tied to the navel of each of them.
And sometimes they emerge as a single fetus emerges by a natural, easy exit, and they emerge with an ignominious exit, as we have narrated.
If some of them are difficult at birth, and reach the condition we have described from unnatural figures, then treat them as we have narrated; and let the midwife be skillful, and let her do what she does with ease and deliberation, and let her devise according to every form with what is prepared for her from ingenuity leading to health.
A reminder of that which is formed in the womb from embryos, and are aborted.
Sometimes one, two, three, four, five, six, seven, and more than ten are formed in the womb; and it has been verified with me that a woman, when she miscarried, expelled seven, and another fifteen, all formed.
That which was done by the measurement of the glorious wise one.
Therefore, this entire matter must be within the knowledge of the Midwife. For perhaps it will come to her similar to that; therefore, let her oppose it with that which is appropriate.
Chapter LXXVI.
Concerning the extraction of a dead fetus.
When you treat a dead fetus with what I have said in the division of medications, and it does not emerge, and you are forced to use an operation with the hand, then you must consider.
If therefore the woman is of sound constitution, and is not afflicted by any of the accidents from which harm is feared for her: then it is necessary that the woman be laid supine on a bed, on her back, and her head be lowered, and her legs raised; and the attendant shall hold her from two sides, or she shall be bound to the bed, so that her body is not disturbed during the extraction of the fetus.
Then moisten the mouth of the womb with humid oils with the mucilage of marshmallow, and fenugreek, and linseed.
Then the midwife shall anoint her hand with these oils and mucilages, then shall introduce it into the womb with ease. Then she shall seek with it the place where she shall fix the hooks on the fetus, and the places suitable for that.
Therefore consider then, if the fetus is prepared to descend upon its head: then fix the hooks in its eyes, or in its neck, or in its mouth, or in its palate, or under its chin, or in its fork, or in the places near the ribs, and under the hypochondria.
If however the descent is upon its feet, then in its pubis and middle ribs.
And it is necessary that she hold the hook with her right hand, and place its curvature in that which is between the fingers of her left hand, and introduce it with her hand with ease, and fix the hook in any of those places we have mentioned, until it reaches the empty space.
Then she shall fix another hook, or a third if needed, in opposition to it, so that there is an equal contraction and it does not lean to one side.
Then let it be extended with equal extension, and let the extension not only be straight, but rather move in every direction, so that its exit may be eased; as is done with a tooth during its extraction.
And it is necessary that the extension be softened in that which is between them. If a part of it is retained, then the midwife should anoint some fingers with oil and insert them from the side, so that she may turn with them that which is retained.
If, therefore, a part of the fetus emerges, then the hooks should be moved to another, higher place; do this until the entire fetus emerges.
If, therefore, a hand emerges before the other parts, and it is not possible to return it, because of its constriction, then it is necessary to wrap a cloth around it. Then draw it out until, when the whole emerges, cut it either at the ankle joint or at the elbow; and thus it is necessary that you do in the other hand and in the two feet.
If, however, the fetus's head is large, or constriction occurs in its exit, or water is gathered in its head, then it is necessary that they insert a thorny Spatumile between the fingers, and split the head with it, and extract the water; or break it with the instrument called Almisdac, whose form comes with the forms of the other instruments in the chapter that follows this one.
Similarly, if the fetus has a naturally large head, then it is necessary to cut away the obstructing parts; or extract it with misdach, as we have said. Then pull out the bones with forceps.
If the head emerges and is constricted at the fork, then it should be split until the moisture in the chest is discharged.
If however it is not tangled, nor yields to the exit, then cut the head into pieces, according as is possible for you.
If the lower part of the abdomen has an abscess, or has dropsy in it, then it is necessary to pierce it until moisture flows from it.
If the fetus is positioned above its feet, then its extraction is easy, and its direction to the opening of the womb is gentle.
If, however, it is constricted at the abdomen or chest, then it is necessary to extract it with a cloth that you have wrapped around your hand, and split the abdomen or chest until that which is in them flows out.
If the other limbs are removed, and the head returns, then the midwife should insert her left hand. If, therefore, the opening of the womb is open, then she should insert her hand deep into the womb, and search with it for the head, and pull it with her fingers up to the opening of the womb; then she should insert one hook, or two hooks, into it, and pull it with that.
If, however, the opening of the womb is constricted, due to a hot abscess that has occurred in it, then it is not necessary to exert effort on it, rather it is necessary to administer the outflow of humid, oily things, and apply plasters, and she should sit in waters that soften and moisten.
If the fetus is on its side, then it is possible to equalize it; then administer what we have said about a living fetus.
If that is not possible, then it should be cut into pieces and extracted.
And it is necessary that you not leave any part of the afterbirth inside at all.
When you have completed your operation, then administer what we have said about hot abscesses that occur in the wombs.
If a flow of blood occurs, then it should be placed in styptic waters, and administer the rest of the treatment.
And I myself have already seen a woman, in whose belly a dead fetus was: then she became pregnant again over it, afterwards another fetus died as well. Therefore, after a long time, an abscess occurred in her navel, and it became swollen until it opened, and began to discharge pus.
I was therefore called to treat her, and I medicated her for a long time, and the wound did not heal.
Therefore, I placed upon it some strong-drawing plasters, and bone came out from the place. Then a few days passed, and another bone came out. I was therefore amazed by this, since there is no bone in the belly.
I therefore thought that they were from the bones of a dead fetus.
I therefore investigated the wound, and extracted many bones, the woman being in her better dispositions: and she has now lived thus for a long time, and a little pus comes out from the place.
And I would not have affirmed this extraneous thing, unless there is knowledge and assistance in it, which the skilled physician administers with the hand from the cure.
Chapter LXXVII.
Concerning the forms of necessary instruments in the extraction of a fetus.
The form of the turn by which the matrix is opened.
Here below is the form of a Straighter, by which books are made equal, which equally has two turns at the two ends of two pieces of wood. However, this turn must be finer than the straighter; and to make it, either from ebony or from boxwood.
And the width of each piece of wood is about two fingers; and the margin of each of the two is about one finger, and the length of each of them is a palm and a half; and in the middle of the two pieces of wood are two additions of the same kind of wood, which are now fixed to both, whose length is half a palm and a little more; and the width of each is about two fingers, or a little more.
And these two additions are inserted into the opening of the matrix, so that it opens with them when you turn the two turns.
Another form similar to it, finer and lighter.
Let it be made of ebony wood, or boxwood, according to the likeness of forceps. However, at its extremity there are two additions, as you see here above: and the length of each of these additions is about a palm: and the width of each is two fingers.
When therefore you wish to open the matrix with it, then make the woman sit on a bed, with her feet hanging down, opening what is between her legs. Then introduce these two joined additions into the matrix: and you are holding the lower end of the instrument between her thighs. Then open your hand with the instrument, as you do with scissors, equally according to the amount you wish to open the mouth of the matrix, until the midwife does what she wishes.
Another form of Vertigo, which the ancients called the Form of the Impeller, by which the fetus is impelled.
The Almisdac form, by which the large head is crushed: and it is likened to a pair of tongs, which has teeth at the end, as you see.
And sometimes it becomes oblong, like tongs of this form, as you see, which has teeth like saw teeth, with which it is cut and crushed.
The form of a hook with a single spine.
The form of a hook with two spines.
The form of a scalpel with two ends for severing the head.
The form of two broad scalpels.
All these instruments are used, and the more their types are multiplied, and they are prepared by the craftsman, the quicker he is at his work, and the higher he is esteemed among people, because of their quantity.
Therefore, do not despise any instrument of them, lest it be not prepared for you. For their necessity is inexcusable.
Chapter LXXVIII.
Concerning the extraction of the Secundine.
When the Secundine is retained at childbirth, then it is necessary that you instruct the woman to hold her breath. Then make her sneeze with condiments: and press your hand upon her mouth and nostrils. If therefore it emerges by this, it is good.
If not, then take a pot, and pierce a hole in the lid: and place in it herbs that open the mother's mouth, such as calamint, rue, dill, chamomile, seham, cinnamon, and centaury: take all these herbs, or some of them, and immerse them in water, and place them over the fire.
Then place a reed plug over the hole of the pot's lid, and the other end in the woman's womb: and let her hold it until the vapor reaches the cavity of the womb.
Then make her sneeze, as we said. For the afterbirth will emerge with ease.
However, if it remains after this treatment, and does not emerge, then instruct the midwife to immerse her hand in sesame oil, or marshmallow mucilage. Then let her introduce it into the vagina, and seek out the afterbirth with it. When therefore she finds it, then let her take it, and extend it gradually.
However, if it is attached deep within the womb, then let her introduce her hand, as we have described, until when she finds the afterbirth, and draw it out gradually in a straight line, so that the womb does not fall during a forceful pulling: indeed, it is necessary that it is moved with ease to the right and left sides, and then the pulling force should be increased. For it will then yield, and be preserved, and escape from the adhesion.
If the mouth of the womb is constricted, as we have already described: treat it with sneezing, and with medication from a pot with herbs.
If, however, it does not emerge with all that we have described, then beware of tiring yourself out in repeated attempts at extraction.
But it is necessary that whatever emerges from it to the mouth of the womb be tied to the woman's hip. Then inject her with a clyster of tetrapharmacon ointment. For this will cause it to putrefy after days, and it will dissolve, and emerge.
However, when it putrefies, an evil odor rises from it to the stomach and head, and injures the weak one. Therefore, appropriate fumigations should be administered to it.
Some of the ancients have already experienced fumigation with nasturtium and dried figs.
The form of the instrument with which one fumigates a woman in the retention of the Secundinae and menses.
Let it be made of glass similar to a lamp, or of earthenware: and let the wide end be placed over the fire: and the fumigation be placed over the embers: let her hold it, until that fumigation is consumed: and again another.
Chapter LXXIX.
Concerning the treatment of an unperforated anus.
Many children are born with their anuses unperforated, which the subtle sifac has already obstructed. Therefore, the midwife should pierce that sifac with her finger; and if not, pierce it with a sharp spatula, and be cautious not to injure the surrounding tissue.
Then place upon it wool steeped in wine and oil. Then treat it with ointments until it heals.
If you fear that it will become obstructed, then place a lead cannula in the opening for many days, and remove it when the child wishes to defecate.
And obstruction of the anus also happens from the consolidation of a wound, or an abscess. Therefore, it is necessary to cut that consolidation, then treat it with what we said concerning treatment and lead.
Chapter LXXX.
Concerning the treatment of Fistulas that occur in the lower part.
Fistulas that occur in the lower part are Nodules and swellings that occur near the anus externally, or in the space of one part: and it is one fistula, or several.
When that nodule ages, it opens, and watery white moisture, or thin pus, runs from it.
And sometimes these fistulas are penetrating. Therefore, penetrating ones are recognized by that which exits with excretion and windiness, when the sick person uses excretion; and perhaps worms exit from them.
And some of them are fistulas when they are in the space penetrating up to the bladder and up to the urethra. And some of them are penetrating up to the hip joint and up to the tailbone.
By this, a penetrating fistula up to the anus is known, even from a non-penetrating one, that you insert your index finger into the anus, and insert a thin probe into the fistula from air or iron, when there is no twisting.
If there is twisting in it, then insert into it a thin lead probe, or a hair from a horse's mane, until you feel the probe or hair on your finger.
If you do not feel it completely, nor does anything emerge from the opening with excretion, nor wind, nor worms as I said, then know that it is non-penetrating: therefore hasten to the cure.
And when the fistula is penetrating up to the bladder, or to the urinary meatus, then its indication is the excretion of urine from it, and the prohibition of it from this so that the place may be consolidated with medicines.
If however it is penetrating up to the hip joint, or to the hip bone, then its sign is that the probe reaches there, if there is no twisting in it up to the bone, and the finding of pain around the part, and that pus emerges from it always, and that treatment in it does not work, nor does it consolidate with ointment.
And in all these penetrating fistulas there is no cure at all: and their treatment is labor and vanity for him, from foolish physicians, upon whom it is formed.
However, in that which is not penetrating and not ancient, healing is hoped for with a saw, according to what I tell you, and I have already proved: and it is that the sick person should lie between your hands on their back, and raise their legs above their hips, and their hips should be inclined towards their belly. Then introduce a lead or bronze probe, if there is twisting in the fistula, until you know where the probe reaches.
If, however, the sick person feels it around the anus, then you should insert your index finger into the anus.
If, therefore, you feel the probe in your finger having already penetrated itself detected, besides what you feel between your finger and the fistula, or flesh: then you know for certain that it is penetrating: therefore, do not work on it, because there is no cure for it, as we have said.
They have said, however, that in some people the flesh is healed, and that through medication of which benefit is hoped for, it is to apply a fine cautery according to the width of the fistula, as previously mentioned, and to insert it heated into the fistula until it reaches around the anus. Then repeat this twice or thrice, until you know that all those added, filtered, flesh-like tissues that resemble the quill of a bird's wing are already burned.
Then treat it with lint wrapped in butter, until those burned tissues come out; then treat it with ointments that generate flesh and consolidate it.
If, therefore, it is healed, it is good.
And if not, then it will never be healed with another treatment.
And if the protrusion emerges, and does not reach your finger in the anus, and there is a thick layer of flesh between it and the protrusion, or similarly: and you see a fistula where the skin surface follows, then cut the skin from the beginning of the fistula, and you proceed cutting with the protrusion which is in the fistula, until the cut reaches where the end of the protrusion reaches, and the protrusion is released and falls out.
Then cleanse those filtered tissues, which are similar to the quill of a bird's feather, so that nothing remains of them.
However, if blood overcomes you, and separates you from your operation, so that you cut those tissues: then better, and of quicker aid, cauterize it with fire, or with a sharp medicine.
Because cauterization with fire aggregates good qualities, the burning of those added tissues, and the cutting off of blood, and the drying of the moistures.
Then treat it with cloths wrapped in butter, or crushed herbs, until the place discharges pus, and those burned tissues exit in the pus.
Then treat the wound with consolidating ointments, which produce hard flesh: and these are medicines that have astringency and dryness.
For when it is restored, then it is already healed: and you do not fear repetition.
If however the fistula has already reached the depth of the anus, and is elongated by bodily superfluity: then introduce your finger into the anus, and investigate with it.
If therefore you feel a stricture, and between you and it is a membrane of sifac or flesh, and it is close to penetration: then there is no operation on it, except according to the path of confidence and hope.
For this is because one of three methods is administered to it: either to cauterize it, as we have said; or to dissect it, until you reach its concavity, so that it is possible for you to introduce a ligature, and cure from the vicinity. And do not proceed much with the incision, so that you do not cut the ligament containing the anus, and inflict on the weak a consequence of evacuation without will. Then treat it with what we have said.
Perhaps indeed it will be cured; or you will lengthen the membrane, and cleanse it after you have cut it close to the anus, as we said, either with forceps or with another instrument of sharp tip. Then cleanse those filtered flesh that are in the incision, until where you can with scrutiny.
Then attempt the restoration of the entire wound with the incision, and the upper opening where it consolidates; and a fistula will remain open in the interior part of the anus, and it will be easier on the weak.
And sometimes the fistula is sutured in this way: and it is, when you introduce a probe into the fistula; and it is on the side of the anus, near the surface of the body with the skin and margin of the anus; then take a probe with a perforated tip, which is like an alef needle, that is, for preparing hides, in this form. And introduce into it a thread wrapped in five threads, and around it. Then introduce the probe with the thread into the fistula, until it reaches its concavity.
If therefore it penetrates into the inner margin of the anus nearby, then pull the thread out from that opening; so that you introduce your finger into the anus, and pull out the end of the thread, and bring the two ends together; and tie each one, and leave it for one or two days; and the more the thread penetrates the flesh, tie it tightly until those tissues between the two thread ends are cut and fall away; then treat the wound until it consolidates and heals.
If however the opening of the fistula is not penetrating, then make it penetrate in whatever way is prepared for you, unless it is very deep. For you do not have the ability to do this on account of the muscle, so as not to injure it.
Then do with it as we have said, until it is healed.
Shape a thorn-like spatula, with a very sharp curve, and the other part is not sharp, so that it does not injure what is unnecessary, as you see.
Chapter Eighty-one.
Concerning the suturing of hemorrhoids, from which blood flows from their incision, and the treatment of fissures.
Hemorrhoids are according to two methods. For they are either within the anus, similar to sacks of coals or prunes, or as if they were bunches of grapes themselves. And from them are small, and large ones: and blood flows from them always, and there is one, and there are many. And they are outside the anus, and at its extremities.
However, those which are outside the anus, are for the most part few in moisture, from which flows yellow water, or little blood with constant running: and they are according to the colour of the body.
And the treatment of that which is within the anus is that you should order the sick person to strain: and it should be pressed out, until it emerges from the anus, and the atheril appear to you. Therefore also suspend them with hooks, or hold them with your fingernail. Then cut them at their roots.
If however the hooks do not hold on them, because of their moisture and their softening: then take them with a rough cloth, or with cotton made from double-layered wool, and pull them out with your fingers. Then cut them, and after the incision sprinkle some warm powders over them, so that they may stand in the place of a cautery. Or cauterize them according to what preceded in the chapter on cauterization. Then treat them with the remaining treatment, until they are healed.
If the anus does not yield to evacuation, then inject the infirm with an enema containing a small amount of mordicatio, so that it washes out what is in the anus, and it yields to evacuation quickly when the infirm expresses himself.
If, however, the piles are external from the anus, then their removal is easy: and it is as if you take them with your fingernail, or take them with a hook, and cut them. Then treat them with what we have said, until they are healed.
And whoever abhors incision with iron, then it is necessary that you administer their suture according to this method. Take a wrapped thread, and insert it into the needle. Then draw the piles upwards, and cause it to penetrate with the needle into its root on the other side: and wrap the two ends of the thread, in the lower part of the needle, and let them be latitudinally: and tighten the piles with a strong tightening. Then tie the thread, and remove the needle.
Do that with all the piles, and leave one of them: and do not sew it, so that blood will run from it.
Then place over the anus a cloth soaked in rose oil, or cerote, and place it over the anus: and tell the sick person to rest. Then leave it until it falls.
When therefore the piles fall, treat them with ointments and other treatment until they are healed.
Concerning the treatment of Fissures. Indeed, they often happen from the drying out of feces and from the constriction of nature.
When therefore they become hardened, and medicine is of no avail for them, then it is necessary to scrape them with a sharp-pointed spatula, or with your fingernail, until they become moist, and the top layer which prevents their consolidation is removed. Then treat them until they consolidate as is appropriate.
However, if they do not consolidate, then scrape them again with a more forceful instrument than the first, until they bleed profusely and are abraded; then treat them until they heal.
Concerning the treatment of inverted and non-inverted Almirmar, and of dry Athelil and Formicae.
Chapter Eighty-two.
Know that Almirmar is nothing other than a rounded swelling the color of the body, resembling the head of a nail; and it forms throughout the body, especially in the lower parts of the feet and toes; and pain arises from it during walking.
Therefore, it is necessary to cut that which is around the nail, and to hold it with shears, or with a hook, and to remove it from its root.
Then treat it; and if you wish, cauterize it according to what preceded in its chapter.
From dry athelil, one is a small, rough, filtered, round eminence above the surface of the body. And they often occur in the hands of children.
And its treatment is to stretch the athelil, and cut it; or to bind it with a silk thread; or with a needle, until it falls. And if you wish, cauterize it according to what has been said with fire, or with a sharp medicine; and now inverted athelil are eradicated, and not inverted with the instrument which I shall name.
A formica is also a small, filtered, thick eminence on the surface of the body, going deep: and when cold finds it, a sensation like the bite of an ant is felt in it, and it occurs throughout the body; and most of it occurs in the hands.
And its treatment is to take a cannula from a goose's feather, or a strong falcon's feather, and place that cannula over the Formica, until the circle of the cannula encompasses its parts. Then turn your hand with the cannula, until you cut what is around the Formica; and you place it deep into the flesh, and eradicate the Formica from its root.
And if you wish, do it with a cannula of air or iron, according to this form.
Let the top of the cannula be drawn up to the height of the deaf wrapping, so that the grasping and turning of it and itself may be relieved over the finger.
And if you wish, cauterize the Formica according to what is mentioned in the cauterization of Athelil in the chapter on the cautery.
Chapter LXXXIII.
Of the forms of instruments that are administered to enemas in diseases of the anus, and bowel evacuation, and colic.
Sometimes the clyster is made of silver, or of bronze, or of cast or hammered copper.
And sometimes it is made of these instruments small, and large, according to those in which they are administered.
Therefore, that which is administered in the treatment of young children should be small; and those whose anuses are weak or painful, likewise their enemas.
Large clyster.
Its tube, that is, the upper end where the bladder is tied, should be wide according to this method; and there should be a distinction so that the bladder is tied as you see, and its lower end that is inserted into the anus should be blunt, that is, not open, smooth, very much, drawing to slenderness; and on one side of it there should be two holes, and on the other side one hole.
And the size of the hole should be according to the thickness of the rays, and slightly thicker; and the bladder in which the medicines are placed should be from the bladder of an animal, or from the paper of an egg, which should be according to the form of a small asofrat; whose root is one palm.
And it should be pierced with many holes as it is rolled, and the space between each hole should be the thickness of a finger. Then insert into those holes a strong thread made from ten threads, or about that number, and bind the bladder with it like an asofrat. When, therefore, the bladder is placed in it, it should be tightened at the head of the clyster between two distinguishing constrictions with a strong tightening. Then administer the clyster with it.
And this is the form of the bladders here depicted, and the holes.
However, the quality of administering the clyster with it is: that you fill the bladder, or the vesicle, with medicine, which should be heated according to the tolerance of the patient: and be careful that it is not too hot, or too cold.
Then the patient should lie on their back, and raise their legs upwards: and place under them an inthaan, that is, a hide upon which people lie, or a linen cloth.
Then anoint the tip of the clyster with oil, or with egg white, or with the mucilage of fenugreek, or similar things. Then introduce it into the anus with ease. Then let the attendant grasp the bladder with both hands together, and express it with force, until it evacuates all that is in the clyster into the intestine.
Then remove the clyster, and let the patient raise their legs to the wall.
If the medicine causes him to exit, then let him hold as long as possible. And if he sleeps with it all night, it is more beneficial.
If, however, you clyster him in diseases of looseness of the belly, and his exit is hurried, then clyster again, until he is cured.
If there are hemorrhoids or harmful abscesses in the anus, then let the clyster be very thin, mild, from the clysters with which children are clystered.
And this be the form of a thin clyster above which there is tight skin.
Chapter LXXXIV.
I have already spoken in the division about the treatment of wounds, which was suitable for the book, and I shall now speak here about their treatment with the hand and with medicaments, according to the completion.
Therefore, I say that wounds are diversified according to the manner in which they are inflicted, and according to the place upon which the wound falls.
Therefore, the things by which wounds are often inflicted are like the throwing of a stone; or the incision of a sword or knife; or a blow with a lance, or wood, or an arrow; and by similar things from many objects.
And the wounds that are inflicted according to the parts of the body are, such as a wound that falls upon the head, or neck, or chest, or belly, or liver; and similar to those from the members.
And I am narrating the treatments of certain wounds, so that I may set forth that reasoning and rule for the remaining wounds.
And I will begin with simple wounds of the head, because the recollection of compound wounds will come at the beginning of the third chapter.
I say therefore. When a simple wound occurs on the head, and there is no fracture of the bone: consider then, if it is from the impact of a stone, or similar things, and has only broken the skin: and is a large wound, and you fear a bad outcome of a hot abscess: then hurry, and perform a cephalic phlebotomy immediately; and do not delay it; and extract from him blood according to the amount of his strength; if there is nothing preventing phlebotomy, especially if the blood that emerges from the wound is scant. And let the phlebotomy be on the opposite side of the wounded part.
And let the phlebotomy be on the opposite side of the wounded part. And let the patient beware of overeating, and wine, and heavy foods.
If, however, something prevents reduction, then reduce his food, and let him not approach meats, nor wines.
And if a hot abscess happens to him, place on the wound a poultice infused in rose oil alone, or with wine in which there is astringency.
If, however, you are certain it is not a hot abscess, then if the wound is fresh with its own blood, and the air has not changed it, place on it the powder I will describe shortly; and if the air has already changed it, then place some ointments on it until it heals.
If, however, the wound is large, or is from a sword cut, and similar things: and its edges do not come together through pads, then bring them together with sutures, according to what I will describe in wounds of the belly concerning their sutures.
If, however, the wound has already been detached from the bone, and hangs, and there is no retention of it, except in a small suspension: then cut it. Then treat the wound with medicines, and restore hard flesh in place of skin, and instruct the sick person to eat foods in which there is thickness, such as heads of grain and cooked wheat, until it is like a pulp; and things similar to these.
If you find putrefaction in the skin, and it does not adhere to the bone, then cut away all the putrefied part and throw it away; then treat it.
If, however, in these wounds there is an artery or vein from which blood flows: and it is not severed by medicines, then investigate the artery. If therefore you find it not severed, then sever it with a Spatumili, or tie it. If necessity compels when that which we said does not help, then cauterize it until the blood is severed.
If, however, these wounds are small, simple, then their treatment is easy, slight: so that you powder them with this powder, because it consolidates them before the air changes them.
Method of the flesh-healing powder. Take one part of frankincense, and two parts of dragon's blood, and three parts of quicklime; let the whole be finely ground, and sifted; and fill the wound with it, and bind it tightly, until it adheres well to it, and coagulates with the blood.
Then bind it further with bandages, and leave it, and do not loosen it while it remains, so that it does not suppurate. For the medicine is not removed from it until it is healed.
And sometimes quicklime alone does it, when you do not have frankincense and dragon's blood available; also, dragon's blood performs the same operation on many large wounds, when it is suitable to be applied to them and bandaged, and they are fresh wounds with their own blood, which the air has not altered.
If, however, the air has already altered it with some change, especially when it is in the summer season, then the intention of applying this powder is not suitable; therefore, treat it with what we have said, so that you apply some digestive ointments to it; or apply poultices of barley flour, made with water and honey, until they emit pus. Then treat the rest with the remaining treatment, until they are healed.
If, however, a fracture in the bone occurs with the wound, and it is small, then remove it with traction: and I will now speak of the treatment of fractures of the head in what follows.
Concerning wounds of the neck.
If, however, a wound occurs in the neck, then there is no difference between it in medication, and a wound of the head when it is simple.
If, however, the nerve of the neck, or artery, has already been cut: then if it is a nerve, there is no cure for it except to avoid treatment that causes excessive astringency, like ziniar, and zegi, and similar things. Because they harm the nerves, and contract them.
And do not put anything cold on the wound at all: because the substance of the nerves is cold, and its continuation is with the brain, which is higher than the limbs; and let that which is used for treatment from medicines be gentle, such as lime washed with sweet water infused with oil several times, or with rose oil, and tutty, when it is also washed with sweet water; and the other mineral stones, in this way, and moist ointments.
If, however, the wound is large, then administer sutures; or contract its lips with pledgets. Then treat it until it is healed.
If however the wound has depth, or a sinus has occurred in its lower part, in which pus has already collected, then pierce it in the lowermost location in it.
If, however, an artery has already been cut in the wound, and its blood flow has ensued: then separate, or cut it, and tie it; or cauterize it, if necessity prompts that.
If, however, the throat's cartilages, or all of it, have been cut, and the surrounding structures have been saved: then bring together the skin's edges with sutures over the throat's cartilage, and do not touch the throat itself, rather make it even and restore it to its natural shape. Then bind it with a proper bandage, and leave it for a few days. Then treat it with ointments that possess astringency and drying properties, until it is healed.
If, however, the wound has already made an impression on the bone, then explore it; then, if there are bone fragments within it, extract all of them, or as many as you can; and leave what cannot be removed until the wound becomes putrid, and its discharge separates and is relieved.
If the wound is recent, then powder over it the powder I described. If, however, bone is seen in the wound and a fistula forms, then treat it with what is mentioned in its corresponding chapter.
Concerning a wound in the shoulder blades.
If there is a blow from a lance or knife, and you see its depth, then consider it. If, therefore, wind issues from the wound, when the sick person breathes heavily from it, then know that the wound is mortal.
But if it does not have depth, and is fresh with its blood: then do not put powder on it in the first hour, nor bind it, so that the blood is not retained in its depth, and returns to the heart, and kills the sick person; but put an attracting ointment on it, and dissolve it twice a day.
If, however, the ointment is not present for you, then put an old poultice in the wound's opening, so that it sucks what emerges from it of the humors; and make the sick person sleep over the wound, so that what is collected in it runs out.
If, however, three days or more have already passed for the wound, and no spasm occurs in the sick person, nor bad tremor of the heart, nor constriction in breathing, and you see the rest of his conditions good, then know that the wound is safe. Therefore, treat him with bandages and the rest of the treatment until he is healed.
If, however, his healing is excused, and it always opens again, then know that it has now become a fistula. Therefore, treat it according to its chapter.
And if the wound is from a sword or knife incision, and is simple on the surface of the chest or back, then treat it with what has preceded regarding sutures, if it is large; or with powder, if it is small.
If, however, it has already made an impression on the bone and chipped fragments from it, then investigate the wound, and hasten to extract those fragments if they are detached. If however they are not detached, then leave them until the wound becomes putrid, for the extraction of them will be made easier.
For other accidental wounds in other limbs, the judgment in treatment is the judgment that we have stated.
Concerning wounds of the belly, and the exit of the intestines, and their extrusion.
Rupture which happens to the belly is great, middle, and small.
From great rupture, therefore, sometimes the intestine, or a number of intestines, exits.
Therefore, their introduction, and their suturing is more violent and more difficult.
And small rupture also becomes difficult in another way. Therefore it follows, that their reduction is more difficult, if you do not hasten in their introduction to their place immediately; because it is inflated, opened, and thickened; therefore, its entrance becomes difficult.
Therefore, a better rupture is the middle rupture; because with it there is not a difficult reduction of the intestine, as it is difficult in these two types.
Know that the suturing of the belly is according to four ways.
Two common sutures agree with the suturing of the belly, and in the suturing of the remaining wounds of the body; and two other sutures properly agree with the remaining wounds. However, they are more proper with the suturing of the belly; and I tell you each one with an exposition and declaration.
Therefore, I say that when the wound is small, and something from the intestine protrudes from it, and it is difficult to reduce it, then that is because of one of two ways. Either because of the smallness of the disruption, as we said: or because there is inflation in the intestine due to the cold air.
When therefore it is so, then it is necessary to warm it, so that you immerse a sponge, or a damp cloth, in lukewarm water alone. Or boil it in water with squill, and cyperus, and spike, and apply it to the intestine with it until the inflation is resolved.
Sometimes wine that has astringency does it, and it is better than water alone in resolving the inflation.
When therefore you see the inflation already resolved, then moisten the intestine with water in which mallow or mallow has been boiled. For by that its entrance is alleviated with a small thing.
If however its return is excused after this treatment, then make a small incision in the disruption with the instrument with which hemorrhoids are cut.
And the first treatment is better when prepared than the incision: and no compulsion is made to cut after necessity and the lack of the instrument.
Let one part of it be twisted and sharp, and the other part not sharp, and the fine end not be as fine as a Spatumilis, but rather a little more blunt: and that is what is similar to a caiada, as you see.
When therefore the wound is enlarged, and the intestine enters, then it is necessary that its reduction be according to its natural form to its proper place, if possible, above it; because that is better.
When, however, the rupture is extensive in the lower part of the belly, then it is necessary that you make the sick person lie on their back, and raise their legs higher than their head. And if it is in the upper part of the belly, then place his head and chest higher than his lower parts.
And similarly, if the rupture is in one of the two parts of the belly, then place your intention and your consideration always in this, that the part in which the wound is be higher than the other part.
And so it is necessary to administer that in large wounds, and in medium wounds.
In small wounds, however, place it according to what is prepared for you.
Then have a pious attendant present, who holds the entire rupture with his hand: and bring together its lips. Then uncover from it for him who follows the suture something after something: and this is the common mode of a single suture.
And it is that you take a needle, or a number of needles according to the quantity of the wound's breadth. Then leave from the extremity of the rupture a quantity of the ring finger, that is, of the physician: and fix one needle past that into it, passing the thread into both extremities of the skin, with both extremities of that which is under the skin from the interior, until you make it penetrate from the other side, and you have now brought together the two extremities of the skin, and the two extremities of that which is thus: and four rows are made.
Then tighten with the double thread in a circular motion of the needle several times from both sides simultaneously, until the two lips of the wound are brought together in a fitting aggregation.
Then leave a thickness of a finger also, and fix another needle in them; afterwards tie it with the thread, as you did with the first needle. Therefore do not cease to do that with what needles you need, until you complete the closure of the entire wound.
And the needles should be of intermediate thickness between thin and thick; because very thin needles cut flesh very quickly, and thick needles are difficult to enter the skin; therefore they must be intermediate in thinness and thickness.
And it is not appropriate for the needle to be fixed in the two closely adjacent skin edges too much, so that the flesh is not cut quickly and the wound is opened before it is consolidated, and they should not be pulled too tight with the suture, so that the wound is not prevented from consolidating.
Then trim the ends of the needles, so that they do not harm the patient during their sleep; and put pads of cloth on all sides, which will hold the ends of the needles; and leave them until you know that the wound is now consolidated.
And this type of suture with needles is more suitable in small wounds. Because one or two, or approximately that number, of needles is sufficient for their suture.
However, the second common suture is to bring together the four edges: two skin edges and two edges of a similar nature, with a needle in which an equal thread, fine and thick, is wound.
Then, when you have pierced these four edges with the needle, pull the needle back from the side from which you began, so that the wound thread falls from the upper part of the wound; so that the suture will be like the stitching of cloth used to bind cloths, and place between each stitch a quantity equal to the thickness of a smaller finger.
However, men know this suture.
And with this suture I myself healed a wound which had occurred to a man in his belly, who had been wounded with a knife, and the wound was torn open more than a palm; and about two palms of the middle intestine had emerged from the intestines, and the rupture was in the middle of the belly.
Therefore I returned it after his intestine had remained outside the wound for twenty-four hours.
Therefore the wound was consolidated in a space of approximately fifteen days; and I treated him, and he was healed; and he lived many years after that, engaged in his affairs.
And the physicians had judged that it would not heal.
And it was by a miracle that I did not treat it with ointment. Because I was in a place where no ointment, nor any medicine, was to be found. Therefore, I placed old linen over the wound twice a day, and diligently washed it with honey water, until it was healed.
I will now state two specific types of suture, according to the account of the text.
And it is that you begin with a sharp suture: and you insert the needle from the outer to the inner part.
When therefore the needle penetrates the skin, and going into the muscle along the length and breadth of the entire body, leave the end thus in the side where you inserted the needle, and make it penetrate in the other end from the inner part to the outer in the other extremity mirach.
When therefore you have made it penetrate, then make it penetrate according to this in the same extremity mirach from the outer part to the inner: and leave the extremity sifac, which is on this side, and make the needle penetrate the other extremity from the inner part to the outer: and make it penetrate with your penetration, as you make it penetrate in the sifac extremity mirach, which is on its side, until it penetrates the whole of it.
Then begin also from this same side, and sew it with the extremity, which is from sifac on the other side, and withdraw the needle from the skin which it has pierced.
Then return it to that skin, and sew the sifac extremity, which is on the other side, with this mirach extremity: and withdraw it from the skin which is on its side, and do it once, and again: and do it time after time, until you sew the whole exterior according to that example.
And the sum of this suture is that it sew the skin of the leather workers, as they sew skins: so that you sew sifac with one turn from one extremity of the skin: and that you let it go with another turn from the other side, until you complete it.
The second species of proper suture, which Galen said.
His speech relates that you should sew it according to the manner in which some physicians sew them, so that they bring every part to its naturally fitting counterpart; and they apply the sifac extremity to its other extremity, and the mirach extremity to its other extremity. And that is done according to what I tell you.
You should place the needle into the mirach extremity closest to you on the outside, and have it pierce to the inside in that part only; and you should let go of the two sifac extremities. Then turn the needle, and make it pierce from the outside to the inside in the two sifac extremities, namely on both sides.
Then turn both of them, also making them pierce from the inside to the outside in the two other mirach extremities, which are on the opposite side.
And this method of suture is better than the common easy suture, and is a suture whose recollection precedes.
This is therefore also Galen's discourse.
And some said. The suture of the belly is rectified with two needles. That is, insert one thread in each of them: and begin with the insertion of the needle, from the one that is with you, and make it penetrate to the other side, according to the suture of those who sew leather equally.
And know that when there is a rupture in the middle of the belly, then its suture is more difficult than the other parts of the belly.
However, the judgment of the treatment of a wound is the judgment of the other wounds.
That is, when you acquire a fresh wound with its own blood, before air alters it, and you reduce the intestine, and suture it, and fit it, then place it over the flesh-inducing powder.
If air has already altered the wound, then place upon it some ointments that are present to you, until they produce pus: and the threads fall, and it consolidates, and mirach. Then treat it as is the treatment of other wounds, until it is healed.
However, if the medicines are not present to you, then place upon it immediately, from when it begins to emit pus, old cloth: and change it twice a day, as I taught you, until it is healed.
For you do not need another medicine in many cases, when the wounds are simple.
If, however, you fear that the wound communicates with the principal limbs in the injury, then it is necessary that you immerse soft wool in oil of temperate warmth, or in rose oil: and place it around the places which are between the groin and under the armpits.
If, however, he feels pain, or prickings, or putrefaction in his intestines (for this often happens) then enema him with astringent, black, warm wine: especially if putridity has already reached the intestine, and has become a penetrating exit right to its cavity.
And know that that which is from the large intestine is of easier healing: and that which is from the small, of more difficult healing.
However, the intestine called the Jejunum does not receive healing from a wound that occurs in it at all: and this is because of its multitude of veins, and their size, and because of the subtlety of its body, and the proximity of its nature to the nerves.
If, however, that which proceeds from the wound is zirbum, and you obtain it fresh, reduce it according to your reduction, by which you reduce the intestines equally.
If, however, a space has passed it, and it has already become green, or black, then it is necessary to tie it with a thread above the place which has blackened from it, so that a flow of blood does not occur. For in zirbum are veins and arteries.
Then cut that which is beyond that ligature, and place the two ends of the thread hanging from the lower part of the wound outside it: so that the pulling of it may be alleviated upon you, and the extraction of it at the occurrence of zirbum, and the emission of the wound's pus.
Memorization of the wound that occurs in the Intestine.
When a rupture occurs in the Intestine, and it is small, then it is possible that it may be restored in some people; for this reason, because I saw one who was wounded in his belly with a spear thrust, and the wound was on the right side of the stomach, and the wound was old; and it became a fistula, and excrement and wind issued from it.
Therefore, I applied a treatment, until I was not confident in its healing; nor did I cease to refine it, until he was healed, and the place was consolidated.
After I saw the place already consolidated, I feared for the sick man, that an evil accident might befall him in his belly; and no such evil accident befell him from that. And he remained in better dispositions, eating and drinking, and copulating, and entered a bath, and exercised in his daily activities.
And now certain experimenters have said that when a wound occurs in the Intestine, and it is small, then it is necessary that it be ligated in this way. And it is, that you should take ants of large heads. Then bring together the two lips of the wound, and place one ant of those which have an open mouth upon the two lips of the wound. When therefore it has settled upon them, and tightens its mouth, cut off its head, for it adheres and does not loosen.
Then place another ant near the first, and do not cease to do this with ant after ant according to the size of the entire wound.
Then reduce it, and suture the wound. For those heads remain attached to the intestine until the intestine is nourished and healed, and no harm comes to the sick person at all.
And now it is possible that the intestine be tied even with a fine thread: which is cleaned from the intestine of an animal, attached to it after it is inserted into the needle.
