And it is, that you take the extremity of this thread from the intestine, and it is much scraped. Then tie a fine linen thread wrapped around its extremity. Then insert that thread into the needle, and sew the intestine with it. Then reduce it into the disruption.
And this method of sewing with ants and intestine is not except according to the way of trust and hope.
Know that a wound that is large, wide, especially if it is in one of the small intestines: then there is no remedy except subtlety, nor is its healing at all.
Concerning the treatment of ulcers and fistulas.
Know that every wound, or abscess, when it grows old and becomes an ulcer, and does not heal, and continually discharges pus, and is not cut away: then it is universally called, wherever in the body it may be, a fistula, and we call it Zuchemen.
And a fistula, in truth, is a filtered, hard, white knot; with which there is no pain, having the concavity of a bird's feather: and because of this some call it Risati, that is, a feather.
And in some hours it is moist, which emits pus. And perhaps the moisture is cut open in some hours. And sometimes this moisture is much, and sometimes little; and sometimes thick, and sometimes thin.
And I have already mentioned in the division, when I said wounds, that every ulcer which does not heal, nor flesh grows on it, is not that but for one of nine causes.
Of which one is, either because of the scarcity of blood in the body, or because of its malice, or because between it and on its lips there is hard flesh, preventing the birth of flesh, or because it is full of filth and dirt, or because the ulcer is itself putrid, and the material applied to it is of poor quality, or because the medicine is unsuitable for its treatment, or because corruption falling in the region is a form of pestilence, or because of a property in the region, as happens in Caesaraugusta, in which there is difficult digestion of illnesses, and the healing of abscesses by nature is delayed, or because there is one bone, or a number of bones, in it.
And I have already shown all these causes and their signs, and their cures with medicines in the division: and it is necessary here to make you know the cure of Fistula and Zuken, which is done by the way of operation with the hand.
Therefore know that Fistula occurs in all members of the body. For of them are Fistulas that reach large veins and arteries, or nerves, or sinews, or the intestines, or the bladder, or to any of the ribs, or to any of the vertebrae of the back, or to any of the joints of the limbs, such as hands and feet; and of them are those that go to the proximity of the principal limb, and similar ones.
Therefore, whichever of these fistulas is of this type, is from illnesses difficult to cure, and does not receive treatment, and dealing with it is laborious and ignorant.
If there is any fistula whose depth does not reach these places I have mentioned, and you hope and trust in its cure, and wish to arrive at its understanding, then take a probe of air or iron, if the fistula tends in a straight line, and explore it with it.
If however there is a winding in the fistula, then investigate it with a slender lead probe. Because lead, with the lightness of its body, is easily moved and bent around the winding.
If the fistula has many openings, it is not possible for you to mark it with a probe. Therefore, irrigate it from one of its openings.
For the moisture you irrigate it with, goes towards the other openings, and runs from them.
Then diligently investigate how you can determine whether there is a bone or nerve, and whether the fistula's hollow is deep or shallow, or whether it is a single fistula with many openings.  Stand over the entire area with all your strength, questioning the ill person, and by feeling the pain when you press with your hand on the area and by similar signs and causes of accidents and abscesses.
When, therefore, you have stood over all that area correctly, then proceed to the treatment with confidence.  And you should consider if the fistula is a superficial one, or located safely far from a joint, or a nerve, or an artery, or a vein, or one of the places I have told you about.  Then, cut the fistula according to what I have previously described, and remove what is inside it: pus, and corrupted flesh that grows from the fistula's opening or the surrounding tissue, and other similar things.  And treat it until it heals.
If, however, the fistula is deep and located properly, then you must cut it as deep as possible. Then cleanse it of all its corrupted flesh.
Then apply strips soaked in sharp medicines, and insert them into the hollow of the fistula that you have grasped with the iron. Do this several times until the sharp medicine corrodes all that remains in the fistula's hollow from the corruption.
Then restore it with ointments that produce healthy flesh, until it heals.
If it does not heal by that, then cauterize it according to what we have described.
If, therefore, the cause of the fistula is bone, and this is confirmed by you, then search for it and cut it according to what I have told you: if nothing from a vein, or nerve, or principal limb hinders you, as we said.
If, therefore, bone is found by you, and there is something of corruption and blackness in it, then scrape it until all its corruption is removed. Then treat it with that which causes it to flesh over, until it heals.
If it does not heal, and discharges pus as it did: then know that you have not reached the complete eradication of the corruption.
Therefore, expose it again and thoroughly scrape and cleanse it with all your strength.
Then restore it. If it therefore heals, it is good; and if not, then know that that corruption is in a deep distant part of the body, which you have not reached. Therefore, you have no skill in it, except to leave it to nature.
If the small bone is corrupted, and it is possible for you, draw it out with fine forceps suitable for it.
If, however, there are many bones, then carefully make an extraction of all of them, and do not leave any of them behind with your strength.
If, however, some moisture creates an obstacle for you, and there is no fear or error in incising them: then incise those moistures and flesh, if there is any, and free the bones.
If, however, you do not have the preparation to remove the bones from your time, then place over the wound something that will cause it to putrefy: and leave it, until that which is around those bones is putrefied from the flesh and moisture.
And guard the wound so that it does not heal. And bind the section that you have cut in your operation. Indeed, place over it, if you fear, a cloth soaked in crushed sulfur mixed with oil; or a cloth soaked in Egyptian ointment, or any other green ointments.
For when those humidities putrefy, and the bones separate from them, the removal and extraction of them is alleviated.
If however there is one large bone, such as a bone of the leg, or a hip bone, and similar to them, and that which is corrupted from it is a large part: then scrape it with the utmost scraping, until that blackness and corruption is removed. Then restore the wound.
If however that which is corrupted within it is a large part, and the corruption has already reached the bone marrow, then it is necessary to saw it, and to cut it completely up to the place where the corruption has reached: then treat it until it consolidates.
And I tell you a story, which happened to a man in his leg: so that you may place it as an example and help upon your operation.
There was a young man of about thirty years, to whom pain happened in his leg, from a cause that arose within his body, that is, from the insides, until the matter reached the leg, and it was swollen with a large abscess: and there was no external cause for it.
So, time passed with him and with the errors of the physicians, until the abscess was opened, and many matters ran together with him. And his treatment was delayed, until the leg became fistulous, and many openings were made in it: which all emitted pus and the humors of the body.
Therefore, a multitude of physicians treated him for about two years, and there was no wise one in the art of hands among them, until I was called, and saw his leg, and the matters that were running from those openings with great flow: also the whole body of the man was now putrid, and his color was yellowish.
I therefore inserted a probe into one of the openings, and the probe reached the bone.
Then I examined all the openings: and I found them to reach one another from all parts of the leg.
Therefore, I proceeded and cut over one of those openings until I exposed a part of the bone: and I found it corrupted: which was already corroded, and blackened, and putrid, and perforated, until it had penetrated to the marrow.
Therefore, I removed what I had uncovered, and prepared it from the corrupted bone.
And I judged that there was nothing in the bone but that corruption which I had cut away and removed. And I had already eradicated it.
Then I applied a restorative treatment to the wound with consolidating medicines for a long time. And it was not consolidated.
Then I tried again, and discovered a second layer of bone above the first: and I found continuous corruption along with the bone; and I removed what appeared to me to be corruption from that as well.
Then I attempted its restoration. And it was not restored.
Therefore I again probed it, and did not cease cutting the bone piece by piece, and attempting its restoration: and it was not restored until I cut away about a palm's breadth from the bone and removed it with its marrow.
Then I restored it with medicines, and it consolidated quickly and healed: and this repetition in its operation and incision was not necessary, except because of the weak patient's condition, and their small tolerance, and my fear for their death; for it happened to them at all hours due to the excess of evacuative syncopes.
Therefore he was healed completely: and firm flesh grew in the place of the bone, and his body's condition became good, and his strength returned, and he resumed his normal functions, and no impediment to walking was caused to him at all.
However, if a prominent bone in a fractured part of the body exists, then it is necessary to saw it, in this manner: and this is so that you take a bandage and tighten it at the extremity of the prominent bone, and instruct someone to extend it upwards, and place another bandage of thicker wool over the first bandage. Then tie it over the flesh which is beneath the bone: and extend its extremity so that you pull the flesh downwards: and you uncover the flesh over the place you wish to saw, so that the saw does not injure the flesh.
Place wood, or a plank, underneath the bone properly below: for when you do this, it does not hinder it from cutting the corrupted flesh.
And it is necessary that the sawing be over the corrupted place for a little while, so that there is no corruption in the concavity of the bone, and it does not appear in its appearance to the senses, and compels sawing in another place.
If the bone is corrupted, and is not prominent, but rather its part continues with another part, and the corruption is in its middle or in a part of it, then uncover the flesh from all sides entirely. Then place wood below.
Afterwards saw it from the first part, where the corruption is, until the sawing is continued from the other part; and let the sawing be along the length from the corruption a little, according to what we have said.
If the corruption is in the joint, cut the corrupted joint itself, and saw the bone where it continues from the other part.
If however the corruption is in the continuation of two joints, then there is no remedy in it but abrasion.
If the corruption is in the finger of the hand, or in the toe of the foot: then the matter is very difficult in it. But it is necessary that you inquire how the corruption appears to you. And abrade it, and cleanse it according to whatever arrangement is possible for you, and with whatever remedy it is rectified for you: when it does not cause you opposition from the vein, or nerve.
And know that there are many chisels and saws for cutting these bones, according to the position of the bones, and their preparation, and their thickness and thinness, and their size and smallness, and their hardness and their rarity. Therefore, it is necessary to prepare a suitable instrument of every kind of operation for that operation.
And know that these operations indicate to you the form of the instrument you need, when you have diligent study. And knowledge of the methods of this art is a testament to the methods of illnesses; for then you will already find for yourself that which is suitable for it from the instruments for every ailment.
And I am giving you, in the final part of this chapter, the number of instruments, which you will use as examples for what you will cut, and the proof by which you will test others.
Shape of another saw.
Shape of the razor's joints. Let the head of this razor be, according to the shape of a star-shaped nail's head; and its points be according to the shape of the points of a whetstone.
And it is not suitable, unless the heads of the joints are rubbed with it when they are damaged, or the bone is large and great.
Shape of the larger razor.
Shape of the broad razor.
Shape of the razor in which there is a concavity.
Shape of the other, slender razor.
Shape of the other razor with a curved end.
Shape of a small, slender, taut razor.
This is similar to a saw, and its end is like a saw; it is shown in many places by reason of cutting bones.
Shape of the razor suitable for the cutting of what is perforated in the bones.
Its end is triangular, with sharp ends.
It is made of India. And similarly, all that we have said must be done with razors, and knives, and saws.
Concerning the incision of extremities, and the sawing of bones.
Extremities putrefy either from an external cause or an internal one.
Therefore, if you have treated that corruption with medicinal cures, and the treatment does not help, and you see the corruption affecting a limb, which nothing is repelling: then you must cut that limb to the point where the corruption reaches, so that it escapes weakness by it from death, or from an evil that is greater than the limb's defect.
And a sign of this happening is that the limb darkens until it rots; that fire has burned it, or that it rots after turning black; until that rot is cut to the point following that limb, and spreads to the entire body.
Therefore, act quickly with its incision, and similarly if the cause of corruption is from the sting of certain venomous creatures, such as the sea scorpion, viper, and rattlesnake, and similar ones.
If, therefore, the corruption or injury is in the extremity of a finger, then cut off the finger, and do not give space to the corruption to proceed to the rest of the hand.
And similarly if it happens in the hand, then cut it off from the root of the forearm, and do not give space to the corruption until it takes hold of the forearm.
And if it invades it, then cut the arm at the elbow at its joint.
If, however, the corruption passes, and you see it about the elbow, then in that is the death of the sick person. And to administer beyond that from treatment, is a matter of virtuosity.
And similarly do with the foot, when corruption invades the toe, then cut it off at one of the joints; and if it takes hold of the foot's sole, then cut off the entire foot. If, however, it ascends to the knee, then cut the leg at the knee joint.
If, however, the corruption has already reached above the knee, then there is no skill in it, except for dismissal, and leaving the sick person to death.
And the manner of cutting off a limb, or sawing it, is that you should tighten the ligament below which you wish to cut; and tighten another ligament above the place. And let the servant extend one ligament downwards; and let the servant extend the other ligament upwards. And you should separate the flesh which is between the two ligaments, with a broad spatula, until all the flesh is exposed. Then it should be cut, or sawn.
And it is necessary that you place linen cloth on all sides, and that the saw does not touch healthy tissue; and that added injury and a hot abscess befall the sick person.
If, however, an internal bleeding occurs while you are working, then cauterize the place quickly, or place some powders that stop bleeding on it. Then return to your treatment, until it is completed.
Then bind the injured limb with a suitable bandage, and care for it until it is healed.
And I tell you what happened to a man in his foot, this same event that I tell you about.
That is, because a blackness occurred in his foot, with a burning like the combustion of fire: and that corruption first occurred in his toe, until it began to affect the whole foot.
Therefore, the man hurried himself, after he saw the corruption occurring in the limb with its intensity, which he found from its pain and burning: and it occurred to him at the joint, and he was healed.
And after a long time passed, that same corruption happened to him in the index finger of his hand.
Therefore, he came to me, and I tried to remove that excess with what I placed on his hand from medicines after cleansing his body: and the excess was not repelled, and it began to proceed to another finger: until the corruption began to affect his hand.
Therefore, he called me to cut his hand: and I did not want that, hoping for a repulsion of that excess from me: and I also feared death for him at the incision of his hand. Because the man's strength was over the case.
Therefore, after he despaired of me, he returned to his land: and it reached me about him, that he hastened, and cut his whole hand: and he was healed.
And I have not brought forth this narration, except that it might be of assistance concerning that which falls under this kind of illness: and that it might be a signification by which one might signify and act accordingly.
Of the treatment of Sinuses, and the nature of clysterization with medicines.
When an abscess occurs in certain fleshy members, and the space of the abscess is prolonged until it forms a collection of pus: then it bursts, or is pierced, and that which is within it of pus escapes, and a hollow place remains, as if it were a vessel: and the skin which is over it, like a cloth that has already become thin: and it has not reached the final stage of its corruption, nor does it affect the bone, nor the nerve, nor the ligament: it deserves to be called a Sinus: and it is not called a Fistula: however, if its space is prolonged until the corruption affects any of these parts, then it is called a Fistula, or zuken.
And its treatment is to cut away that entire skin as it is rolled back, and especially when it has become thin, and is like a cloth; and you are confident that it does not adhere to the place, because of its corruption.
However, if you suspect that the skin adheres, and that corruption does not follow from that final adhesion; and if there is a thickening of flesh in the skin, then treat it with a clyster; and it is necessary to consider it as a Sinus.
Then, if it is large, and the pus that is emitted from it has a foul odor, then clyster it with Egyptian medicine, which is vinegar, and oil, and ziniar, taking equal parts, and these are to be aggregated in a vessel; and they are to be boiled over fire, until the medicine turns red; and it comes to the consistency of honey.
Then take of that which you need; and dissolve it with water and honey, and clyster the sinus with it; and constrict its opening, and leave the medicine in it for a period of two hours.
Then extract it with pressure; do this for so many days, until the sinus is cleansed and the foul odor is removed.
And sometimes it is clysterized with ash water, when this medicine is not present for you; and it is necessary to take the ash of vine wood, or the ash from holly wood; and pour water over it. Then strain it, and clyster with it, until you are confident that the sinus is now cleansed.
If the patient does not tolerate Egyptian medicine, nor ash water, then clyster him with water and honey, to which has been mixed something of crushed ziniar. Or clyster him with honey and wine mixed, because of the property of honey is to cleanse and wash away; and wine causes the sinus to adhere, especially when there is an excess of astringency and dryness in the wine.
When you have done this several times, and are confident that the corruption of the sinus is now removed, then clyster him with that which causes flesh to grow in it.
And it is that you take from the dyafinicon plaster, and dissolve it with rose oil and astringent wine; or clyster him with certain medicines and other ointments, whose properties I have written in the treatise on ointments.
If the sinus opening is constricted, so that the clyster tube cannot enter, then widen it slightly with an iron implement; or place in it a linen cloth wrapped in Egyptian ointment, or in basil ointment, until it widens.
And similarly if the opening is also wide, then bring together its edges with a suture, and leave an amount in which the clyster can enter without addition.
And similarly if the opening from which pus runs is elevated upwards, then cut it in the lower place within it, so that pus runs from it downwards; because when pus is confined in the depth of the sinus, it prevents flesh from growing there.
If it is not possible for you to cut the sinus approximately below the part you wish, then try to prepare the limb in a way that the pus runs from it easily, as preparation allows you. Try to keep nothing of the pus within it, and take the consolidating medicines that are placed on the sign, suitable to what you want. And spread them over a linen cloth. And let the cloth be of such a size that it covers the entire sinus. Then cut with scissors, in a straight line to the opening of the sinus, a hole a little larger than the opening of the sinus. Then spread some mild ointments on another cloth, according to the size of the hole, and place it over the first, at a distance from the sinus opening, so that the pus may escape from it, and do not remove the large cloth, except after a few days at a time.
However, the one over the sinus opening is the one you remove every hour, so that what gathers in it from pus may escape, and so that you may know whether the matter running from it, the virus, is much or little, or digested or undigested.
And with this, again, consider the location of the sinus itself, whether its owner feels pain there, or there is no pain, and whether there is an abscess there, or there is no abscess there.
Therefore, when your treatment proceeds thus, and you see the matter escaping over the sinus opening, then know that the sinus is now adhering, or approaching adherence; then, therefore, loosen it in the morning, or on the third day; and when you loosen the wound, then change the cloth and renew the ointment.
However, if a thin virus emerges from the sinus after a long time, then do not despair of its adherence, for its healing is approaching, because there are men who delay the generation of flesh in their wounds, and there are those who act contrary to that.
If, however, undigested pus emerges from the sinus after many days, then know that the sinus does not adhere.
If, therefore, the adherence of the sinus is delayed, and its progress prolonged: then, in the drying of the medicines, let the physicians so arrange that the medicines are moist in their substance, and dry in their virtue; such as the ointment of diaphynicon, when superfluity of addition from zegi is added to it.
And the most ultimate of its cures is that you take ointment of diaphynicon and dissolve it with rose oil, and sprinkle over it old wine equal in its substance.
Then mix it with that, and use it.
And it does a similar action to this, when ointment of diaphynicon is not present to you, honey, when you boil it until it thickens, and use it. Or take myrrh, and aloes, and frankincense: grind the whole, and powder it over honey while it is over the fire. Then line a cloth over it, and bind it over the sinus: and line with the honey which you boiled until it thickened: and powder spices over it, and place over it the cloth, and bind it.
And sometimes long aristolochia, and the roots of the lily of the valley, and the lily, and herb flour, and centaury are administered in it. Use them simple, or aggregated, grind them, and sieve them: and powder them over the honey that I have described, and use it.
If the sinus persists and is not cured by this treatment, then know that there is corruption deep within it: or it is already making an impression on the bones, or on the other hard members, or ligaments: then cure it by the treatment of fistulas, as has been previously described.
On the treatment of whitlow and worn nail, and the incision of the added finger, and the section.
A whitlow is an abscess that arises under the nail of the thumb of the hand, or the foot: and perhaps it arises in the other fingers.
When, therefore, the condition persists, and its treatment is neglected, and the abscess becomes a hot abscess: or it is corrupted and emits pus, until it corrodes the root of the nail: and perhaps it corrupts the entire nail, and perhaps the corruption reaches the bone, until it has a foul odor: and the end of the finger becomes broad, and its color becomes dark.
Therefore, when you treat it, with what we have said in the division, and your treatment does not help: then it is necessary to cut with an iron all the excess which remains of the nail. Then cauterize the wound after that. For cautery is very helpful.
If, however, the bone is healthy, and the nail is also healthy, and the superfluous additions of the nail are now pressing the flesh inwards, and beginning to prick it and injure it: then it is necessary to place a fine needle under the angle of the nail which pricks the flesh, and drive it upwards: and cut the flesh easily, and put upon what remains of the flesh burning, corrosive medicines, until its entire removal. Then treat it with ointments, until it is healed.
If, however, corruption has already affected the bone, then it is necessary to cut that bone and extract it: for it will never be healed while the corrupted bone remains in it.
If you see corrosion and corruption spreading into the finger, then cut it at one of the joints, according to the previous mention of it. Then treat it, until it is healed.
If, however, the nail suffers attrition, or a blow, and a severe pain occurs in it, then it is necessary to first bleed the weak person. Then cut the nail with a sharp Spatumili in a transverse cut from above, for visibility.
And beware that the cut does not reach the flesh under the nail, for you will cause severe pain to the afflicted by it, and there will be a reason for an addition of flesh to grow in that place.
Then treat the place with that which calms the pains.
The extra finger that arises in some human hands is perhaps entirely fleshy; and perhaps some of them have bones, and perhaps a nail is on it; and some arise at the joint's root, or its birth is in some of the finger's knots. Therefore, the one that arises in some knots of the finger does not move; and the one that arises near the finger's joint perhaps moves.
Therefore, the one that is fleshy of them, its incision is easy. That is, to cut it at its root with a broad spatula.
However, the treatment of the one whose birth is at the root of the joint is difficult. Therefore, abstain from its incision.
In that one however, which is born on the finger at the last of the nodes, then it is necessary to cut first its flesh with a round incision up to the bone. Then saw the bone with one of those saws suitable to it. Then treat it until it is healed.
But consolidation which happens to fingers together often happens either from the birth of the man, that is, is born with it, or from the consolidation of a wound, or burning of fire, and similar things.
Therefore it is necessary to separate that consolidation until the fingers return to their natural form. Then place between them bandages or cloths soaked in rose oil, so that they do not consolidate quickly, and separate between them; or place between them a thin sheet of lead until the place is consolidated as it should be.
And similarly, if consolidation happens between certain fingers and the palm, then cut that consolidation, according to what is prepared and suitable for the shape of the limb.
Concerning the incision of a defect.
Ulcers are wrapped veins, large, full of melancholic superfluities that occur in many parts of the body; and their effect is most often in the legs, especially in the legs of those who travel and work, and carry burdens.
Therefore, it is necessary to administer a shaking of the body from black bile occasionally with a strong shaking.
Then phlebotomize the patient at the basilic vein.
The treatment of them with iron is according to two methods. One of them is that they should be cut and the black blood extracted. And the other method is that the vein should be raised, and the whole of it extracted.
The section of it is according to this method. First, bathe the leg in much hot water until the thick, foul blood is dissolved. Then bind the patient's leg, from the upper part of the thigh to the lower part of the knee, with a bandage. Then cut the vein in one, or two, or three places with a wide incision.
Then raise, that is, draw the blood in your hand from the lower part of the leg upwards, and from the upper part, the phlebotomist should first attend to the patient. Then cut the nail (or something) downwards: until an amount of blood sufficient and bearable by the patient's strength emerges.
Then tie it off; and tell him to abstain from foods producing black bile; and again perform evacuation and phlebotomy, when the veins are filled, and it harms the patient.
However, its elevation is according to this method. Shave the leg of the patient, if there are many hairs on it. Then immerse him in a bath; or apply a warm water embrocation to his leg until it turns red, and the veins protrude; or have him exercise vigorously, if a bath is not available, until the limb becomes warm.
Then cut the skin in the direction of the vein incision lengthwise, either at its end near the knee or in the lower part near the heel.
Then open the skin with hooks, and scrape the vein from every side, until it appears to the sense: and it is at its appearance, that you see it sloping from red to black. When it then emerges from the skin, you see it white, as if it were a cord.
Then introduce a probe beneath it, until when it is lifted and exits the skin: suspend it with a blunt, gentle hook.
Then make another incision near that incision by three fingers. Then scrape the skin from the upper part of the vein, until it appears. Then lift it with probes as you did, and suspend it with another hook, as you did at first.
Then make another incision, or several incisions, if you need them. Then lift it, and cut it at the very last incision, near the heel.
Then pull it out to the incision above it.
And do this until you pull it out of the incision which is higher, or above all the incisions, until the whole thing comes out: then cut it.
If however it does not yield to you in pulling and lifting it, then insert a needle with a strong doubled thread, and tie it, and pull it out; or insert a rod under it, and turn your hand in every direction until it comes out. And be careful not to cut it. For if it is cut, it becomes very difficult for you to lift it, and harm enters upon the weak from it.
When therefore you have pulled it all out, then put on the wound place wool soaked in wine and rose oil, or oil, and treat it until it heals.
If the vine is contracted, having twists for which there is a wrapping to the sides, and is not as we said in terms of straightness, then it is necessary that you cut along every twist of it and the places of its wrapping.
Then suspend it with hooks until you lift it all out: and be careful in your cutting on it not to cut a vein, or wound it; for it will become difficult for you to lift or remove it. Therefore, preserve your caution.
Form of the incident whereby the vine is healed.
Form of a blind hook.
It does not have the curvature like the other hooks, nor a sharp tip so as not to wound a vein: and it is of a thick, flexible form; for if it were slender, it would cut a vein with its slenderness; indeed, let its thickness be excessive, as we have said.
On the extraction of the vein of the leg.
This vein is generated in the legs in hot lands, like Al-Azeej, and the land of the Arabs, and in arid, hot lands, of little tenderness, that is, in which no grass or tree grows.
And perhaps it is generated in other parts of the body besides the legs.
And indeed its generation is from the putrefaction that occurs under the skin, as occurs within the bodies of snakes and worms (ascarides), and worms generated between the skin and the flesh.
And the sign of the beginning of the occurrence of this vein is that a violent inflammation occurs in the leg. Then the place becomes blistered. Then the vein begins to emerge from the place of that blistering, as if it were the root of a plant, or an animal.
Therefore, when its extremity appears from it, then you must wrap a piece of lead over it, whose weight is from one drachm to two drachms, and tie it; and leave the lead suspended from the leg.
Whenever therefore something emerges from it outward, wrap that in lead and tie it. If it extends very far, then cut a part of it, and wrap the remainder. And do not cut it at the root before the whole of it emerges. Because if you cut it, it contracts and enters the flesh, and causes an abscess and putrefaction in the place, and a bad ulcer.
Therefore it must be gently handled and drawn out gradually until the last part of it emerges, and nothing of it remains in the body: and from this vein indeed, in some men, something emerges which is in its length five palms and ten. And it has come to me that it emerged from a man of twenty palms.
If, however, you cut it during your hour of medication, then introduce a probe into its opening, and pierce it with a long, slender probe, until all matter within it is evacuated, and treat the putrefaction of the area with medicines for a few days. Then treat it with the treatment for abscesses.
And this vein sometimes has many branches, especially when it appears at the joint of the foot, or in the foot itself, and it has many openings, and a branch emerges from every opening.
Therefore, treat it as we have said in the division, and with what has been previously stated.
Concerning the incision of worms generated under the skin, and it is called the cattle disease.
This disease is called in a certain land among us the cattle disease, because it often happens to oxen, and is a small, single worm that is born between the skin and the flesh.
And it goes throughout the body, ascending and descending, and it is shown to the senses by its passage from limb to limb, until it breaks through wherever it breaks through the skin, and emerges.
And its generation is from the putrefaction of certain humors. As worms and snakes, that is, intestinal worms, and threadworms, occur in the belly.
And it is feared because of its malice. Because when it travels through the body and rises to the head and reaches a place, then perhaps it bursts there, and emerges, and destroys the eye; and this happens many times.
Therefore, when one intends to treat it and extract it, this is not done except by its movement and its appearance to the senses.
Therefore, it is necessary to bind that which is above it and that which is below it with a strong constricting bandage. Then cut above it and extract it.
If it has burrowed into the flesh and you do not find it, then apply a cautery with fire to the area until it dies.
And the greater fear is its corruption, by which it corrupts the eye, as we have said.
If, however, you see it already having reached the head near the eye, then bind below it the eyebrow with a good constriction. Then cut above it and extract it.
And it is necessary that the sick person use cleansing of his body, with medicines dissolving bad putrid humors.
And let him beware of foods generating putrefaction.
Concerning the incision above the illness Nakir.
This illness is called Nakir in our land: and it is a pain that occurs in certain limbs, then moves from limb to limb.
And I myself have already seen it, as I relate to you. I was called to a weak woman in certain villages. And she uncovered her arm, and I saw a small swelling in the vein of the arm's cord.
And after I remained one hour, I saw one swelling moving with a quiver, as a worm moves, ascending to her shoulder more quickly than possible, so that it is like quicksilver, when it runs from place to place.
Therefore, the pain receded from that place and fixed itself in the shoulder.
Then I sat for one hour, and it ran to the rest of the body, until it reached the other arm.
Then she told me that it was turning around in her whole body, as I had seen.
Therefore, I was amazed at the speed of its change from limb to limb. And I myself had not previously seen this illness with my own eyes in this way. However, I have seen many who find pain shifting from limb to limb: and I have not seen it with my eyes, as I have seen it in the woman.
And I do not think it is anything else, except that it is because the woman was from those who are from villages, with dry bodies and detected veins: therefore, this windy movement appears to the senses.
And it is necessary that they do not appear in this way in those who are delicate, and with moist bodies, of hidden veins.
When, therefore, you wish to cure it, and the patient feels the pain: then if it is apparent to you with the eye, as we have said, then tighten above it, and below it quickly: and cut above it, until that confined windiness emerges: and cauterize the place.
If, however, you do not see it with your eyes: then treat it with shaking the body, and with that which expels winds, and pacifies them with foul pills, and pills of serpent, and similar things.
Concerning the extraction of Arrows.
Arrows are diversified according to their types, and according to the places in which they strike the body.
Their diversity is such, for some have large points, and small ones, and concave, and blunt ones. And some have three angles. And some have tongues. And some have stars.
Those, however, which are according to the members on which they fall, are according to two ways. For either the members are from the principal concave members, such as the brain, and the heart, and the liver, and the lung, and the kidneys, and the intestines, and the bladder, and similar to them. Therefore, when it falls into any of these members, and signs of death appear to you, which I tell you afterward, then you ought to avoid the extraction of that arrow from them, for death follows him in whom it is, in many dispositions. And when these bad signs do not appear to you, and the arrow is not hidden deep within the member, then extract it, and treat the wound.
For signs of the brain, when an arrow falls into it and penetrates the bone and exits through the membrane that is over the brain, are: because violent sweating, and blindness, and dizziness, and redness in the eyes, and swelling, and redness of the tongue, and spasm, and confusion of the intellect, and vomiting of bile occur from it. And perhaps blood exits from the nostrils or ears. And perhaps speech is cut off, and the voice destroyed. And white moisture resembling gruel emerges from the wound site. And from it emerges something like the watery part of flesh.
If therefore these signs appear to you, then abstain from treatment, and remove the arrow if it does not emerge.
The signs of an arrow striking the heart are: that it is near the left breast; and that, when touching it, it feels as if it were fixed in a hard thing, not in an empty one. And perhaps the arrow's movement is similar to the pulse's movement, and black blood flows from the wound. And that is followed by coldness of the extremities, and cold sweat, and syncope. Therefore know that death undoubtedly descends.
And a sign of an arrow wounding the lung is the outflow of frothy blood from the wound; and the vessels that follow the neck suppurate, and the sick person's color changes. And he breathes out a great, deep breath; and seeks the attraction of cold air.
If an arrow strikes the covering, which is in the chest: then it is near the small ribs; and there is a great breath with intense pain, with a gasp; and all the shoulder members are moved.
And if the arrow strikes the stomach, then perhaps some indigested food will come out of the wound.
And if an arrow strikes the abdomen, and is fixed in it, some excrement, or zirbum, or intestine already ruptured, will issue from the wound. Therefore, there is no hope of curing it, nor of removing the arrow.
If the arrow strikes the bladder, and urine issues forth, and something proceeds from it to the exterior, and the pain is stronger than the weakness: then know that it is mortal.
However, the remaining limbs, such as the face, and neck, and throat, and shoulder blade, and ribs, and vertebrae of the back, and collarbone, and hip, and leg, and similar members are saved according to various conditions, when the arrow does not strike an artery, or a nerve, and the arrow is not poisoned.
And I tell you what I have seen of these arrows, so that you may understand about your treatment through it.
Because an arrow struck a man in the tear duct of his eye, at the root of his nose. Therefore, I extracted it from the other side, beneath the fat of his ear: and he was healed, and no harm came to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Jew, which had struck him in the fat of his eye beneath the lower eyelid: and the arrow was already hidden, and I did not obtain from it except a small end of it, with which it adheres to its shaft: and it was a large arrow from the bows of the Turks, with soft iron, in which there are no ears.
Therefore, the Jew was healed, and no misfortune happened to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from the throat of a Christian.
And it was an Arab arrow, and it is one which has ears. Therefore, I cut above it between the two guides: and it was already submerged in his throat. Therefore I proceeded cautiously with it, until I extracted it: and the Christian was healed.
And I extracted another arrow from the man which had fallen into his belly: and we thought that he would die from it.
Therefore, after thirty days had passed, and nothing had changed in his condition, I cut above the arrow: and I worked on it, and I extracted it: and he was healed, and no harm befell him.
And I saw a man in whom an arrow had fallen into his back, and the wound was healed over it. Therefore, after seven years, an arrow emerged at the root of his hip.
And I saw a woman into whose belly an arrow had fallen, and the wound was healed over it, and the arrow remained within her.  And no change occurred in her condition, and the woman found no harm in any of her natural functions.
And I saw another man, in whose face an arrow had fallen: and the wound was healed, and it remained, causing him little pain: and many similar things to these.
And I removed an arrow from the king's chief men, which had fallen into the middle of his nose, and had already inclined slightly to the right side: and the entire arrow was hidden.
Therefore, I was called to treat him after the arrow's impact for up to three days. Therefore, I found the arrow wound to be very tight.
I therefore searched for it with a fine probe, and did not feel it: and indeed, he felt a prick and pain beneath his right ear. Therefore, I hoped that the prick was from the arrow's tip.
Therefore, I plastered the area with a plaster possessing attractive and digestive properties, confident that the area would form an abscess and that I would see a sign of the arrow so that I could cut above it: and it did not happen in the area that there was an accident signifying that the arrow had struck the area.
Therefore, I applied the plaster to it for many days: and no accident happened, and the wound healed meanwhile; and he remained weak from its extraction for a space of days, until one day he felt the arrow within his nose. And he told me that.
And he told me that. Therefore, I applied a sharp, corrosive medicine to that wound for many days, until it opened; and when I probed it, I felt a thin arrowhead adhering to the wood.
Then I added to the opening of the wound with that sharp medicine, until the arrowhead appeared to us visibly; and I spent approximately four months with him during that time.
Then, after the wound had been enlarged, and I was able to insert forceps into it and move it, and it did not yield to removal, I did not cease to refine and devise methods upon it, until one day I began to use properly made forceps, according to the form they will take at the end of the chapter, until I extracted it. Then I restored the wound.
And indeed, physicians judge that the cartilage of the nose is not restored. Therefore I restored it, and the wound was consolidated, and the infirm man was healed with complete health; and nothing at all harmed him.
And I tell you the quality of the extraction of certain arrows: so that you may set it as a rule and signification concerning what I have not said.
Since the parts of this art and their distinctions are not encompassed by the narrative; nor does that book encompass it, and a skillful craftsman compares the little with the much: and with what is present, with what is absent; and finds a new operation and a new instrument in the descent of foreign things of this art when they occur.
I say, therefore, that arrows do not emerge from the members to which they are fixed, except in two ways. Or by traction from the place from which it entered. Or by the opposite of the other part.
And that which emerges from where it entered is either an arrow progressing in a fleshy place, and is drawn out and emerges.
If it does not yield to exit at the hour in which it falls, then it is necessary to leave it for some days, until the flesh that is around it putrefies, and its traction is eased, and its emergence.
And similarly, if it is fixed in bone, and does not yield to you for exit, then leave it also for some days, and repeatedly apply traction and motion every day until it emerges.
If it does not yield to you for exit after days, then it is necessary to perforate around the arrow in the bone itself from every side with a fine drill, until the arrow's path is widened. Then pull it and extract it.
If however the arrow is already hidden in a place of the body, and is concealed and hidden from sight, then seek it with probes. If however you feel it, then pull it out with certain instruments suitable for its extraction.
If however you cannot get at it, due to the constriction of the wound, and because of the length of the arrow deep inside: and there is no bone, nor nerve, nor vein there: then enlarge upon it, until the wound is widened, and it is possible to extract the arrow.
If however there are ears to which it is attached, then free the flesh fixed to them from every side, by every method you can, and try, if it cannot be released by breaking the ears and twisting them, until it escapes.
And when you administer the extraction of the arrow, wherever it is, then use the twisting of your hand, with forceps, to all parts, until you make it escape: and be subtle to the utmost subtlety, so that the arrow is not broken, and its extraction becomes difficult for you.
If you cannot get at it within one hour, then leave it for several days, until those things surrounding it putrefy. Then try again: for it will then be easier.  However, if a flow of blood opposes you, then administer what we have said about treatment in its chapter: and guard your skill from cutting a vein, or nerve, or tendon.
And use all the means possible for the escape of the arrow: and let it be with ease and calmness, or slowness and firmness, as I have told you.
And it is necessary, that when you pull the arrow, you make it weak according to the form in which the arrow was wounded. Because it is more helpful. If, however, that is not possible for you, then administer what is possible for you from the figures.
However, if an arrow emerges from the opposite side of the other, either something already proceeds from it outwards, or the arrowhead creates a bump on the skin's surface above, and you see it protruding: therefore, cut above it, and let the cut be according to the size that the forceps can grasp.
Then pull it: for it will then be easy to remove.
However, if it remains embedded in the bone, then twist your hand in a circular motion until the arrow presses against the bone, and widens the space for itself; then pull it.
And if not, leave it for a few days; then try it again until it comes out.
If the arrow's wood is embedded in it, then push it out along with it.
However, if the wood has broken off and you wish to administer a push, then insert a concave instrument into it, so that its concavity enters the arrow's tail; then push it out with it.
If the arrow is concave, then push it with an instrument entering into it. For the arrow is relieved by it.
If the arrow is poisoned, then it is necessary to cut away all the flesh in which the poison is, if that is possible for you. Then treat it with whatever is appropriate for it.
If the arrow is lodged in the chest, or in the belly, or in the bladder, or in the side; and it is close to what you are touching with the probe, and it is possible for you to cut above it; then cut, and beware of cutting a vein, or a nerve, and pull it out.
Then sew the wound, if you see it, and treat it until it is healed.
And this is the form of the forceps with which arrows are extracted.
Let the extremities be like the beak of a bird, made of iron: when they grasp the arrow, they do not release it.
And many forms are made from them, large, and small, and medium, according to the quantity of the arrow's size, and its smallness, and the breadth of the wound, and its constriction.
The form of the concave pusher.
And the form of the deaf pusher.
This is the deaf extremity, like a ray, so that its entrance into the concave arrow may be eased, and it may be driven with it.
Concerning the phlebotomy of veins.
Thirty veins are those to be opened, whose use runs through the body.
Of these, sixteen are in the head: two pulsating veins, which are behind the ears, called the two Herbs; and two arteries, which appear in the two temples; and two veins that are in the two tear ducts of the two eyes, called the Looking veins; and a vein prepared in the middle of the forehead; and a vein that is at the extremity of the nose. And two Guidegi, which are in the neck. And two veins that are in the upper lip of the mouth. And two veins that are in the lower lip. And these are the veins called Algecherich. And two veins that are under the tongue.
The veins, however, which are opened in the arms, are five veins in each arm.
One of them is the Cephalic, and it is from the wild side: and the common people call it the vein of the head. And the Fusca. And there is the Medial vein, and it is composed of a branch of the Basilic and a branch of the Cephalic: and the common people call it the vein of the body. And the Basilic, and it is situated on the domestic side: and it is also called the Tickler. And the common people call it the vein of the belly. And the Brachial vein, and it is situated above the fold: and it is the one in which bloodletting is done, and it is the one that appears above the thumb by clear appearance.
And the Salvatella: and it is the vein that is between the ring finger and the little finger: and it has two branches.
And in the leg and in the foot there are three veins. One of them is that which is under the curve of the thumb on the wild side. And the second is the Saphena: and its place is at the heel on the domestic side. And the vein Nesse: and its place is at the heel on the wild side.
And in the other leg there are three veins similar to those.
Concerning the phlebotomy of two veins behind the ears.
Phlebotomies of the two veins behind the ears are beneficial for chronic colds, and hemiplegia, and headache, and bad old ulcers of the head.
And the quality of the phlebotomy is according to what I relate. And it is, that the patient's head be shaved, and his occiput be rubbed in the place of the two veins with a rough cloth by good rubbing. Then let the patient tie his neck with a bandage, until the two veins appear. And their locations are behind the ears in two depressed areas of the head. Therefore, seek them with your finger, and where you feel their pulse beneath your finger, mark that spot with ink.
Then take a phlebotomous knife, and it is called the cutting one. Then insert it beneath the vein in the skin, until the phlebotomous reaches the opening. Then elevate your hand with the vein and skin upward, and cut the vein with the skin with an incision that cuts into it. And let the length of the incision be the quantity of two joined fingers, or approximately that: and make as much blood exit as you wish.
Then bind it with bandages, and leave it until it heals.
And already in the beginning of the book was the incision of them, and the cauterization of them.
The phlebotomy of the two arteries in the two temples is beneficial for chronic headaches; and for crooked noses and frequent eye diseases; and for the excessive flow of acute humors to the eyes.
And the quality of phlebotomizing them is according to what I tell you. Let the sick person hold their neck tightly, until two veins appear clearly to the eye; and let their pulse be evident under your finger. Then mark the spots with ink.
Then, lift the skin above the vein upward with your index finger, and insert the lancet underneath, and lift the vein upward. And cut it, as you did in the other two veins: and cause blood to flow according to your need.
Then loosen the constriction of the sick person, and place your finger over the vein for one hour. Afterwards, place a poultice and pads on it, and tighten it firmly over the constriction; and leave it until it is healed.
And already the remembrance of them, and their incision, and their extraction, has preceded at the beginning of the book.
Indeed, the benefit of phlebotomy of the forehead vein is after phlebotomy of the Cephalic vein, for old facial ailments, and for migraine, and redness, and foul ulcers.
And its quality of phlebotomy is, according to what I tell you. The sick man tightens his neck with a bandage until the vein appears. Then take the instrument which is called Fossorium.
Place the prominent point which is on the head of the Fossorium over the vein itself, and strike it with a comb, or with something else in its manner: and let the blood run according to the amount that you wish. Then loosen the wound of the sick person, and bind it, until it is healed.
And sometimes it is opened with a wide phlebotome. However, it should not be a phlebotome with a sharp tip, like the other phlebotomes, but rather with a wide tip. And open it with the phlebotome across the vein; since the bone is close by, and perhaps the phlebotome will break when it is slender.
The benefit of the two veins which are in the two tear ducts of the two eyes is for the eye from scabies and redness; and swelling in the eye, and diseases of the face.
And the quality of their phlebotomy is such that it tightens the weak neck with a bandage. Then open them with the phlebotome, and you be standing over its head.
And let the phlebotomy be performed across, slightly lengthwise, with a small, somewhat broad phlebotome, for there is no flesh in the place. For if the phlebotome is of a slender extremity, perhaps it will break.
Then make the blood flow out as much as you need. And place a compress over them, and bind them for one night, and unbind them.
Phlebotomy of the veins of the Nose is beneficial in acute fevers, and violent headaches, and diseases of the face, such as migraine, and redness that occurs in the face, especially when it is chronic.
And the quality of its phlebotomy is such that the infirm person tightens their neck. Then hold his nose with your left hand, and take a slender, long lancet, and insert it into the middle of his nostril, between that which separates the nose according to its straightness. Because the vein is not apparent to the senses there, and because blood begins to flow immediately.
And you should hold your hand with the lancet for a short time, and make blood flow according to your need. Then tie it with one bandage. For it is quickly restored.
The phlebotomy of Guidegi's two veins is helpful for constriction of breath, and the onset of leprosy, and melancholic illnesses, and impetigo and evil ulcers, and those that corrode, that is, eruptive herpes.
And the quality of phlebotomy of those veins is such that the weak man should be held up by both hands on his neck with a bandage: and the craftsman should stand over the head of the sick man, and the sick man should be seated upon a seat.
Then the phlebotomist should open the vein to a length of ample phlebotomy for a little while. Then draw out a moderate quantity of blood, or according to that which you see necessary for it. Then do likewise with the other vein.
Afterwards, loosen the bandage, and bind the two veins with a middle constriction, so that the sick person is not choked. And leave it until morning. For the wound is healed.
Concerning the phlebotomy of veins by Algeberich. The veins, according to Algeberich, are helpful in phlebotomy, because they contribute to alchol in the mouth, and the corruption of the gums, and bad ulcers, and the fissures of the lips, and bad ulcers, even those which are in the nose and in its surroundings.
And the quality of phlebotomy of them is, that you should make the infirm person sit before you, and bind his neck with a cloth. Then, roll back his lip, and look at the two veins: one is on the right of the lip, and the second on its left; and the demonstration of both is through the blackness of both. And that is true, because around them are small, black veins. Therefore, cut each one with a cut proceeding.
If, however, there is doubt upon you, and you do not know which of them it is, then aim at the incision of the larger of them, and the more evident.
And similarly do concerning the two veins which are in the upper lip; and indeed custom runs much with it; and cut the two veins which are in the lower lip.
Concerning phlebotomy of the vein under the tongue after the phlebotomy of the Cephalic vein. However, the benefit of phlebotomy of the two veins which are under the tongue is that phlebotomy of the Cephalic vein is for the suffocations which are in the throat, and the ailment of the uvula, and the ailments of the mouth.
And the quality of phlebotomizing them is such that the infirm person sits between your hands in the presence of the sun; and raise his tongue, and look under his tongue on one side of it for a vein, and on the other side of it for a vein; and the colour of each of them slopes towards blackness.
Therefore phlebotomize each of them; and be careful not to extend your hand too far in phlebotomizing them. For under it are arteries, and perhaps a flow of blood will occur from those arteries.
The three veins, however, which are phlebotomized in the elbow, are those in whose phlebotomy a custom has run throughout all men.
And their phlebotomy is done according to two methods. Either by piercing with a broad myrtle phlebotom, or by making an incision small enough to be considered a groove, or by cutting with a knife phlebotom, and that is the Alnesil. And this is its form.
And from them are broad and narrow varieties, according to the width and constriction of the veins. The form of the myrtle phlebotom should be broad, as you see. And it is suitable for opening concave, full, proceeding, apparent, large veins, and those containing thick, turbid blood.
And this phlebotom is of smaller width, and with finer extremities.
And it is suitable for the phlebotomy of thin veins, which contain thin, yellowish blood.
The Basilica vein, which is one of these three veins, is a help to phlebotomy. Because it draws blood from illnesses that are under the throat and neck, from there following the chest, and the stomach; and it is fitting for the phlebotomist, when phlebotomizing it, to be cautious, and to be above fear from it, for there is an artery under it. If, therefore, he errs and adds in the phlebotomist's puncture, he will cut that artery, and a flow of blood will occur. Therefore it is necessary, that he not phlebotomize it with a phlebotomy-piercing instrument; rather, let him cut with a lancet.
If the Basilica vein is not clearly visible, then it is necessary to seek another vein, and to change to another, or to look for one of its branches; or to phlebotomize the brachial vein instead. For it is indeed obvious, and cut it with the phlebotomist's lancet, as we said.
If, however, you wish to phlebotomize the same vein, then it is necessary first to constrict the arm, so that you touch the spot until you recognize the pulse location. Then mark the spot with ink, thereafter the arm, and cut the vein with a concave incision using the phlebotomist's lancet, as we said. And beware that the incision does not fall a space from the artery's location.
And when you see, as you tighten the ligature, inflation in the spot you marked with ink, then that inflation is the inflation of the artery: therefore, avoid it.
If you see blood at the phlebotomy leaping out, like a child's urine, and it is thin, red blood, then know that it is from arterial blood. Then quickly place your finger over it for a long space, then remove your finger. If therefore blood is cut (for it is often cut), then tighten the arm and release it, and let the sick person beware of movement of it, and let fear be upon him.
And let him not move it for several days until it is healed.
If however the blood is not cut, and it conquers you, and there is no medicine present for you in your moment, then cut the artery, if it appears to you. For its extremities contract, and blood is cut.
Or take the bark of the fig tree, and split it; take one half, and tie it over the place of the vein with a proper tightening, with bandage and dressings until another day.
Then if the blood is cut, it is good. And if not, then treat it with that whose previous mention was from the place of powders that cut off the flow of blood; and the excision of its blood is not difficult in many dispositions, due to the small place of the wound, and the possibility of a bandage from the arm.
Phlebotomy of the Fusca vein is helpful, as it draws blood from the upper part of the head and the lower part of the body; because it is composed of a branch of the Basilic vein and a branch of the Cephalic vein, as we have said.
And it befits the phlebotomist that there be caution in his phlebotomy thereof, for beneath it is a nerve. If, therefore, the phlebotomist adds in the puncture, and finds a nerve, there arises numbness in it, and the healing becomes difficult, and perhaps it is not healed completely.
And this nerve often appears to the sense. However, if it is hidden in some people, and is subtle and does not show itself, then it is necessary that you place your phlebotomy such that you phlebotomize the section with Alnesil, and avoid the nerve with your strength.
If the vein is between two nerves, then divide the vein lengthwise.
Of the Cephalic vein. Phlebotomy of the Cephalic vein is beneficial, because it draws blood from the head, and is helpful in eye ailments.
And it is necessary in this vein properly, if you will, to phlebotomize it by pricking with an olive-wood phlebotome, or with a myrtle-wood phlebotome, because it is the safest of all veins, since there is no artery, nor nerve, beneath it.
However, it is appropriate for you in phlebotomy to avoid striking the head of the muscle with the phlebotome, and to seek a gentler spot; and it does not harm it if it is not found with the first strike, so that it may be repeated with phlebotomy several times.
However, perhaps it may become inflamed in some people when not phlebotomized in the first strike, but that inflammation does not harm it in any way.
Concerning the usefulness of phlebotomy, and the precautions to be observed in it. The quality of phlebotomy, and its accompanying occurrences, concerning which you must consider its correction.
First, you should know that phlebotomy is not performed except in the preservation of health, and its persistence, and as a precaution against the approach of illnesses.
And that phlebotomy is performed on one of the three veins in the elbow, namely the Cephalic, the Fusca, and the Basilica. And phlebotomy is to be performed in the beginning of Spring on an empty stomach, in the true spring and summer, and in Autumn and Winter after eating, when signs of fullness appear.
And phlebotomy should be performed on the first, or on the third day, after three hours have passed.
However, phlebotomy performed in illnesses does not have a specific time; but when necessity and suitability for it arise, in the night, or the day, and in every hour, and in all time.
And children should not be bled until they reach fourteen years of age. Nor should a pregnant woman be bled, except from the fourth to the seventh month. Nor should the elderly be bled who have already passed sixty years.
Therefore, when someone wishes to be bled, regardless of the reason, it is necessary that they cleanse their bowels beforehand with a gentle clyster, if there is a great deal of retained stool, so that the veins do not draw putrid, harmful superfluities from the intestines to the principal members during the bleeding.
Nor should a patient suffering from nausea, or one who is surfeited, or one recovering from intoxication be bled, until that is removed from them. Nor should a sick person with dropsy.
And bleeding should be avoided again after colic, vomiting, diarrhea, frequent intercourse, labor, exercise, vigils, fasting, and anything that weakens the body's or animal's strength.
Then consider the subtlety of the humors beforehand, if the blood is thick with foods, drinks, and medications; if it is possible for him to do so.
Then let him enter a bath, if nothing prevents him; or let him exercise with some exercise, so that the blood may become thinner.
And place his phlebotomy on his chest, around the third day, as we have said; and strive that his chest be evacuated that day from all evil animal accidents: such as anguish, and anger, and fear; and from all bodily accidents, such as labor, and pain, and superfluity of intercourse, and similar things.
And let him have present in his seat things with which the custom of men has run in their use: from aromatics, and scents, and festivities and similar things by as much possibility as possible.
Then let the phlebotomist sit upon a cushion that is higher than the cushion of the one undergoing phlebotomy; afterwards, let the phlebotomist pull out his arm and rub it with his hand twice or thrice. Then bind the ligature with a strap, and wrap it twice.
And let the constriction be moderate, because when the constriction is unequal, or excessive in the constriction, then it hinders the flow of blood; or in its loosening, it also hinders the flow of blood.
Then after the constriction, it is necessary that the one undergoing phlebotomy rub their hands together until the veins swell and are evident to the senses.
Then the phlebotomist should wipe the incision with a little old oil, properly.
Then he should place his left index finger lightly on the vein itself, under the spot where he intends to make the incision, so that the vein does not move away and the incision is not missed. Because some veins are like a fleeing chord during phlebotomy: and some are full of airiness; and when you place the incision on them, they contract under the incision, and the phlebotomist is deceived, and the incision does not open the vein; and if he does open it, it is narrow.  Therefore, it is necessary that the phlebotomist be firm and careful in all these matters.
Then he should withdraw the incision. If therefore the vein opens at once, then it is good. And if not, then repeat it another time slightly below or above that place with haste, if the place is not already inflamed.
If, however, it becomes inflamed, or the patient weakens, then let him rest for a day or two, and do not tighten the bandage. For perhaps it will attract a hot inflammation. Nor should he enter a bath.
Then he should repeat the phlebotomy, if he wishes.
If the opening is small, or the blood flows subtly, and you fear that not enough of the desired quantity will be removed from the blood, then repeat the phlebotomy in the same opening, straight on, and add a little to the procedure.
And do it quickly, before the place becomes inflamed, for an inflamed place occurs in many people with a minor operation.
If therefore you see it already inflamed, then do not repeat it on it at all, for it will not excuse anything in your eyes. And place something of the dregs of oil upon it, because it alleviates the flow of blood. And it is better in this place than the oil itself, and than other oils.
And similarly administer the dregs of oil in all your phlebotomies, through which phlebotomies veins at the prohibition of the flow of blood: and the Tyrians, Alfarach, and Sagzenea do it, when one of them is placed on the site.
For blood is made thinner and resolved, when it is thick.
If a large abscess occurs at the site of the phlebotomy, for it often happens, especially to one who is not a phlebotomist except on that occasion, or if the opening of the vein is small, then quickly place a sponge soaked in water and salt, warmed for a short time, on the site, and compress it for an hour, because it dissolves.
And it is necessary to do this after the blood has exited the vein itself, with its completion, or from another vein.
If some blackness or greenness remains at the site after a few days, it does no harm: and if you wish, place some dissolved aloe or myrrh on it, or something from the juice of mint, that is, of calamint or something similar to it.
And very often abscesses and prominences occur at the phlebotomy of the Basilica.
Therefore, place your hand upon it; then, if you find softness upon your compression of it, then that prominence is bad. Therefore, beware to put something upon it from that which we said. For perhaps blood of the artery will flow from it.
But make a plaster to it with that in which there is astringency, so that the place may be hardened. Then treat it with the remaining treatment until it is healed.
And it is necessary to extract from every man's blood according to the quantity of its virtue. And what appears from the color prevailing over the blood.
For if the blood is black, then let it go out until it turns red; and similarly, if you see it thick, then let it go out until it becomes thin; and similarly, if it is sharp, until its sharpness is removed.
And it is necessary for one who is full, strong, and needs bloodletting once, that the vein's phlebotomy be enlarged and the phlebotomist be wide; and in one who is weak, it should be the opposite of that.
And it is necessary to extract it in stages, and that the opening be narrow; and better that is administered in vein phlebotomy is that it be a transverse section, not a puncture.
And this method of bloodletting is safe from blood flow and from nerve incision; and it is more praiseworthy and safer than bloodletting by width and length.
And to whom fainting is accustomed to occur during bloodletting, it is necessary that he eat beforehand a little bread soaked in pomegranate-seed water, or secaniabin, if it is heated; and extract from his blood in three or four stages.
And if he is of a cold complexion, then let him take beforehand bread soaked in the syrup of miua, or in syrup of honey aromatized with spices, or in aromatic scented syrup.
If fainting occurs during bloodletting, and the cause of it is the excessive outflow of blood, then it is necessary that he drink water of meat, and a subtle scented syrup; and use aromatics with algalia, and let his chest be lined with it; and let him use the rest that we have said in the division on fainting in the chapter on evacuation.
However, whoever wants to evacuate their arm and release their blood a second time, it is necessary for one whose bloodletting is due to much evacuation and whose strength is weak, to release the blood gradually according to their strength in successive days.
However, whoever wants to evacuate their arm and release their blood a second time, and whose body is strong, then let them do it after seven or nine hours from their first bloodletting.
Whoever wishes to draw blood from their body to the opposite part to which it inclines, it is necessary that they be evacuated on the second or third day.
In whose body, however, there is much blood already heated and sharpened, and causing fever to occur, it is necessary that blood depart from it in one turn, and a great quantity be drawn out of it; and the opening be enlarged until fainting occurs; after you have observed all the conditions of bloodletting; and so that you place your hand upon his pulse at the passage of the blood, lest death occur in place of fainting. For it often happens that, when the bloodletter does not know, and negligence falls.
And it is not appropriate, when you wish to release the arm and release blood a second time, when the vein's opening is already closed and the blood's exit is difficult, to press upon it forcefully and quickly extend or flex it. For that is very bad; indeed, leave it until it is bled a second time.
Or remove with sharpness the clotted blood from the vein's opening, and place some dissolved salt in water upon it. And place upon it something from tyriaca alfaroch or sagzenea, and apply gentle pressure until blood emerges.
If however the vein is already suppurated, then leave it and do not touch it until the abscess subsides.
If necessity provokes you to draw blood a second time, and there is no excuse, then either bleed the patient above that place or in another vein of another arm.
The arm's vein, however, is bled at the location of the Fusca and Basilica veins, if these are not found, and are hidden, since it is composed of them.
And the quality of the bloodletting is such that the patient should immerse their hand in warm water until the skin reddens and the vein becomes clearly visible.
Then it should be tied above it with a ligature of moderate tightness. Then bleed along the vein's transverse direction gradually, not laterally, nor longitudinally: and let the opening be wide, and let your incision for the bloodletting be above the joint of the hand for a short while.
If the flow of blood is hindered, then again immerse the hand in warm water, and let the blood flow into the water until you achieve your desired outcome.
However, if you are in the days of summer, then you may refrain from repeatedly immersing the hand in warm water. And indeed, the flow of blood is mostly placed in warm water during the time of winter.
And bloodletting of this vein is safer than all veins, because there is no pulsating vein or nerve beneath it.
Bloodletting of the Saluatella vein of the right hand is beneficial for liver ailments.
And the quality of its bloodletting is such that you should tie the mahason of the hand with a ligature, or with your hand, after you immerse it in warm water, until the vein swells and is clearly evident to the senses. Then cut the vein across. And if you cut it entirely, it will not harm him in any way.
And be careful not to sever your hand with the lancet. For beneath it are the nerves of the fingers exposed from the flesh.
Then return the hand to warm water, and let the blood flow into it. For if you do not return it to warm water, the blood clots in the vein's opening and is prevented from flowing.
When you have extracted the necessary quantity of blood, then place oil and salt upon the vein: so that it does not quickly solidify.
And similarly it is necessary to do with every constricted branch.
The benefit of phlebotomy from the left hand is: because it is helpful for ailments of the spleen; and similarly do in its phlebotomy, as you did in the second equally.
The benefit of saphenous phlebotomy is for ailments that are in the lower part of the body, such as ailments of the uterus, and the retention of menses, and illnesses of the kidneys, and old sores of the hips and legs, and similar ailments.
And the method of his phlebotomy is that the sick person should put his foot in warm water, and do friction upon it, until the veins are prominent; and tie above the joint of the foot with a bandage. And indeed the vein's location is near the heel, appearing towards the toe; and from it branches many branches on the front of the foot.
Therefore, phlebotomize it in its larger branch, or at the heel in its aggregation, because it is better and safer.
If you phlebotomize it on the front of the foot, then beware of the nerves which are under it on the front of the foot.
And place your phlebotomy, by which you phlebotomize it transversely, as if you wish to cut it; and let the phlebotomist be Nesil.
If, however, the outflow of blood is prohibited, then repeatedly immerse his foot in warm water; and let the blood run into it, until it is evacuated.
If the phlebotomist errs in the first attempt, then he should repeat the phlebotomy a little higher up, for the place is safe, and you do not fear harm from it, when you beware of the nerves, as we said.
And similarly with the Saphena of the other foot equally.
The Sciatic vein's location, as we said, is at the heel on the wild side.
And the relief of phlebotomy is for it, for the pain of the hip, when it is due to hot blood.
And the quality of its phlebotomy is that the sick person should enter a bath and hurry; and he should bind his leg from the side of the hip up to the heel by four fingers with a long thin strip: for it does not appear except because of that.
Therefore, when it appears, then let the phlebotomist cut it according to whatever skill is possible to you, either transversely; and it is better, or to cut it with an incision, or to divide it by a section. For its location is safer, and it is very hidden in many people.
For its location is safer, and it is very hidden in many people. If, however, you do not find it, and it does not appear at all to the senses: then let the phlebotomist cut some branch of it, and that is the one which appears on the back of the foot, and towards the ear; and beware of nerves, and make the blood flow in the quantity you wish.
Then loosen the constriction, and place a skin over the phlebotomy site, and bind the place. For it heals quickly.
Concerning cupping, and the quality of its use.
Cupping is done from horns, and from air, and from glass.
And cupping indeed is done according to two methods. With scarification, and the extraction of blood; and the other is cupping without scarification.
