And this cupping which is without scarification, is according to two methods. Or indeed it is with fire. Or it is without fire.
And cupping which is administered with scarification and the extraction of blood are fourteen places of the body. Of which one is, cupping of the Alnocrati, and it is the last of the head: and Alkehel, and it is the middle of the neck: and cuppings of the Alhagdahim, and they are two sheets of the neck on both sides together: and cuppings of the mentum, and it is under the jaw in the lower part of the mouth: and cuppings of the two shoulder blades: and cuppings of the Alachosos on the end of the tail: and cuppings of the two armpits, and they are in the middle of the two arms: and cuppings of the two thighs: and cuppings of the two calves.
And cupping indeed does not extract blood from the small veins scattered in the flesh, and because of that no virtue is lost from them, as is the case with venesection. Nor should cupping, whether with fire or without fire, be administered in any illness arising from fullness until the whole body is emptied.
If necessity prompts cupping from illness or because of custom, we administer it at any hour at the beginning of the month, at the end of it, and in the middle of it, and at whatever time it may be.
That is because there are people who, when blood multiplies in them until they need its extraction by cupping, experience heaviness and a feeling of pressure in their head.
And among them are those who experience fullness and redness in their face, head, and neck. And among them are those who experience itching in their face and forehead, and darkness and dullness in their eyes.
And among them are those who scratch the site of their cupping. And among them are those whose laughter increases. And among them are those who find the taste of blood in their mouth: or the tongue becomes ulcerated and they spit blood. And among them are those whose sleep increases. And among them are those who see in their dreams blood and redness, and slain persons, and wounds, and things similar to them.
Therefore, when we see any of these things, and especially if it is in the third middle of the month, we order the application of a wind instrument to be made after two or three hours have passed during the day.
The benefit of cupping the nape of the neck is that it contributes to relieving heaviness in the head and what flows to the eyes. But it is necessary that it be after the evacuation of the whole body.
And this cupping is in the place of a cephalic phlebotomy.
And it is not permitted for one of cold temperament, or in whom there is catarrh, to use it. For it brings him great harm.
And because of that, it is not fitting that the elderly and those with cold ailments of the head use it.
But whoever becomes assiduous upon it, forgetfulness is generated in him.
And because of that, you should instruct the surgeon to withdraw his hand with the wind instrument a little lower, fearing the generation of forgetfulness.
The use of a wind instrument, or Alkehel, is in the place of phlebotomy of the foot, and phlebotomy of the basilica vein, and therefore benefits asthma, and shortness of breath, and the fracture of the instrument for breathing, and coughing, and repletion.
And it is necessary that the wind instrument Alkehel be propelled somewhat, because if it is placed lower, it generates weakness in the heart and stomach.
The use of the alhagdahim wind instrument benefits accidental headaches, and ophthalmia, and migraine, and perforation, and toothache; and it is in the place of phlebotomy of the basilica vein.
And it is necessary that you instruct the surgeon not to press his hand with the scarification, so that he does not cut an artery and cause bleeding.
The use of a wind instrument under the chin benefits sores in the mouth, and gum disease, and similar diseases of the mouth; and it is in the place of phlebotomy of the alieherich, which are in the two lips.
The use of spatulas benefits alchafacham, which is caused by reflux and heat.
The use of the wind instrument on the inner parts of the two focuses benefits the phlebotomy of the three veins, the Basilica, and Fusca, and Cephalica, because it draws blood from all the small veins in the flesh, and those small veins draw from other larger veins until the extraction reaches the three veins.
And it is necessary that you instruct the surgeon not to proceed too far in the scarification, because the area is denuded of flesh, and nerves and arteries are beneath it.
The cupping instrument applied to alchausos benefits hemorrhoids of the anus and lower ulcers.
And it is necessary that you instruct the cupper to use a large, air-filled cupping glass, because the area needs strong suction, and perhaps the delicate cupping glasses break; and let it be scarified with a large incision.
Cupping of the legs produces significant reduction, because they draw blood from the entire body, and they benefit old pains in the kidneys, uterus, and bladder; they induce menstruation and benefit bothor and ademenul; and they stand in place of phlebotomy of the two saphenous veins and alhorcobeni.
However, they weaken the body greatly, and cause fainting in many people.
And the benefit of alhorcobeni cupping is close to the benefit of cupping the two legs.
The quality of cupping placement is to apply an initially empty cupping glass and to draw with moderate suction; and do not prolong the cupping placement, but apply them quickly and remove them quickly, so that the fluids come to the location with equal arrival; and do not cease repeating this and continuing it, until you see the location already reddened and inflated, and the redness of blood appears; then scarify, and repeat the suction gradually. Then consider in the disposition of the bodies.
For whoever is of tender flesh, of rare pores, it is necessary that you scarify him once, no more, so that the location is not ulcerated.
And instruct the cupper to enlarge the scarification slightly, and deepen it slightly; and to moderate the suction with ease, speed, and smooth movement.
If there is thickness in the blood, then it is necessary that you scarify him twice. Indeed, in the first instance, a path is opened to the thinning of the blood and its becoming watery; and in the second, indeed, to the careful examination of the extraction of thick blood.
If, however, the blood is very foul, then let the scarification be done a third time, so that the final result may be achieved.
And finally, when we wish to extract blood gradually, or a little bit, one scarification is sufficient for us. If we wish for the extraction of much blood, we scarify with many scarifications.
And if we measure that the blood is thick, then it is necessary that it be scarified with a deep scarification; and the equal limit in the scarification is the depth of the skin.
What is that which must be administered from oils at the position of Cupping, and what should the cupper beware of?
In one whose skin is thick, hard, dark, and whose pores are narrow, it is necessary that the cupping places be anointed with opening, softening, dissolving oils: if it is in summer, such as alkeiri oil, or violet oil, or oil from sweet almonds, or oil from pumpkin seed.
And if it is in winter, then narcissus oil, or lily oil, or camomile oil, or sarbatin oil, and similar ones.
If however the superfluity is thick, cold: then the oil should be amaracin oil, or balsam oil, or alben oil, or anise oil, and similar ones.
If however the cupper has large pores, soft flesh: then it is necessary that he refrain from oil, and it is necessary that these wash the cupping places afterward with rose water, or with cold water, or with soothing water, or with pumpkin water, or with purslane water, and similar ones.
He whose blood is of much moisture, should wash the cuppings with vinegar, or with myrtle water, and sumac, and similar ones.
However, whose superfluities are thick, let him wash his cuppings with old wine, or with sorrel water, or with anise decoction, or camomile, and similar to them.
And it is necessary to be cautious about cupping in the bath, or in the vicinity of the bath; indeed, it is necessary to administer it after exiting the bath by an hour, or by two hours.
And it is not necessary that someone sleep after cupping.
What is it that cupping, or bloodletting, should be governed by before cupping?
And after it, it is necessary, firstly, to consider: then, if the cupped or bled person is choleric, and sharpness and inflammation overcome his blood: then it is necessary that he take cooling things, such as pomegranate juice, and also endive with vinegar and lettuce, and secaniabin, and iuleb, and similar to them.
And put his food as poultry, and the meats of sheep sekebiget, and foods seasoned with agrimony; and similar to them.
And whoever's complexion is cold, it is necessary that he take in drink syrup of honey, or of almiua, or alsecaniabin made from seeds; and let him take medium fragrant wine, which is between old and new; and it is prescribed that he use little food. And put his food as poultry, and canary birds, and sparrows, that is, small birds, and young pigeons issiadebet.
And it is necessary that the drink on the day of cupping and bloodletting be more than the food; and it is necessary that in some men, a tyriaca alfaroch, or a medicine of musk, that is, musk, or alesselithe be given in drink before cupping, and before bloodletting, or after, so that the main members might be strengthened, and the blood be refined. And it is not necessary that they take it in heated drink.
Concerning cupping without scarification. However, cupping without scarification is cupping that is applied to the liver, and spleen, and gout, and to the stomach, navel, and kidney area, and the hip joint; since these members do not tolerate scarification upon themselves.
And they are not sought out for anything except either the drawing of blood from a member to another member; just as we place cupping on the breasts in illness of a nosebleed. And we use them to relieve cold, viscous windiness in a member; such as placing a cupping on the stomach and navel. For it indeed rarefies the member and warms it, and removes the pain because it has dissolved that windiness.
It is also placed on the kidneys, when obstruction or stone occurs in them. For by its power of suction perhaps it opens the obstruction or removes the stone from its place.
And similarly it does when placed on the liver and spleen, regarding the windiness to be treated in both.
And these cupping glasses are administered empty with only a cut. And sometimes they are administered with fire.
And sometimes they are administered full of lukewarm water in cases of pleurisy, and that is so that the cupping glass is filled, and is large, with just warm water, or water in which herbs suitable for the condition have been boiled.
Form of a cupping glass administered with fire.
Let its opening be the width of two open fingers, as we have formed it; and let its depth be half a palm; and let there be a small hole on its side, roughly in the middle of it, of a size such that a needle can enter it. Let it be made of bronze or brass, with thick, smooth, even, and polished edges, so that it does not injure the limb on its placement upon it; and let there be a cross-bar of bronze or iron in its middle, where the candle with fire is placed.
And now this cupping glass is made large, larger than the one we have described, and a little smaller than that, according to the illnesses and the age of the one who uses it.
For the cupping glasses of children and thin people are different from the cupping glasses of men and those with large bodies.
The method of placing this cupping glass with fire on the limb is to light a wick with fire from linen properly, or a small candle of wax; and place it over the middle of the cross-bar which is in the middle of the cupping glass; so that the fire is burning approximately at the lower part of the cupping glass, so that the body of the sick person is not burned.
Then it is placed on the limb; and a finger is placed over the hole I mentioned, until, while holding the cupping glass, as needed, we remove the finger, and vapor will exit through that hole, and the cupping glass will immediately release.
Then light the wick according to its method, and repeat it, if you need it.
However, in a cupping glass used in the illness of pleurisy with water, there is no cross-bar, nor a hole; and it is not administered unless because it is filled with water, and placed on the limb only.
And this is its form.
And the larger this cupping glass is, so that it can hold a lot of water, the better it is.
Concerning the suspension of leeches.
Leech applications are not administered in many places, unless in those parts of the body where the application of cupping is not possible, due to their small size, such as the lip, the gums, and similar areas. Or in a limb denuded of flesh, such as the finger, the nose, and similar parts.
And the quality of using them is: to obtain leeches which are in fresh, clean waters, free from putrefaction.
Then they should be kept in fresh water day and night, until they are hungry and nothing remains in their belly. Then the body should first be evacuated by bloodletting and cupping. Then wash the sick limb until it is red. Then place them upon it.
When they are filled, fall, and suction of the area with a cupping device is possible, then it is most helpful; and if not, then wash the area with vinegar. Then with much water, it should be rubbed and squeezed out.
If the course of blood after the fall of the Sanguisugarum is prolonged and is oozing, then dip a linen cloth into cold water and place it over it until the oozing is stopped.
If the blood multiplies, then powder crushed zegi, or gallnuts, and similar astringents, on it, until the blood stops.
Or place the halves of peeled beans on the area and leave them until the beans adhere to the place, for the blood is stopped.
And if a repetition of Sanguisugarum is necessary, when possible, change them.
If, however, leeches are prevented from suspension, then the area is wiped with fresh blood. Or stick a needle into the places, until something comes out from the blood. Then place them, for they themselves, when they feel something of the blood, adhere immediately.
When therefore you wish them to fall off, pulverize something of ground aloe, or salt, or ashes, upon them: and they will fall off immediately.
This chapter is also very necessary in the art of Medicine, and is about the restoration of fractures and dislocations of accidents in bones.
Know, O Children, that the foolish physicians and the common people boast of this chapter, and those who have never examined the books of the ancients, nor read a letter from them.
And for this reason, this method is private among the sciences in our land. And I have never encountered any good in it at all, and I have not acquired from it that which I have acquired except because of the length of reading the ancient books, and the study on the understanding of them, until I extracted the knowledge of it from them.
Then I adhered to experiment and study for the length of my life.
And now I have described to you from it in this chapter everything that contains my knowledge: and upon which my experiment has passed, after I have abbreviated it for you, and delivered it from the occupation of elongation, and abbreviated it by the end of abbreviation, and shown it at the end of declination: and I have formed in it many shapes from the shapes of instruments that are administered in it, since it is from the addition of declination, as I have done in the two preceding chapters.
Chapter II.
It is necessary, as we say in the prologue of this chapter, that the summaries and chapters, the understanding of which the craftsman is first required to achieve, to stand upon their truth; namely, so that you, and those who are ready, may know, or learn this noble art, and others from you.
Therefore, I say that when a fracture, or dislocation, or twisting, or fall occurs to someone, then it is necessary, first of all, to apply bloodletting to him, and to release him, or both at once, if nothing prevents it, such as weakness of strength; or if the person affected is a child, or an infirm old man; or if the time is one of intense heat, or intense cold.
Then let him be content in his food, with cold vegetables, and the flesh of birds and kids; and forbid wine, and heavy meats, and filling himself with foods and all food that fills the veins with blood. Until when you are secure from a hot abscess, and do not fear the effusion of matter to the limb; then let the sick man return to his first regimen with which his custom ran.
Therefore, when the broken bone begins to be restored, then it is necessary that the sick person be fed with nourishing foods, with much, heavy, thick nourishment, in which there is viscosity; such as foods made from cooked wheat with water, and rice, and heads, and feet, and bellies of cows, and eggs, and fresh fish; and heavy wine, and things similar to these. For with this regimen, the coagulation of the fracture is made more quickly and better.
And know that broken bones, when they are in men who are in early old age and decrepit, it is not possible that they be continuously joined and consolidated according to their own nature always, because of the dryness of their limbs; and those that are in them are consolidated and joined at the end of their leniency, like the bones of small children.
However, nature attaches something to the broken bone from every side, similar to glue, in which there is thickness, by which it adheres and binds it, until they are glued together and bound together, until it comes to the end of strength and firmness, as it was in the beginning, until nothing hinders it from its operations; and for this reason it is necessary that you place the nourishment of the sick person with foods in which there are heaviness, viscosity, and thickness, as we have said.
And know that fractures are of diverse kinds, according to the diversity of the limbs: for the fracture of the leg is different from the fracture of the skull; and the fracture of the breastbone is different from the fracture of the spine; and likewise all the other limbs are different from each other; and I have come with a recollection of every kind of fracture, explained in its own chapter, distinct from another. And the kinds of bone fractures are also diversified within themselves.
Because a fracture of it occurs in an arched shape, besides which splinters happen in it. And sometimes its fracture occurs along the length of the bone: and its fractures are splintered, that is, splinters, and proceeding additions, such as are in a reed when it is sharpened with a knife for writing. And not proceeding: and there is a fracture with a wound and disruption in the head; and there is a fracture with a small fissure.
And every type has a particular disposition, or instrument, for its restoration, according to which its recollection will come, distinct in its own places.
And from what is recognized as a bone fracture, there is its twisting, and its own prominence; and its appearance to the senses, and its sound when you press it with your hand.
Therefore, when there is no apparent twisting in the place, nor sound upon touching it with your hand while you touch the bone with agitation, nor does the infirm person find great pain there, then there is no fracture there, but it is possible that there be a twisting or a small fissure; therefore, you should not move it with your hand and compression entirely, but rather place upon it from medicines, whose recollection comes, after that moment which is appropriate to the place; then a thin bandage is applied.
And know that when a place is bent and split in two, besides the struggles which happen in it, nevertheless each part is removed from its mate; then it is necessary for you to begin at your own time to rectify it, by making it even before a hot abscess happens to it.
If however a hot abscess happens to it, then leave it for a few days until the abscess subsides. Then make it even according to any method possible by gentleness and skill.
And know that its restoration and its straightening are easier than the bone, in which struggles occur.
Then bind it, according to its remembrance will come to you.
If however there are struggles in the bone, then an extension of this broken limb is necessary from two parts, if it is a hand or foot; or with your hand, if the limb is small; or with two ropes, or with a rope and a hand.
And let your position be such that you place the limb upon an equal place, according to its natural shape, until when the broken parts of the limb are extended; then strive to return those additions to their places with all your skill and in any way possible; and beware, according to your strength, that through your operation no pain or harm befall the weak; and strive, according to your strength, to apply one of the two bones with its mate, according to the best forms.
And it is necessary at that time to try and touch it with your hand. If therefore you see anything different there, rectify it and equalize it according to the extent of your ability.
And beware of violent extension and strong compression, as many fools do. For many times, through their operations, they cause a hot abscess or contraction in the limb; as we have already seen it from their operations many times.
Then, after equalization and proper operation, and constriction of that limb, and rest and tranquility, advise the sick person about it. And have him beware that he does not move it during his waking hours, and his sleep at his turning, and his stirring, and his defecation, and all his movements with all his strength. And that he beware, lest the position of the limb be one which will follow with pain. That is, because when he feels pain or injury in the position of the limb, then it is necessary that he change it to another position in which he does not feel pain.
And take care with him that his position is even and straight, so that twisting does not occur in the limb when it is restored.
And the quality of the fracture of the limb is, according to what I relate.
Know that fractured limbs are diversified in their smallness, and their size, and their shape.
Therefore, those of them that are small, such as the arm, and the finger, and the foot, and similar to them, then it is necessary that there be a wrapping of soft, thin cloth. However, those of them that are large, such as the thigh and the leg, it is necessary that there be in them a broad, hard wrapping.
Because a broad ligament connects a large limb and binds it from every side with an equal constriction, so that no spot enters it.
Then, after your evacuation, begin to place on it a suitable liniment, in soft linen, over the place of the fracture itself. Then begin to wrap the bandage over the place of the fracture itself in three or four wrappings, according to what the limb deserves: and draw your hand a little with the bandage.
Then proceed with it towards the upper part from the place of the fracture, and bind less tightly than your previous binding of the fractured place. Then move away with the wrapping from the place of the fracture a little, and gradually loosen the constriction until you obtain a large amount from a healthy place.
Then take another strip, and wrap it again over the fractured place with wrappings. Afterwards proceed with the wrapping towards the lower part from the fracture. And let your procedure in the tightness of the wrapping, and its loosening, be according to what we have said in the first, upper wrapping.
Then place between the wrappings soft tow, or cloth, with which the twist of the fracture is equalized, if there is twist in it: and if not, do not put anything in it.
Then wrap another strip over it. Afterwards, immediately place properly made stars over these wrappings, if there is no swelling in the place, nor a hot abscess.
If however there is swelling in it, or an abscess: then place on it that which will sit on that abscess, and remove the swelling; and leave it for a few days. Then bind the stars over it.
And let the stars be from the halves of broad reeds, shaved and cleverly formed; or let them be from the wood from which sieves are made, which are made from pine, or from gerard, that is, from palm branches, or from calingi; and let the width of each star, according to this form and this same figure.
However, it is necessary that the star placed over the wound itself be somewhat thicker and wider than the other stars; and the length of the star should be according to what is permitted by the member's size and smallness.
Then bind over the stars with another bandage, according to the same first binding. Then bind over it with threads properly, according to what we said about the binding: and let your binding be over the fractured place more; and the further away it is from the fracture, the less the binding.
And it is necessary that the middle threads be of medium thickness and thinness, and be of soft linen.
Because if the threads are thick, as I have seen from the operation of foolish men who place their threads made from twisted linen cords, this is a great error; because the binding falls with them outside the quality.
And again, thin threads are not suitable, because you will not achieve by the binding with them what you want.
And it is not necessary that there be less than a finger's breadth between stitch and stitch.
If the patient is injured by the ends of the stitches after the binding in healthy areas, then place under them soft tow, or dyed wool, until nothing is injured by it.
However, when there is a fracture with a wound, and a rupture occurs in the skin, I will come with a recollection of it in the second chapter according to its particularity.
And know that not every broken limb needs to be bound with stitches from the first day. That is because when the limb is large, you should not place stitches upon it unless after five days, or seven, or more, according to your safety regarding the outcome of a hot abscess.
And this is the method of plasters, with which the ancients restored those that are placed upon a fracture and dislocation.
Method of an abbreviated common plaster, restoring a fracture, suitable for many complexions, especially women and children, since it is from that which is not overcome by heat nor cold.
And it is, that you should take from the mill's powder, and it is the flour's marrow which hangs in the mill's walls at the grinding of the millstone: and make it as it is, except that you sieve it with the white of an egg: and place its confection not thick, nor thin. Then use it.
Another method of plaster, restoring a fracture, and dislocation, and twisting. Take almonds, and laudanum, and acacia, and enula, and mugath; and juice of all, ana 10 scruples; myrrh, and aloes, both ana 5 scruples; and alethilic 20 scruples. Grind all, and sieve, and mix with alethilic water; or with the white of an egg, if the patient's complexion is inflamed. Then use this plaster. For it is of good composition, restores broken bones quickly, and benefits many people.
Another method of plaster, which also benefits fracture and twisting. Take mugath, and almonds, and white marshmallow root, of all, ana 10 scruples; myrrh, and aloes, both ana 5 scruples; acacia 6 scruples; Armenian bole 20 scruples. Grind all with much trituration, and sieve, and make into a paste with water, or the white of an egg; and administer.
Method of plaster for joints and bones separated from their places; and it relieves the pain that occurs to them, and the cracking of the bone, and the fracture. Take fresh wool, and immerse it in vinegar, and boiled oil; and place it on the affected area.
And in this plaster there is no power of restoration, but it is good in quieting a hot abscess; and it expels pains properly.
Method of plaster, which restores a broken bone. Take leaves of the wild fig, and leaves of the wild poppy: and grind them all together, and make a plaster from both.
Method of a shortened plaster, administered in the restoration of a broken bone; and when you wish resolution of a remaining abscess. Take of the root of marshmallow, and chamomile, and violet flowers, and herb flour, of each one part:  grind them all together, and mix them with wine, if the limb is not inflamed.
If it is inflamed, then make it with water of moist coriander, or with water, and use it.
Method of another stronger plaster in resolution, than this first, when a hard abscess occurs in the restoration of a limb. Take of marshmallow root, and flaxseed, and pomegranate fruit, and melilot, and sorrel, and violet flowers, and chamomile, of each one part; grind them all together, and mix them with willow water, or with fresh water, or with wine. All of that according to the warmth of the limb, and the rest of its heat.
Hippocrates, however, did not say in his book that anything should be placed upon a fractured limb during its restoration except a poultice made from wax and oil: nothing else; and he narrated that it should be of a consistency between thickness and thinness.
However, Galen thought that things should be placed on a fractured limb during its restoration that have a drying effect on heat, such as myrrh, and aloes and frankincense, and similar things to those which we have mentioned.
Method of the plaster which is beneficial for injury and pain. Take mugwort, and chickpeas, and the hairs of a man's head, cut off, or the feathers of a bird, and marshmallow, and salt in equal parts: grind them, and sift them, and mix them: and make a plaster from it.
The amount of time, however, for which the bandage should remain, and then be loosened, is that you should consider, if no pain, nor itching, occurs to the patient, nor is the broken bone moved from its place: then do not loosen it for many days. But if intense itching, or disturbing pain, or swelling occurs in that place: then hasten, and loosen it in an hour, and remove the plaster from it: and take a soft cloth, or a soft sea sponge, moist: and immerse it in warm water, and wash the place with it, until the itching subsides, and the pain ceases.
But if intense itching, or disturbing pain, or swelling occurs in that place: then hasten, and loosen it in an hour, and remove the plaster from it: and take a soft cloth, or a soft sea sponge, moist: and immerse it in warm water, and wash the place with it, until the itching subsides, and the pain ceases. Then allow the limb to rest for one hour. Then place upon it cut wool, soaked in vinegar and oil, or rose oil: and bind it above for one night, until you are certain of avoiding a hot abscess, and the swelling of the limb subsides, and its pain is removed.
Then apply again a fine bandage and a small plaster: and do not tighten it with your first constriction, and be subtle with it, until it is healed.
If you see that the abscess, redness, pain, swelling, and all the other afflictions have completely disappeared, and you no longer need plaster and bandage, then treat it again as you did at first, equally.
If nothing of what we have said happens to the limb, then do not loosen it until after three, or four, or five, or seven days. And when it has been left for twenty days, according to what appears to you from the loosening of the limb, as we have said, until when the fracture adheres and the flesh's coagulation approaches it: then add even more to your original bandage and also increase the dressing of the weak nourishment, according to the previous mention of it.
If, however, you see the fracture site already drying out and weakening more than necessary, then know that nourishment is prevented from reaching it. Therefore, apply warm water to it every time you loosen it, and let it be there for all three days, and lessen the bandage somewhat.
Through this operation, therefore, nourishment flows to the limb, and it heals quickly.
However, what foolish restorers do in the case of a broken limb a second time, when it is not restored the first time as it should be, and is restored according to a twisting, is an error in their practice and a great fear.  And if it were correct, the ancients would have written about it in their books, and would have practiced it.
And I have seen no trace of it at all in those texts, and it is better not to do it.
Concerning the accidental injury to the head.
The types of fracture are diverse, and its shapes are numerous; and its causes are distinct.
For there is a fracture that occurs from a blow with a sword; and the blow removes the bone completely as far as it reaches the siphac, which is beneath the bone, just as an axe does in wood; and because of that, this type of fracture is called Axial.
Or the sword cuts some part of the bone, and cuts its surface, and the incision does not penetrate to its end; and this type of fracture is called Kalaan, that is, a lifting absolutely.
And it is a wound of two fractures, either large or small.
And there is a fracture, which is a crushing or abrasion; and its cause is a blow with a stone, or a fall upon a stone, or similar things.
And this fracture is also either penetrating, which is now approaching the membrane that is under the bone, or it is on the surface of the bone; and the wound of these two fractures is also either small or large.
And there is a fracture, which is hidden within the bone to the fineness of a hair; and it is a small fissure, and because of that this type of fracture is called Capillaris.
And of these is a fracture that happens from a fall, or a blow of a stone, and similar things, which cause a lamina of the bone to enter the interior, and a hollow space is made: just as happens with metal cauldrons, when a blow strikes them, and a part of them enters the interior.
And most certainly this happens in the heads of moist bones, such as the heads of children.
And all these types of fractures are dealt with separately, and I will come with a remembrance of it all in its place.
And we know all these types of fractures by probing upon them, and investigating them with probes, and removing the corrupted flesh which is upon them.
Indeed, the Capillary type is known by this, that it is detected upon the bone, and is scraped clean, and is smeared upon it with ink: for the fracture appears black.
In the treatment of a fracture, first consider the patient's symptoms: then, if you see from the symptoms that it signifies clearly an impending fear, such as vomiting of bile, extension, and loss of understanding, and loss of voice, and fainting spells, and acute fever, and protrusion of the eyes, and redness of them, and similar symptoms in various conditions, undoubtedly it will not be cured: and if you see symptoms that do not terrify you, and you hope for recovery, then begin its treatment.
That is, if a wounded person comes to you immediately after being wounded, and it is during the winter days, then it is necessary that you strive in the removal of the bone before the fourteenth day according to every arrangement.
And if it is during the summer days, then it is necessary that you hasten to remove the bone before the seventh day, before that which is under the bone from the membrane is corrupted, and those accidents that we have recounted occur.
If the fracture of the bone has already reached the membrane touching the brain, and is with fragments and grinding, then it is necessary to cut the broken and ground part, according to what I tell you.
And it is to shave the head of the wounded person, and expose the bone in whatever way is possible for you, and according to the shape of the wound, and what is easy for the patient.
If during the exposure of the bone, a flow of blood or a hot abscess occurs, then apply to it what is necessary. And it is to fill the place with cloths soaked in wine and rose oil, and to keep the wound at rest until the abscess subsides, and you are secure from the flow of blood. Then begin in the treatment of the bone, and the removal of it.
And that is done according to one of two methods.
One method is to cut the bone with a knife of sharp edge; and this is its form.
Then apply another knife, a little wider than the first knife; and this is its form.
Then apply yet another knife, wider still than the second.
And the second method is that you should have a number of different knives, some wider than others, and some shorter than others; and let their edges be sharp, and let them be of iron or steel; and use ease in striking the knife, so that it does not strike the head, and injure it.
If however the bone is strong, hard: then it is necessary to pierce around it, before you apply the knives, with drills called *Terebra non profundantia*; and they are not called this, unless because they do not pierce through the boundary of the fleshy bone to that which is behind it; because the drill has a round end above that which is under its sharp head, similar to a margin, and a small circle prevents it from sinking into and piercing the thickness of the bone.
And it is fitting for you to take many of these drills, each one suited to the thickness of the bone, until you have a drill for every skull; whose sharp end's quantity in length and shortness should be according to the thickness of that skull.
And this is the form of three types of drills, or trepans.
The method of piercing around the fractured bone is: to place the drill upon the bone, and to revolve it with your fingers, until you know the bone has been pierced. Then change the drill to another place, and place one between each hole, the size of the thickness of a radius, or approximately that.
Then cut with the chisels what is between each hole from the bone, and make it thinner than you can over it in fine lightness, as we have said, until you eradicate the bone, either with your hand, or with some other of those instruments which you have prepared for it: such as pliers, and fine forceps.
And it is necessary that you guard against all caution, lest the drill, or chisel touch anything of the bone.
When therefore you have encircled the bone by piercing, and it has been separated from the bone, if it has been continuous with it, and you have drawn it out, it is necessary to smooth and make equal the extremity of that which remains in the bone with another instrument similar to a chisel. However, it should be thinner and more subtle than other chisels.
If, however, there remains anything of the small bones, and splinters, then take them away with ease with what you have of your instruments. Then treat the wound after that with the bandages and ointments that we have described.
And another method of operation is easy, entirely free from fear, which Galen said, and praised greatly: and this is his statement. You must first begin to expose the part of the bone where the fracture is more severe and obvious, until when you have exposed that part, and placed the end of this lenticular chisel upon it, whose form is this.
And the lenticular part cuts nothing smoothly; and its pointed part, running lengthwise along its two sides, as you see: so that the lenticular part rests against the bone, its pointed face striking the bone.
Then strike the chisel from one side with a small mallet, until you cut the entire bone easily, as it is loosened; and you should be assured by the bandage that no accident happens in it that you fear at all.
And if the surgeon were not more foolish than men, and more ignorant of it, nor were asleep, then if anything remained attached to the bone from the bandage in some places, he would cut it off with the end of its lenticular chisel, and free it from it easily. It is freed from it without harm, and without fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate to the bandage, and that which is cut away from the bone is its surface, and roughnesses and fine irregularities remain in it: then it is necessary that you smooth that roughness, and remove those irregularities with fine scrapers from which you have prepared a number of different sizes, so that it may be possible for you to administer from them in every suitable place those that are most appropriate and straightest, according to that to which the operation leads you, and the fractured bone itself, and its shape. And it is necessary that you administer in your judgment, by which you first scrape the bone, more broadly with those scrapers. Then use after it a finer one.
And it is necessary that you administer in your judgment, by which you first scrape the bone, more broadly with those scrapers. Then use after it a finer one. And do not cease to do this according to continuity, until you arrive at administering the thinnest and finest of them all.
In each of the remaining small fissures, and fine fractures, it is necessary to administer care, according to that which leads to its straightening.
And it is a matter not hidden above all to whomever has any study in this art, and has stood upon that which we have written and shown in the great fractures.
If, however, a flap of skin is retained when you remove the bone, then it is necessary that you take a linen cloth, according to the size of the wound, and immerse it in rose oil, and place it over the wound opening. Then take another cloth, doubled or tripled, and immerse it in wine and rose oil, and place it on the first cloth; and do this as easily as you can on top of it, so that it is not burdened by this.
Then administer a broad bandage over it; and do not tighten it, unless by an amount which only holds the wound together; and leave it with this treatment for one day, or two days, until you are certain of being free from a hot abscess. Then loosen it, and administer after it medicines with which superfluous dryness is overcome: such as lily roots, and herb flour, and frankincense powder, and birthwort, and similar things to them; and it is that you should make from these medicines a powder, which is pulverized over the wound as they are dry.
And universally administer in it any medicine of whose property it is to cleanse, and not to bite. And take care, according to your ability, that the wound in your treatment is clean; neither be there dirt of ointment, nor filth.
And take care, according to your ability, that the wound in your treatment is clean; neither be there dirt of ointment, nor filth. Nor allow any venom to collect in it at all; for when venom collects upon the brain membrane, it corrupts it, and putrifies it, and a great harm comes upon the sick person afterward; and this happens in the head disease, when the mouth is cleansed from it, especially when its care is neglected, blackness appears on its surface.
Therefore consider, if the accidents which we have mentioned occur to the sick person, know that it is undoubtedly harmful.
And if blackness does not occur, except from the medicine that is placed upon it, and it is in the power of that medicine to cause that blackness: then it is necessary that you take one part of honey, three parts of rose oil, and linen with them. Then place it upon the diseased area; afterward, treat it with the types of treatment that are appropriate, until it is healed.
Concerning the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that the nose is broken only when two of its upper parts are broken simultaneously, or one of them: because it is composed of two bones; since its lower part is cartilaginous, it is not broken, and it does not suffer except from abrasion, and twisting, and crookedness.
If one of its two parts is broken, then you should insert a small finger into the nostril, and straighten that fracture with your index finger and thumb on the outside, until the nose returns to its natural shape; and do this skillfully and easily. And be careful that your treatment does not cause pain to the sick person.
If the fracture is in the upper part of the nose, and the finger does not reach it, then it should be straightened with the end of a rod, on which a small thickness is placed.
If the fracture is in two parts, then do the same; and hasten its restoration on the first day of the fracture, if possible. And if not, then after the seventh or tenth day, with the rest of a hot abscess.
Then insert into the end of the nose a linen bandage, if the fracture is in one part; or insert two bandages if the fracture is in two parts; and let the thickness in the bandage be of such a quantity as to fill the nostril.
And some of the ancient restorers said that bandages should be soaked in butter and changed daily. However, that does not seem to me, rather it is necessary that the bandage be soaked in egg white, mixed with mill dust. Then leave the bandage until the bone is firm and the cartilage is hardened.
And sometimes a goose feather is inserted into the nose in place of a bandage, after a soft cloth is wrapped around it; and this is done so that it holds the fracture of the nose more firmly and so that the infirm person is not prevented from breathing.
And this is not necessary. If you wish, do it; and if you wish, do bandages.
If, while treating a hot abscess, the nose should again become affected, apply a plaster to the nose with cerote, or with cotton soaked in rose oil, or with a little diachylon.
If, however, a hot abscess does not occur, then it is necessary that you plaster it with sesame flour and powdered frankincense mixed with egg white. Then place soft lint over it, and do not bind the nose with anything at all.
If, however, the bones of the nose are broken or shattered, then it is necessary that you cut over them and extract them with instruments that are suited to them. Then suture the wound and treat it with whatever heals and consolidates it from suitable plasters or ointments.
If a wound occurs between the nostrils, then it is necessary to treat it with bandages and administer a lead cannula until it heals.
Concerning the restoration of the lower jaw, when it is broken.
When the lower jaw is broken, and its fracture is not with a wound: then consider if its fracture is only from the outside, and it is not broken in two, and bends inward: then its knowledge is easy.
Therefore, if the fracture is on its right side, insert the index finger of your left hand into the patient's mouth; similarly, if the fracture is on the left side of the jaw, then insert the index finger of your right hand; and with it, push the bend of the fracture from inside towards the outside with your other hand from the inside of the bone properly, and with it make it even.
If the fracture of the jaw is already separated into two pieces, then it is necessary to apply traction from both sides, according to its alignment, until its equalization is possible.
If however, it has already happened that the teeth are moved or broken: then bind those you hope to remain with gold, silver or silk thread. Then place a poultice on the fractured jaw. Then place a folded cloth over it, and place a large properly made compress over the cloth, or a piece of sole leather of equal length to the jaw.
Then bind it above, according to the manner in which its ligature is prepared and its application is suitable, until it is not broken; and command the sick person to use tranquility and quiet; and give him soft drinks as food.
If however, you estimate that something of its form has already changed in any way: then hurry and loosen it on the third day. Then rectify whatever is altered in it and plaster it with mill dust, with egg white, or with sesame flour, after you have removed the poultice; and place soft wool over the plaster.
While that plaster remains attached to it, and the position of the bone is not altered, leave it, do not loosen it, until it is healed, or the fracture is tightened. For this fracture is often tightened in three weeks.
If in the meantime a hot abscess occurs, then administer what we have said many times for its calming, until that abscess is removed.
And if the fracture is with a wound, consider. For if a piece is already separated from the bone, or from the splinters, then be subtle in this, that you remove those splinters with what suits you in removing them from the instruments.
If truly the wound opening is constricted, then enlarge it with a phlebotom knife according to the quantity of your need.
Then, when you have removed those splinters, and nothing remains of them, then suture the wound opening, if it is wide; and if not, place over it from plasters which suit it, and cause it to heal, until it is healed.
Concerning the restoration of the Furcula, when it is broken.
Indeed, the Furcula is most often broken on the anterior part, on the humerus side, and its fracture occurs according to one of three methods.
Either it breaks and separates into two, besides which splinters may occur in it; and this is easier to restore.
Or, such that splinters occur in the bone; and this is more difficult to restore.
Or, such that it is a fracture with a wound.
And the operation for it when the fracture is without a wound is that you should have two assistants present, so that one of them holds the support that follows the fractured Furcula, and the other extends the neck towards the part.
Then align the fracture with your fingers until its shape is as it should be, and there is neither a prominence nor a concavity in it.
If you need more extension, then you should place a sphere of cloth or wool under the patient's armpit, and its size should be according to your need; and extend and elevate the Furcula, and compress the sphere with your hand, until you align the fracture as it should be.
If you cannot draw the extremity of the Furcula outward because it has reached a deep position, then the patient should be placed on their neck; and place a pillow of medium size under their shoulder, and the assistant should press the shoulder downwards until the bone of the Furcula that is deep is raised upward; and then straighten the fracture and align it with your fingers.
If, however, you feel a splinter from the Furcula, and it moves, then you should cut above it and extract the splinter with ease.
If however it is retained in the bone, then make an incision into it with one of the scalpels you prepared for it, after you have placed under the Furcula the instrument that preserves the sifach: and the instrument is of wood or iron. And this is its shape.
It resembles a spoon that has no cavity.
And let its width be according to that which you need of it from the size of the bone, and its smallness. Its length, however, also according to what is possible for you in the operation.
And it should have two ends, as you see: and one end should be wide, and the other narrow.
If, however, the rupture of the wound that you made when you extracted the piece of bone is wide, and you secure it from a hot abscess: then bring together the two lips of the wound with sutures.
And if the rupture is small, or you fear a hot abscess: then fill the wound with cloths and powders according to the amount of the wound's fissure.
If a hot abscess should occur, then soak a cloth in rose oil, vinegar, and wine, and place it over it.
A constriction of the bone, however, when it is without a wound and without an incision, is such that you should place a plaster on the bone made from mill flour with egg white. And place soft lint over it. Then place a sphere beneath its protuberance, if you need that. Then take a himematem, that is, a wheel which the Saracens hold, which is very long, and its width is one palm, or about that. Then place a doubled pad over the lint and plaster. Then take a stick from a thin board, whose width is three fingers, and similarly in length. Then wrap it in cloth. Then wrap that cloth with the stick in the place of the alhimemati that falls on the fracture and place. Then place the alhimemati over the fracture as it revolves, and wrap it around its neck, and under its healthy and under its unhealthy titillico: and reduce it over every part, and as you see that the constriction holds the fracture in a proper constriction: and it is that which is not hidden from you, and everything, so that the stick is not removed from above the fractured bone. And therefore it is necessary that you observe the sick person every day. Therefore, whenever the ligament softens, and you see the stick already being removed, then straighten it and tighten the ligament.
Then place the sick person's sleep on their back, and place a small pillow under their side at their sleep, so that with it their support is raised from their side. Therefore, the fracture of the collarbone is lifted by lifting the shoulder.
Or tie his arm to his neck, and do not loosen the bandage if it does not happen in the place the accident happened because of itching, or from an abscess, up to twelve days.
Then renew the plaster, if you see his face towards it. And add to the constriction, and leave him until it is restored and congealed.
And indeed a fracture of the collarbone is tightened and strengthened in most cases within twenty-eight days; and sometimes it happens in certain people in a shorter time.
Concerning the restoration of a fracture of the scapula.
The scapula is rarely fractured, and in its broad area; and only its margins are broken from it.
Therefore, when a part of it is fractured, and it is fractured in its middle, and this is not recognized except by touch, then, according to the nature of the fracture, attempt its preparation. And restore it to its natural shape by all means of ingenuity possible to you.
Then place on the area mill dust with egg white, and soft linen, and place on top a small cushion made of doubled cloth. Then place on it a broad splint of thin wood, according to the size of the entire scapula, or a little larger.
If, however, there is a hollow under the splint in any part of the scapula, then fill that hollow with soft linen until the splint is level.
Then bind over it with a long bandage, with suitable constriction and firmness of the splint, until it does not move from its place.
And consider the bandage every day. Therefore, whenever the bandage softens, tighten it, and adjust the splint if it moves from its place; and let the sick person lie on their healthy side.
And the scapula is indeed restored in twenty days, or in twenty-five days.
When therefore this number is completed, then loosen the bandage; and you will be in safety. For it is from bones whose junction is not feared, nor their separation.
If however a piece of bone protrudes, and pierces under the skin: then cut above it, and remove it. And do what we said in the fracture of the furcula, from the subsidence of a hot abscess, if anything happens from it.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured breastbone.
The breastbone sometimes breaks in the middle of itself, and that rarely happens. Its extremities are indeed broken and crushed very much from its accidents.
When its middle breaks, inclining downward, and a violent pain, and difficulty in breathing, and a cough occur; and perhaps he vomits blood, and a cavity in the broken bone is revealed, and is not hidden from sense, its restoration is that the weak man should lie on his back, and place a bolster between his shoulder blades.
Then press his shoulders, and gather the ribs from both sides with your hands, and make them equal in their alignment, in every way possible and fitting, until the shape of the bone returns as it should.
Then place a plaster and a bandage over it, and place a splint of thin wood, or willow, or similar light materials, on top of it, after wrapping it in cloth. Then make it precise in tying it over the broken bone, so that it does not move: and proceed with the bandage around the back several times, and tighten it with a proper constriction.
Then check the bandage every hour, and whenever it loosens, tighten it. And if its necessity prompts its removal because of itching, or corrosion that occurs in the area, or pain, or abscess: then promptly untie it, and remove the plaster, and rectify what has happened with its method of treatment.
Then replace the plaster, if you see fit: and apply a bandage to it until it heals.
Concerning the restoration of Ribs, when they are broken.
Know that a rib fracture does not occur except in the thick areas that follow the spine, and from their extremities at the front. And attrition does not occur in them unless because they are cartilaginous. And its knowledge is not hidden from the sense in examination with the fingers.
And its restoration is done so that one aligns the fracture with the fingers according to the possible way, until the shape is equal, according to what is necessary. Then apply plasters, and bind the fractured bone with splints, if it needs it.
And if the fracture of the ribs is sloping inward, then a severe pain and a prickling sensation occur in the patient, like the prickling that occurs in someone with pleurisy, because the bone pricks the membrane, and he also experiences difficulty breathing, and coughing, and vomiting of much blood; and this is a difficult condition to treat.
And the ancients were ingenious in this, with many methods; and among them are those who say that you should give the patient foods that generate inflammation and windiness, so that the belly will become inflamed and distended, and the fracture will be pushed outward.
We, however, abhor that a rapid cause of a hot abscess be made if it does not already exist. If, however, it already exists, then add to it.
And some of them said: let a windy substance be placed over the affected area, then sucked with force, and this is a similar line of reasoning. However, it is feared that the windy substance will draw superfluities to the affected area, because of the weakened condition.
And some of them said: it is necessary that the area be covered with wool already soaked in hot oil; and cushions should be placed between the ribs until it is full, so that there is an even binding when you wrap it around the circumference.
Then the sick person should be treated with the treatment for pleurisy, through food and medicine.
If a violent condition constricts the patient, which he cannot tolerate, and the mouth is pricked or the covering is wounded by a puncture, and we fear for the patient, then it is necessary to cut over the area and expose the broken rib. Then let us place upon it the instrument that preserves the sifac, whose description preceded: and let us cut the bone with ease, and let us cut it.
Then let us bring together the two lips of the wound, if it is large, with sutures; and let us treat it with plasters until it is healed.
If, meanwhile, a hot abscess should occur, then hurry, and infuse poultices in rose oil, and place upon the area; and let the sick person be treated with what is appropriate for the abscess.
Meanwhile, let him also lie on the side on which he sleeps, until he is healed.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured spine of the back and neck.
A fracture of the neck bones sometimes occurs, and attrition rarely happens to them; and similarly to the vertebrae of the back as well. Therefore, when this happens to someone, and you wish to know whether he will be healed or not, then consider. For if you see his hands already softened, and numb, and dead; and he cannot move them, nor extend them, nor contract them; and when you grasp them with your fingers, and prick them with a needle, he does not feel it, and does not feel pain in them: then know that he is not healed, and in most cases is mortal.
If he moves them, and feels the contraction with fingers and the prick, then know that the neck bone is sound, and that the sick person will be healed with treatment.
If a similar affliction affects the vertebrae of the back, and you wish to know whether he will be healed or not, then consider his feet.  If you see that they are softened, and the same happens to them as to the hands; then, when he lies on his back, wind and excretion come forth involuntarily; and when he lies on his stomach, excretion comes forth involuntarily; and when he lies on his back and wants to urinate, and cannot: then know that it is fatal. Therefore, do not labor in his treatment.
If, however, nothing of that happens to him, the matter is less serious.
And the treatment for what happens from that is to try to soothe the hot abscess: thus, put rubbed rose oil on the vertebra alone; or with the yolks of roasted eggs.
Place that upon it several times a day, until the hot abscess is soothed; then place one of the strengthening, drying plasters over the place, and bind it with a bandage; and prescribe rest and tranquility to the sick person. And let him not sleep on the part where he finds pain until he is healed.
If, however, it happens that the bone is rubbed raw, or something has already separated from it, then it is necessary to cut over the skin and remove that. Then bring together the two edges of the wound, if it is large, with sutures. Then treat it with some healing ointment until it heals.
If, however, the last bone of the alhausos is broken, and is the end of the tail, then it is necessary to insert the thumb of the left hand into the anus, and to set the broken bone with the other hand, as far as possible, and with a second adjustment.
Then place over it, and remove it; and treat the wound with the preceding treatment, until it heals.
Concerning the restoration of the Anchae.
Chapter XI.
The bones of the Ancharum rarely break. If they do break, then their fracture is not, except for a shattering at their extremities; and they are split lengthwise, and are deflected inward; and pain occurs in the afflicted place, and a prickling; and the leg that breaks becomes numb.
And its restoration is indeed that you extend your hand over it, until you stand upon the fracture as its shape is.
If the fracture is only at its extremities, then make that fracture equal, according to what is prepared for you from the equalisation, until its shape is made similar to its natural shape.
If however the fracture is lengthwise, or already inclines inward, then make the infirm lie upon his stomach, until its fracture's restoration is prepared for you. When therefore you have made it equal, place a plaster over it; then place a splint of wood, or of leather, over it; and bind tightly above it, so that you need not fear the shifting of the fracture, nor the removal of the splint.
And make the concavity of them equal with that which fills it, until the binding takes it according to equality; and command the infirm person to sleep upon their back, or upon their healthy side.
If however a hot abscess occurs to him, then cease from its extension and its restoration, until the hot abscess subsides; and place upon it that which subsides it, according to what preceded; then return to its restoration; and bind it as is appropriate.
If however it happens in the bone of the satagie, or it is shattered, extract it.
Of the restoration of the fracture of the Adiutor.
The Adiutor is that which is between the elbow and the head of the Scapula.
If therefore it is broken, then its restoration is according to one of two methods.
One of which is: that you take a piece of wood of medium thickness, according to this form, which follows.
And two bandages are tied at the two extremities.
Then it is hung from a raised place, and the sick person sits on a seat. Then he shall place his broken arm upon the wood, until it reaches the notch, in the middle of which the curvature of the wood is attached.
Then suspend something heavy above it, so that the attendant extends it downwards. Then the physician shall set the fracture with his hands together, until the fracture returns to its proper place.
And another method is that the sick person be laid supine on their neck, and suspend their hand from their neck with a ligament. Then command two attendants, that one of them hold that which is above the fracture with their hands, and that the other hold below it, and that each of them extend it to their side.
And if you wish the extension to be stronger, then bind below the fracture with a ligament, and above it with another ligament, and let each of the two attendants extend them to their own sides.
If therefore the fracture is near the extremity of the humerus, then it is necessary that one ligament be placed below the armpit, and another below the fracture towards the elbow.
And similarly if the fracture is near the elbow, then it is necessary that a ligament be placed above that place, and above the elbow itself.
Then equalize the fracture with ease without exertion, until when the fracture is equalized as it should be, and a good application is applied; then bind it, if a hot abscess does not occur.
If however a hot abscess occurs, then leave the binding until the seventh day. And place over it wool cut into small pieces soaked in vinegar and rose oil, until when the abscess subsides; then bind it; and the method of binding it is that you place a plaster over the fracture.
Then place a bandage of new cloth over the plaster. Then let the arm be supported by the aid itself, and place his hand open over his shoulder.
And place a cloth, and bind the arm over the support, so that the arm stands in place like stars; if nothing hinders you from it, nor is anything from the broken bone disturbed upon you.
If you fear that something from the broken bone may be disturbed upon you, then use splints; and it is that you place a splint over the fracture itself which is wider and stronger than the other splints. And place between each splint the width of a finger; and let the length of the splints be according to the fracture with the addition of three fingers on every side.
Then bind over the splints the bandage which I mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is that your bandage should be tighter over the place of the fracture. And the more the fracture is extended, the smaller the bandage should be.
If therefore you see the placement of the splints and the bandage, as we have said, in the hour that you restore the limb immediately, then do so; and if you fear a hot abscess, then leave the bandage and splints until the seventh day, as we have said.
Then consider the ligament for all three days, that there not happen itching, or swelling, or the food be prevented from reaching the limb, on account of the disposition of superfluity of the bandage. Therefore rectify all that, according to what we have said.
If however you are confident that nothing will come from that: then do not loosen the bandage, unless after three days, or more.
And let the sleep of the infirm person be upon his back, and his hands upon his stomach: and place beneath a supporting mattress full of wool evenly.
And consider at every hour of night or day, that the broken limb shape is not loosened, or the bandage softened. Therefore rectify all that with your skill.
And give the patient food according to the order we have previously described, so that the food is initially light, until his mouth begins to be strengthened: then it is necessary to increase the thickness of his food.
For by custom, the healing of the leg and foot is that it is strengthened in forty days; then, therefore, it is necessary that it be loosened, and a bath administered, and it be medicated with ointments that rectify it.
If the fracture is foul, bruised: then do not loosen the bandages and splints from it until fifty days, and until two months.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, which are called two forearm bones.
One of which is small, and it is the one that follows the thumb. And the other is large, and it is the one that is situated beneath the small one, at its lower part.
Perhaps therefore only the larger forearm bone is broken, or the smaller one, and perhaps both are broken at the same time.
When therefore the smaller forearm bone is broken above, then its restoration is easy, and its healing is easier.
And when the forearm bone is broken below, its fracture is bad, and its healing is difficult.
And worse than that is when the two bones are broken simultaneously.
If therefore the bone that is broken is the smaller forearm bone above, then it is fitting for the physician, in its restoration, to apply a small extension with ease, until he has made it even.
If however the larger forearm bone is the one that is broken, then it is necessary that he apply a much stronger bandage.
And it is necessary that he apply a hand shape during its restoration, and a prolonged extension upon a cushion. And let the thumb of the hand be higher than all the fingers; and let the lower joint be below all the fingers, or the rest; and let the patient sit squarely upon himself, and let the cushion be before him, or directly in front of him, at a height such that the patient does not incur fatigue.
Then let the attendant extend the arm from the lower part, either with his hand or with a bandage. And another attendant extend it from above similarly.
Then let the physician equalize the bone until he restores it to the best shape he can.
If in the fracture of the bone there are fragments, then try to restore every fragment to its place by your skill.
If however a separate fragment appears in it, and it pierces the skin, and you have no hope of restoring it: then cut over it, and remove it according to the manner that we have said in those that have been previously mentioned.
If however the fracture is with a wound, I have already made a special chapter about it, therefore take the treatment from that.
If however a hot abscess occurs at the beginning of your restoration, then line a cloth with a cerate made from rose oil and white wax, and let it be medium between thickness and thinness: and bind the cloth over it with a thin bandage, until when the abscess subsides: then remove the cerate, and put a plaster prepared from mill powder with egg white. Then place splints, and let the splint placed over the fracture itself be somewhat wider and stronger.
And know that the number of splints for the arm is six in most arrangements, whether the fracture be in one or in two foci simultaneously.
Then apply your bandage to the site of the fracture more strongly and forcefully, and somewhat more with the bandage to the upper or lower part of the fracture: or apply a softer bandage to the one previously, according to what has preceded in the beginning of the chapter.
And let the cloths that wrap around the fracture be soft, moist cloths; and let them not be hard, rough. And let the thread with which it is tied be of linen proper, medium between thin and thick, as we have narrated.
And consider the limb and ligament after days. If anything happens that requires correction, such as itching that occurs in the limb, then it is necessary to embrocate the limb with heated water, until that itching ceases, and leave the limb unbandaged until it rests, then reapply the bandage.
If the bandage has already softened, and the bone has already been set, and similar things to these: then correct it all by your skill, and consider it again.
For if nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb, because of the superfluity of the bandage, then it is necessary to soften it a little, and leave it for a few days, until nourishment flows to it, then bandage it again.
If, however, nothing of what we have said happens to the sick person, then it is not necessary to loosen it, unless after twenty days, or about that many.
Then the sick person's hand should be suspended from his neck. And let his neck be level, and be guarded by his skill from violent movements. And have him sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture is restored in thirty days, or in thirty-two days: and perhaps it is restored in twenty-eight days. All that is according to the dispositions of complexions and the dispositions of strength.
Concerning the restoration of fractures of the Hand.
Know that fracture of the hand's pectines and the joints of the fingers rarely occurs. And it does not occur to them unless by very frequent friction.
Therefore, when a fracture or friction of the sole occurs, then it is necessary that the infirm person sit in a square position, and in front of him be a seat according to equality. Then place his hand upon it extended.
Then let the attendant extend the broken bones, and let the physician adjust them until they are joined with a good juncture.
Then it is necessary that he place a plaster and linen, if a hot abscess does not occur. Then place a bandage over it according to the size of the place, and you have already wrapped it in linen.
If, however, the fracture is near the lower part of the sole of the foot, then make a sphere out of cloth: and order the sick person to compress it with his broken foot: then tie it with a long cloth.
And let the bandage be of leather, in which there is gentleness, so that the leather is applied to the foot's body: and bind it, according to what is necessary.
If, however, the fracture is on the outside, then it is necessary that you place a bandage over it and another bandage from below on the sole: and let the hand be open and straight. Then place a bandage, as the hand is turned; and stretch it between the fingers with a bandage.
If a fracture occurs in any of the joints of the fingers, then if it is the thumb, it should be set according to what is necessary. Then it should be bound with the sole; and if you wish, put a small, straight bandage on it, so that the fracture is elevated and does not move.
If, however, the fracture is in the remaining fingers, such as the middle, index, ring, or little finger, then it should be set and bound to the healthy finger following it; or all should be bound according to their continuation; and that is better. Or place a small bandage on it, as we said for the thumb.
And consider in your restoration, and after the resolution of the hot abscess: and apply with it what is necessary, when anything happens from it, according to the repeated narration of it.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured hip.
The hip bone is frequently fractured, and is revealed to the senses; because it is turned towards the inner and posterior part.
And its restoration is done so that the ligament above the fracture is tightened, and another ligament below the fracture. And the patient lies on his face.
Then the attendant should extend each ligament on its own side equally; and this, when the fracture is in the middle of the bone.
However, if the fracture is near the root of the hip, then one ligament should be on the side of the crest, so that the extension falls upwards; and another ligament below the fracture.
And similarly if the fracture is near the knee, then let the ligament be near the knee, so that the extension is downwards.
Then the physician should align the bone with both his hands, until he restores it to the likeness of its natural shape; and the bone is joined with a good junction.
Then it is necessary that you apply a plaster and a bandage, if an abscess does not occur on the limb. But if an abscess occurs in it: then leave it for a few days, until the hot abscess subsides, then return to your treatment.
However, its bandage should be like this. It is necessary that you wrap a hard bandage over the fracture, that is, a bind; and double it twice, or three times; and let there be excess from it. Then wrap the leg, until the heel reaches the root of the buttocks.
And insert a long thread between the thigh and the leg near the knee below, and let the ends of the thread come from the upper part of the two parts. Then roll over the leg and thigh what is left over from the bandage.
Then place on the thigh, in the place of the fracture itself, splints, and place one splint on the bone of the leg.
Then fill the spaces between the leg and thigh with soft cloths, so that the tightness is equalized.
Then begin with a strong bandage from the middle over the place of the fracture, with three or four turns. And the further you extend the bandage from the fracture site, the smaller and softer and gentler your bandage should be.
Then pull at the two ends of the thread, which you inserted between the thigh and leg. Therefore tie together with both of them the three splints which are above. Then proceed with the two ends of the thread to the lower part, until you reach the ankle of the foot. And with both of them also tighten the ends of the splints on the other side, so that the bandage does not move from its place.
Then loosen the bandage over it, while it remains, so that no corruption, abscess, or swelling, and similar things, occur to the limb. If anything of that sort happens. Therefore, hasten with its removal, and rectify whatever has happened from it completely, according to what we have often said.
If, however, there is a piece of bone in the bone that pierces: then it is necessary, that you should smooth it, if it is possible for you. And if not, split over it, and remove it, and treat it with what was previously mentioned, until it is healed.
And sometimes this fracture of the hip is bound, in addition to which splints are added to it, just as we said in the discussion of the arm and shoulder.
However, in its restoration, thus fractured, lameness does not befall the sick person. And if it is restored alone, in addition to which the other leg is applied to it, then it is necessary that the one to whom it belongs should always limp.
And know that the hip is bound in fifty days, or a little is added, or a little is diminished: all of that is according to the diversity of complexions and the other dispositions.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured kneecap.
Know that a fractured kneecap rarely occurs, but it often experiences attrition.
If, however, a fracture does occur, then it is only either a fissure or a hole in its parts. And it is both with a wound and without a wound. And it stands over all of it with sensation.
And its restoration is such that the parts that have been disrupted are brought into alignment with the fingers, until they are joined together as much as possible through alignment and ease and workmanship.
Then place a poultice of sesame flour, or mill flour, with egg white; and place a round pad on top of it, if you need one; and bind a suitable bandage over it.
Then consider all the treatments we have described in other fractures, such as a hot abscess and similar conditions, in order to apply the appropriate remedy for each complication until it is healed.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured leg.
Know that the leg has two bones, one of which is thick and is called the Cruris, and the other thin, and is called the Focile.
And fractures occur in both of these bones in the same ways as fractures of the two arm bones; and therefore its restoration is performed similarly to the restoration of the arm, and the procedure is the same.
If the two bones are fractured simultaneously, the leg is twisted in all directions.
And if the thinner bone is fractured, the leg is twisted towards the front.
And if the thicker bone is fractured, you find it lower; and it is among those that are not hidden from you.
Then administer extension, and alignment, and the ligation of splints according to that standard.
However, if the fracture of the leg is a foul fracture, having much blood: let the extension be less, and your relief gentler.
