And in the leg indeed there is something more of the operation than in the arm: and it is, that when you align the splints and complete all your operation, then take two strips of pinewood, which are used in making roofing that is placed under the boards; and they are made from the blades of palm trees; and choose those which have a small thickness, and let them not be thin; and let their length be according to the length of the leg from the knee downwards.
Then wrap each one in a cloth with two wraps along its length. And place one part of the leg on one side, and the other on the other side: and let them be from the knee down to the foot.
Then tie the two strips in three places, from the two ends and the middle. For when the constriction is forbidden, the leg deviates to the right and left, and stands in good position.
And now indeed, let a cradle of wood be administered along the length of the leg, and let the leg be placed in it, so that it may preserve it from movement.
And indeed it is most necessary to do this in a fracture when there is a wound with it properly.
Then consider the leg every day, and strive with utmost care against any abscess, or swelling, or any other ailments. If therefore any of those things occur, then oppose it with whatever is appropriate, until it is healed, if God wills.
Of the fracture of the bones of the foot and toes.
Indeed, a fracture of the heel bone does not occur completely.
However, fractures of the bones of the foot sometimes occur, and fractures of the toes also occur rarely; and they do not occur except in most cases from abrasion.
If a fracture of the bones of the foot occurs, and you see those bones rising up against each other, then the sick person should place their foot on prepared ground, just as when walking.
Then you stand and place your foot upon that which is raised from those bones; afterwards, press or step on it, and straighten it until it returns to its place.
Then place a plaster, and linen above it; and put a small board beneath the sole of the foot, with two flat ends.
Then bind it with a suitable bandage, after you have wrapped it with cloths, and whatever else it needs.
When therefore three or four days have passed, then loosen the bandage. For you will find the bones straightened, whether they were broken or dislocated. Therefore, the operation is on them, as you see.
If, however, any fingers are broken, then restore them and straighten them according to what I have told you in the restoration of hand fingers. Then place a splint along the length of the finger, and let it be a little wider than it; then place under the foot this board that I have told you of, and bind it with a suitable bandage.
If, however, what is broken of the bones is two, or three, or more, then place a splint of its size over each finger, wrapped in linen cloth; and bind the board at the bottom of the foot; and let it stand on one end of the board outside the sole of the foot, so that it holds firmly.
And it befits you, lest you forget, to consider all that I have said to you concerning the remaining fractures from accidents that we have discussed, and to apply every accident with what is appropriate.
Of the fracture of a woman's vulva, and the breastbone, and a man's penis.
When a woman's vulva is fractured, then make her sit square. Then incline her slightly towards her back, and let her be held back.
Then let the midwife fill her vulva with wool, until she fills it; and let it become like a sphere in the vulva.
Then the woman should be shaken, and her back raised gradually. For that wool will come out, until it becomes like a sphere before the gate of the vulva, and the fractured opening returns.
Then place a cushion on her back. Therefore, when she wants to urinate, remove the wool with ease, then let her urinate; and return it according to the manner in which you first introduced it; then let her return to her original cushion.
Let her do this for seven days, or thereabouts, for the opening itself is restored.
And if you wish, take a sheep's bladder, and tie a reed cannula over its opening, and introduce the whole bladder into her vulva. Then blow into the cannula with force, until the bladder within the vulva is inflated, for the fracture returns.
Then let it be filled with wool, and let it remain for several days, according to what we have narrated, until it is healed.
And when the bone of the pubis of a man, or woman, is fractured, then administer in its restoration, and its equalization, that which we have narrated concerning the bone of the hip.
And the rectum is not concealed in these near fractures, which rarely occur, especially in those who have some skill or practice.
And understand my book well, for the fracture itself indicates to you, in many situations, the right way to restore it and to constrict it: therefore understand.
But in the penis of a man, when it is broken, take the throat of a goose, and insert the penis into it. Then place upon it a wrapping of cloth, and let it be wrapped, and let it be left for three days, or thereabouts, and it will be healed, if God wills.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured bone, when it is with a wound.
It is fitting for him who has suffered a fracture with a wound, especially if it is large, such as the hip bone, or the thigh bone, or similar to it, that you hurry, and immediately bleed him: if the conditions for bleeding are apparent to you, as we have previously stated.
If the wound flows blood, then it is necessary that you hurry to cauterize it, so that you powder the crushed zegi upon it: if you do not have another present.
Then begin to restore the fracture on that same day: and do not postpone it, if there is no abscess.
However, if a hot abscess occurs, then postpone its restoration until the ninth day, until the abscess subsides. And do not approach it on the third and fourth days at all, for you would cause him to suffer bad accidents.
If, however, the fractured bone is prominent above the skin, then it is necessary that you try to reduce it, and to align it with your hands with ease and slight extension.
If, however, its extension and alignment with your hands is not prepared, then reduce it with this instrument. And it is an instrument made of iron, whose length is the quantity of seven or eight fingers, and its width is according to the quantity of the wound. And for that, it is necessary for the physician to take from them three or four, according to the quantity he needs for that treatment in every kind of fracture.
And let it be round, and let it have a small thickness, so that it does not double under compression upon it at the time of the operation; and let it have sharp edges on its ends. And let its top be of a thickness from its middle to its lower part.
And this is its shape.
It is necessary in this operation that its sharp curved end be placed upon the prominent end of the bone; and drive it with it in one turn until when the bone returns and is made equal by a certain equalization.
Therefore, try to make the fractured ends equal to each other.
If its fractured ends are thin, and the instrument does not receive it in a good manner, then it is necessary that you cut the end of that bone until the instrument has power over it.
If however you cannot reduce them with what we have narrated at all, then cut it with what suits it from the incisions that we have described; or saw it with one of the saws in whatever way is possible for you.
Then scrape that which remains on the bone from roughness and small splinters.
When therefore the bone is reduced, and the infirm person finds after its reduction a severe, hurting pain, then know that the bone has not returned to its natural place.
Therefore, do that. For you confer great aid on the infirm person, when your restoration of the bone is completed.
Then immerse a cloth in astringent, black wine. And properly if it is in summer. And do not place a poultice over the wound, nor anything in which there is oil, lest it cause putrefaction and corruption in it.
Then administer splints at the hour of completion of your restoration of the bone. And leave the wound uncovered, so that you make an incision with scissors in the dressings according to the size of the wound.
And beware with all caution, lest you bind the wound with the fracture. For many times foolish physicians have done this: and they have caused upon their sick either death, or decay, or a discharge of pus, that is, of matter. And let your constriction be soft, gentle, different from the constriction of the other fractures.
If the wound is bad, or is a large wound, and you fear some bad accidents which we have mentioned concerning it, and find pain suspended in the place, then you should not place splints over it. And make for them wrappings from hard cloths in the place of splints, and bind it with them.
When, however, a day or two has passed, and you see the wound already beginning to generate pus, then remove from over it the cloth which you placed over it with wine. Then administer bandages and ointments with which it is customary for us to treat wounds, such as tetrapharmacon ointment, and similar ones.
And it is fitting for you to loosen the bandage. And consider the wound every day morning and evening, until it is consolidated and healed.
And it is necessary that the limb be prepared in such a way that pus runs from it downwards with ease.
If many days pass over a wound, and it does not consolidate, nor is pus separated from it, then know that there are small bone splinters there. Therefore, it is necessary that you probe the wound with probes. And if any of those splinters are separated, remove them and extract them. And those of them that are not separated, and pierce the limb, and cause pain to occur, try to cut them out, and remove them by any method possible to you.
If the wound suffers from any sort of ulcer, or festering, or other form of corruption and putrefaction, then it is necessary to apply to each of them the appropriate treatment from the preceding discussion in its chapter.
And concerning that in which my words should stand, and to which you should give your immediate attention when a bone is broken and protrudes from the limb, such as the hip bone, and the kneecap, and similar large bones, it is not to presume to draw it back, for often death occurs from this; rather, let it be, until it becomes rotten.
For perhaps it will fall away by itself after twenty or thirty days.
Then, if it seems to you, treat the wound; and if not, leave it.
Concerning the treatment of swelling at the site of a fracture.
This swelling often happens at the site of healing from a fracture. Especially those near the joints; and the shape of the limb is disfigured by it. And perhaps it prevents the limb from its natural function.
Therefore, if the swelling is recent, administer to it medicines that have a styptic effect, such as aloe, frankincense, myrrh, sarcocolla, acacia, and similar to them.
So that you may take some, or all of them, and mix them with astringent wine, or with egg white, or with vinegar; and place them upon the swelling on linen, and bind them firmly over it.
And leave the bandage, and do not loosen it for many days. Then loosen it, and apply another, until the swelling is removed.
Or bind over it a lead sheet properly. For lead has the property of removing everything that is feared from the limbs.
However, if the swelling has already hardened into a stone, and it is tight, and necessity quickly compels its removal: then split it from above, and cut away the projecting excess; or scrape it with certain scrapers, until it is removed; and treat it until it is healed.
Concerning the treatment when it is restored, and the limb remains afterward delicate, not according to its nature.
When a fractured bone is restored, and the limb remains afterward delicate, weak: then that happens not only for many reasons. Among which one is the multitude of loosening of the ligament and its ligation not according to what is proper; or because of an excess of constriction of the ligaments, until the nourishment is prohibited from running to the limb; or because of a multitude of unnecessary embrocation at an improper time; or because of a scarcity of blood in the body of the weak person, and his weakness.
And the treatment of that is to nourish the weak person, and to make his body tender, until the blood is multiplied in him. And administer a bath. And introduce joy and gladness upon him.
Then put pitch on the limb, so that the pitch attracts much nourishment to it. And apply diligently embrocation with warm water, until the nourishment flows, and it returns to its natural shape.
Concerning the treatment of fractured bones when they are restored crookedly.
When, after the limb's healing, a crookedness, and protrusion of the fractured bone, or a knot, occurs in a limb already restored, and its natural form is disfigured, yet the limb is not hindered from its natural function, then it is not necessary to heed the opinion of one who believes that the limb should be broken again.
And there have already been many foolish physicians and restorers who have done that in our land.
And this operation is indeed highly reprehensible, leading to death, or great fear, so that it may be deadly.
But if the lameness and knotting are recent, then it is necessary that it be soaked in warm water, in which softening herbs have been boiled, such as mallow leaves, and its root, and melilot, and similar things. And let a plaster be made with a softening plaster, such as a well-made diachylon.
Or let the mucilage of the mallow root be taken, and be beaten with hen's fat, or sesame oil: and let it be plastered with it.
Or let fat figs be taken, and ground with pigeon dung, and similar things to them which are called from medicines lessening consolidation.
And sometimes the knotting is loosened with assiduous, long, and gentle rubbing, which is done with wet hands. And let movement of the limb be administered to every part at all hours.
But if twisting has already preceded, and become strong, and has been turned into stone, and necessity has been provoked to cure it with iron: then it is necessary that it be cut above it, and the continuity of the bone be loosened, and that which is superfluous from the knotting or bone be cut with very fine incisions: and let ease be administered to it with care and diligence.
Of speaking in Dislocation.
Dislocation is the exit of any joint from its place. Therefore it is prohibited from its movement, and the figure of the limb is disfigured. And it causes great pain and severe injuries to the afflicted.
Therefore, when dislocation occurs to someone, then it is necessary to hasten its restoration; nor should one delay it at all.
For if it is delayed, the place becomes suppurated, and perhaps the reduction of the dislocation along with it.
Therefore, it is not appropriate to move or extend it during the time of its suppuration. Because it often causes spasm and painful injuries.
But when that occurs, then it is necessary to hasten to phlebotomy of the infirm. Then let it be left until the abscess subsides.
Then the limb should be bathed with warm water and oil. Then it should be reduced with ease.
And every limb should be treated with that whose memory comes into its place.
I have indeed already arranged the chapters on dislocations, even as preceded in fractures, from the upper part of the body down to its lower part.
Concerning the treatment of the dislocation of the lower jaw.
The two lower jaws are rarely dislocated except rarely.
And the dislocation of both occurs according to one of two ways. For they are either removed from their places by a small removal, and softened for a little while. Or they are dislocated by a complete, full dislocation, until they are softened to the chest, until the patient's saliva runs and he cannot hold it, nor can he close his mouth, and his tongue becomes entangled with his speech.
When, however, the dislocation is small, then it returns in many ways by itself with little effort.
If, however, the dislocation is complete, full: then it is necessary that its reduction be administered quickly, and not be delayed at all: and it is that the attendant hold the patient's head, and the physician insert the thumb of one hand into the root of the dislocated jaw between his teeth, if the dislocation is on one side. Or let him insert two thumbs of his simultaneously, if the dislocation is on two sides. And the other fingers of his hands be outside, with which he balances.
And command the patient to cleanse his jaws and free them to chew on every side. And the physician shall align the jaw, and press the jaw, until it returns to its place.
If its reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are locked together, then administer vaporization with hot water and oil until the reduction of both becomes easy: nor delay the reduction of both at all, as we said.
When therefore they return and are aligned, and the patient's mouth is closed and does not soften, then place cushions of cloth upon them, with cerate made from wax and rose oil.
Then it shall be bound with ease with soft bandages.
And let the patient sleep on their back, and let their head be supported between two pillows, so that they do not move it to the right and left, nor attempt or be forced to chew anything: rather, let their food be administered by gentle sipping, until when the pains are removed, and the jaw is bound, then let them eat what they wish, and let it be administered with ease.
Let them not exert themselves with the operation of their mouth with eating and drinking, and yawning, until the jaw is bound and healed.
If the reduction of the two jaws becomes difficult, when they are dislocated at any hour, and do not return to their places, then often fevers and persistent sweating occur, and perhaps the patient's bowels are loosened, and perhaps they vomit pure bile.
Therefore, when you see this, then know that it is a disaster, and often the one who experiences this dies within ten days.
Concerning the reduction of the dislocation of the collarbone and the shoulder extremity.
The collarbone indeed does not dislocate from the inner side, because of its connection with the chest. And it sometimes dislocates outward, and that is evident to the senses.
And its restoration is that the sick person lies on their back, and extends their arms. Then compress the place with the force of your palm, for it will return.
Then place a plaster and bandages over it, and bind it tightly.
However, its extremities that follow the elbow, and are continuous with it, are not dislocated, except rarely. If, however, it is dislocated on any day: then it is necessary that it be reduced and straightened according to what we have said, and as is prepared for you.
Then place a plaster and bandages over it, and bind it tightly. And advise the sick person to adhere to rest and quiet until healed.
And with this same treatment, the humerus extremity is reduced when it is also separated from its place.
Concerning the reduction of the humerus dislocation.
Know that the humerus is not dislocated except according to three ways.
One of which is that it is dislocated to the side of the armpit downwards. The second is that it is dislocated to the side of the chest. And perhaps it is dislocated to the upper part of the humerus: and that happens rarely.
And it is not dislocated to the rear, because of the shoulder blade. And it is not dislocated to the front, because of the nerves.
And very often it is dislocated and exits downwards towards the armpit, especially in those whose flesh is scant. Because it exits quickly in them, and enters quickly.
However, in those whose flesh is abundant, it is the opposite, namely because it exits with difficulty, and enters with difficulty.
And perhaps in some men, through a blow or fall, the humerus becomes inflamed with a hot abscess, and it is thought because of this, that it is already dislocated.
Therefore, it is necessary that you test it until you stand upon its certainty, and then proceed in its cure.
And dislocation is recognized when it is turned towards the armpit: and distinguish between a dislocated humerus and a healthy humerus. For you find a clear difference among them, and you find a concavity in the head of the humerus, and under the armpit, upon touching, the head of the humerus seems like an egg itself; and the sick person cannot bring the hand to their ear, nor move it through all motions.
And similarly, if it is dislocated towards the chest or upwards, then you find a clear touch, and it is not hidden.
And indeed, a simple reduction of this dislocation is possible when it is recent or the patient is a child.
And its reduction is for the attendant to raise the hand upwards. Then place your thumbs under their armpit, and raise the joint with strength upwards to its place; and the attendant raise the hand, and extend it upwards. Then let him place it below, for it will return quickly.
However, if it does not return by what we have said, and many days have passed since the dislocation occurred, then it is necessary that the patient be bathed in warm water, and an embrocation be administered which softens and soothes, such as if roots of marshmallow, fenugreek, and melilot are boiled in water, and administered.
Then the patient should be laid on their back; and a sphere and wool, which is equal between softness and tightness, should be placed under their armpit.
Then the physician should place his heel on the sphere, and raise the head of the humerus with strength, and pull the patient's hand downwards.
And if you wish, reduce it according to this method: and it is so that you should have a man present who is taller than the patient, and stand on the side, and insert his arm under the patient's armpit, and raise the armpit upwards, until the patient is suspended in the air. And another attendant should pull the patient's hand downwards towards their abdomen. If however the patient is light, then it is necessary that something else be suspended with him, so that it weighs him down; for the dislocation returns immediately.
And it is also reduced according to another method: and it is that a long piece of wood should be fixed in the ground, whose head is round in shape, like a mortar's sleeve; which should not be thick, nor thin. Then, after soft cloths are placed over the head of the wood, the patient is placed beneath it, standing along the length of the wood.
Then his hand should be extended downwards on one side, and his body extended on the other side with strength; for the joint returns to its place quickly.
If its reduction is difficult by all that we have said, then use this treatment: and it is that you should take wood whose length is the amount of two arms, and its width the amount of four fingers, and its thickness the amount of two fingers; to which there should be a round head, so that its entrance into the depth of the socket is eased, according to this shape.
Then soft cloths are bound over the round head, so that the wood does not injure the patient.
Then, place it under the patient's socket, so that the hand or arm is extended downwards along the wood; and the wood is bound to the support, arm, and hand's extremity.
Then place the arm on a step of a ladder, widthwise, and extend the hand downwards, and let the rest of the body hang suspended on the other side. The joint immediately enters.
When therefore its entry is completed in whatever manner possible, then it is necessary to place a sphere of equal size made of wool beneath the socket. Then place the prepared plaster, made from mill dust with frankincense and egg white, over the entire shoulder, as it revolves from above.
Then bind a suitable bandage beneath the socket, and rotate the bandage over the plaster from above; and bind his hand to his neck, and let it hang, and do not move his hand for seven days.
And it is necessary that little food be given to the weak one, until the limb is strengthened; because this is quicker for its healing.
Then, after seven or five days, a larger one is placed, and it is exercised with movement. If therefore it is strengthened and does not soften, then it is already healed.
If, however, the joint is dislocated many times, because of the moisture that occurs in it, or because of some other cause: then it is necessary that a cautery with three openings be applied to it, according to what has been said in the chapter on cauteries.
If all this is done, and the ligament is loosened after seven days, and the joint is not strengthened, and the plaster and constriction are applied to it repeatedly, and it is not strengthened, and it falls, and it cannot raise itself upwards: then know that the nerves belonging to the head of the humerus are now cut, or stretched, or softened: and know that the joint will never be fixed in its place.
However, the reduction of a dislocation towards the chest and nipple, or to the back, is done with pushing and pulling with the hands, until it returns, and further constriction and care are applied to it until it is healed.
If, after healing, heaviness occurs in the limb, and slowness in its movement, then let the patient use a bath frequently until that heaviness is lessened, and it returns to its original nature.
On the treatment of elbow dislocation.
Know that the elbow joint dislocates with difficulty, and similarly returns with difficulty.  Indeed, it dislocates in all directions, and especially forward or backward.
And its dislocation is not hidden from you, since it is apparent to the sight and touch, regardless of the manner in which it is dislocated. And when you join the dislocated elbow with the healthy one, that is made manifest to you. For the joint is hollowed out, and the arm cannot double itself, nor touch its shoulder.
And it is necessary that you hasten and reduce the dislocation immediately, before a hot abscess occurs to it.
For if a hot abscess occurs to it, its reduction becomes difficult; and perhaps it will not be completely healed, especially when it is towards the back. For it is worse than all types of dislocations, and causes them more intense pain; and often death descends with it.
And its restoration, when it is of those that can be reduced, is that his hand be extended with two hands; and his arm be spread out, and the physician's hands be above the elbow and below it, and he impel the joint with the thumbs of his hands together, or with the root of his palm, until it returns to its place.
Or truly the dislocation is towards the front; then it returns thus, that he double his hand with his hand, until he strike with the root of his palm the elbow which is dislocated.
If the dislocation does not yield to reduction, then administer a very forceful, strong extension; and it is that two assistants extend the arm, and two assistants also hold it, so that it is not moved during the extension.
Then the arm is to be revolved in every direction after a long doubled cloth, or a wide bandage, is wrapped around his hand.
And when the physician touches the joint with his hand, let him anoint it with oil, so that it may be of those things which aid in the lubrication of the joint with ease. Then he should drive the joint with a forceful impulse, until it returns.
And after its reduction, it is necessary that a plaster be placed upon it, in which there is astringency and desiccation with egg white; and it should be bound with a suitable bandage. And let the arm be suspended from the patient's neck, and leave it for several days.
Then it should be released. If therefore the joint is strengthened in its place, then release the bandage from it, and leave it.
And if you see the joint not being properly bound, then reapply the plaster and bandage, and leave it for several days until it is bound. Then release it.
If however, after the reduction of the joint, heaviness occurs to it, and its disposition is in motion: then administer moistening in bathing, and gentle friction, and compression, until it is calmed, or place over the joint the fat of a ram's rump. Then it should be bandaged, and leave it over it day and night. Then remove it, and immerse it in a bath. When it sweats, then rub the joint with moderate friction. Then repeat over it in the manner of the poultice, once, and twice, and thrice, with each bath entry, until it is soothed.
And if you wish, place upon it cow dung, moist, heated with butter, and bind over it.
Do that several times. For thus it is soothed, and returns to its proper state.
Concerning the treatment of dislocation of the Almahassem.
The joint between the arm and hand, the Mahassem, is frequently dislocated.
And the reduction of its dislocation is easy. Unlike other joints. However, speed is required in reducing its dislocation in the hour in which it is dislocated, before the place becomes suppurated; or a hot abscess occurs there.
And the reduction of the dislocation is to place the weak hand's Mahassem on a board, and the attendant extend his hand; and the physician place his palm on the prominence of the joint, and push it until it returns.
However, it is necessary to consider if the palm has already receded to the back of the hand; then the infirm place the back of his hand on the board for extension and reduction; and if the dislocation is proceeding toward the back of the hand, then place his hand's inside upon the board, that is, the inner part of his hand be placed on the board, so that the physician's hand falls upon the very prominence of the joint.
If therefore it returns immediately, it is good; and if not, then bind it with a soothing plaster for the abscess: and leave it, and do not repeat it.
For if it does not tolerate, nor can you effect its reduction after days have passed, but the joint remains according to its twisting; and it causes no harm to the infirm person, except that the hand is softened, and cannot grasp anything; then know that the nerves are severed, or bruised.
Therefore, there is no remedy for it, except to cauterize it near the place of its injury. And perhaps that will not help anything.
When therefore Almahassem does not return, then place upon it the plaster, which we have described. Then let it be bound, and let it be left for five days; afterwards let it be loosened and let it be left, and let the hand be exercised.
If however its movement is deprived, and something serious happens to it: then gently warm it with heated water, and rub it several times, until it is soothed.
Concerning the treatment of the dislocation of the Fingers.
When a finger is dislocated to any side, or a finger is dislocated to the back of the palm, or its front: then extend the finger, and push the dislocation with your thumbs until it returns.
Then support the head of the finger, and suspend it towards the part to which it is dislocated, and leave it for two days.
Then suspend it, and extend it, until it is straightened in its own day.
When therefore it is night, bind it according to the narration itself: and do not cease to loosen it in the day: and exercise it with movement: and bind it at night.
Do that for a few days, until it is strong.
And similarly do with it, if it is dislocated towards the palm of the hand: and bind it to the part itself, and perform your first operation on it, until it is healed.
And similarly do with it when it is dislocated to the other parts.
Concerning the treatment of the dislocation of the spine of the back.
When it happens to someone that the spine of the back, or the neck, is completely dislocated, or many vertebrae are removed from their places, then there is no cure for it, because death hastens for the sick person.
And the sign of that is that the excretion of the sick person comes out without his will, nor can he hold it: and many times certain members of him are softened from it, or his two legs, or his two arms, or one of them; or one vertebra is removed from its place.
For often it is removed, for often their removal happens in four directions.
Therefore, that which is removed to the back is called a hump.
However, the treatment of it is, that you should consider then, if a hump occurs from childhood: then there is no treatment for it, nor is there any cure from it at all.
However, in that which occurs from a fall, or a blow, or similar things to those, the ancients have already described methods of treatment in lengthy language. Of which most often there is no benefit.
I, however, have now abbreviated it, from that, of which little excuses itself from much of its proximity to my intention: and by my exposition, which I have set forth, and have fashioned its instrument in a different manner from that in which they showed it, and exposed it.
I say therefore, that in a hump which occurs on the front part, there is no remedy, nor cure from it.
And similarly in that which occurs on both sides also.
And it is not cured from them, unless that which occurs on the back properly, with that which I relate: and it is, that the sick person be stretched out upon their face upon an equal platform near a wall: and damp straw be spread beneath it, so that their chest is not harmed.
Then let a straight piece of wood be fixed in a hole in the ground, at the end of the platform, approximately at its head; and another piece of wood approximately at its feet at the other end of the platform; and let an attendant hold the wood, and it not be firmly fixed in the hole. And another attendant hold the other.
Then wrap around the head of the sick person, and under their armpits a soft, firm bandage: and extend the end of the bandage to the wood that is at their head: and tie it to it.
Then bind another bandage above their elbows, and above their knees, and at their ankles. Then gather all the bandages together, and tie them to the other piece of wood that is at their feet.
Then let each attendant stretch the bandage towards the piece of wood, and do not remove the two pieces of wood from the places where they are fixed: but let them not be firmly held, as we have said.
And let the physician place his palms upon the vertebra with strength, until it returns to place; or let him place a board upon it. Then he binds his feet to the board, until it returns to place.
If, however, it does not return through this treatment, then take a board whose length is approximately three arms' lengths. And hollow out a space in the wall, of which we said that it is near the sick person, for the end of the board to enter. Then place the middle of the board on the vertebra. And let the physician place his feet on the other end. And tie it tightly until the vertebra is compressed, and returns to its place.
Then if you wish, work with a turning tool that is grasped by your hand: and it is so that you fix two pieces of wood, each of the length of one arm, in the earth at the head of the sick person on the furthest part of the footstool, and two pieces of wood at his head on the furthest part of the footstool.
And let the distance between each piece of wood be one palm; and now a hole has been made in every place through which the turning tool passes; and let every piece of wood be firmly fixed in the earth so that it does not move at all.
And let a round piece of wood be inserted; and the turning tool is in it, around which the bandage revolves in the two holes of the two pieces of wood.
And at its extremity is a hole in which a piece of wood is fixed, whose length is the same as that which revolves; and in the other two pieces of wood is similarly one of the same.
Then tighten the bandages that you have applied to the chest of the sick person with the turning tool which is at his head; and the bandages that you have applied to his legs with the turning tool which is at his feet.
Then let a servant stand by each turning tool, who shall turn his hand with the cord, with which the turning tool revolves; and the physician shall adjust the Spine, as we have said.
This is the form of the turning tool, which follows.
Then, after the Spine is restored and the place is levelled, then it is necessary that you place a drying plaster made from egg white. Then, a bandage. Then place on top of the plaster a small board, whose width is three fingers, or approximately that. And its length is the amount that covers the place of the Hump, and over the healthy part of the Spine.
Then it is bound with the bandage we spoke of. And let the sick person use fine nourishment until he is healed.
If something of prominence remains in the place at the end of the healing: then it is necessary that medicines that soften and soothe be administered, with the administration of the board we have described, for a long time. And now a lead plate is administered to it.
And sometimes a prominence occurs in the last vertebra of the back, and it is thought to be a dislocation, and that is the bone which now protrudes. Therefore it is not appropriate to presume to treat it with this medicine, for it might perhaps cause death.
Concerning the treatment of a dislocated Ancha.
Know that the joint of the Ancha, and the joint of the humerus, do not suffer anything but dislocation alone; nor do they suffer what happens to other joints from a slight separation and concavity.
And indeed the joint of the Ancha is dislocated according to four parts. That is, because it is dislocated forward and backward.
And it is most often dislocated inwards; and it is slightly dislocated forward and backward.
And the treatment of its dislocation inwards is: because when you join the weak leg to the healthy one, it is longer; and its knee is more prominent than the healthy one, and the weak one cannot bend its foot at the groin; and there is a location that follows the groin, ulcerated with a hot ulcer, because the head of the thigh has already reached there.
And the sign of him who suffers dislocation outwards is that its accidents are contrary to these accidents.
And the sign of him who suffers dislocation forward is that he stretches the healthy leg to its full extent; however, he does not bend it without pain which is in the knee; and if he attempts to walk, he cannot step forward; and his urine is retained, and his groin is ulcerated, and in walking his heel is on the heel.
And the sign of him who suffers dislocation backward is that he does not extend his knee, nor can he bend it before bending his groin; and his leg is also shorter than the other; and his groin is softened, and the head of the thigh is at the location of the ilium.
The reduction of this dislocation, however, is such that you must then consider, if the dislocation is old, which has already grown old with the person who has it. Then if you administer its reduction, and it remains in its condition: then there is no cure in it at all. Therefore, it is not necessary for a complete presumption to be made on him.
However, in the one whose dislocation is recent, and is from one of the four types of dislocation, then hasten to this, so that you wish to join the joint and extend it inwardly and outwardly, and move it right and left; for perhaps it returns, and does not require further treatment.
If, however, it does not return in this way, then it is necessary that you prepare a strong assistant, and he extend his leg from the lower part; either with his hands, or with a bandage, which is tied above his leg above the knee; and another assistant extend his hand from above, so that he inserts his hands under his kneecaps. Then he shall tighten with a gentle bandage above the root of the thigh, and hold the end of the other bandage.
And let his extension be either from the anterior part of the groin to the part of the crotch, or from the posterior to the part of the back.
And let the extension of all of them be at the same time, until the sick person is lifted with his body from the earth, and remains hanging.
And this species of extension is a common species of the four species.
If therefore the dislocation returns with what we have said, it is good; and if not, then it is necessary for every species that I shall say about its own cure.
In the reduction of it indeed, when the dislocation is inward, it is necessary that the sick person be laid on his healthy side. Then he should place a bandage on the root of the thigh, in that which is between the head of the thigh and the place that is beneath the groin. Then extend the bandage upward from the groin region to the higher part of the body, to the area of the crotch.
Then let another strong attendant take his arms, and reduce the large part of the sick person's thigh, and extend it outward with forceful traction. For now it returns to its place.
And this type is easier than the other types of treatment, by which this limb is reduced.
However, if it is resisted by you, and does not obey you for entry with this type of treatment at all: then it is necessary that the two feet of the sick person be tied together with a strong, gentle bandage over the two heels, and over the two knees: and the length of each one from its counterpart shall be the quantity of four fingers: and let the sick leg be extended more than the other by the quantity of two fingers.
Then let the sick person be suspended above a wooden beam, which is in the house: and let its length from the ground be the quantity of two arms.
Then command a strong young man to secure the head of the thigh: and let him be suspended with the sick person by another young man: and let another young man, securing the thigh with strength, propel it, for the joint returns to its place with speed.
In its own return, however, when the dislocation is outward, it is necessary that the sick person lie on a platform, according to what we have described in the case of a hunchback. And a bandage is to be tied over his sick leg, properly, and over his chest.
Then let two pieces of wood be placed, one at his feet, and the other at his head. Then let a piece of wood be placed in the middle of the platform, securely fastened; upon which let moist cloths be wrapped, so that it does not harm the sick person; and let the wood be between his thighs, so that it is not drawn downwards during extension.
Then let every attendant pull to his side, and let the physician equalize the reduction with his hand.
If therefore it obeys the return, it will be good.
And if not, place it upon the board itself, and press it, just as we said in the case of a hump, evenly. However, it is necessary that the sick person lie on his healthy side.
And when the dislocation is towards the front, then it is necessary that the sick person's leg be pulled quickly; and it is according to this same account on a platform; and let the physician place the palm of his right hand on the sick person's groin. Then he tightens it with the other hand, and with that tightening, places it extended downwards to the part of the knee.
And if the dislocation is towards the back, then it is not necessary that the sick person be stretched downwards and raised above the ground; rather, it is necessary that he be placed on a hard object, as is also necessary if his hip is dislocated outwards, according to what we said about his lying on two hips; and it is on his face, and the ligaments extended, according to what we said recently.
And it is necessary that compression be administered with a board also over the place to which the joint emerges, when the reduction is completed as is necessary.
And the sign of its return is not hidden from you: and it is, that you extend the two legs of the sick person; and when you see them equal, and the sick person tightens his leg, and expands it without excuse, then know that the limb has now returned as is necessary; then join the two hips, and apply a plaster, and bind it with a bandage, that is, a roller: so that the hip is not moved to any part; and let the sick person adhere to rest for three days, or four.
Then untie the bandage and the plaster, and compare the leg with the other; then if you see them equal in status, then know that the dislocation is now firm; therefore dismiss the sick person to walk.
And if you see any softening, then return, and plaster it, and bind it according to your first binding; and leave it also for three days.
Then untie it and make him/her slow in walking on both sides for some days, until it is strengthened well.
Concerning the dislocation of the Knee.
The knee is dislocated in three ways. It is dislocated outwards, inwards, and downwards, that is, backwards.
And it is not dislocated forwards.
And a sign of its dislocation is that you instruct the sick person to apply their leg to their hip. If however it does not adhere with the hip, then know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration of all the ways of its dislocation is that you make the sick person sit upright, so that if possible he extends his leg over it, and adheres your back to his face, and place his foot between your feet.
Then make his knee adhere with your palms, and straighten them between your fingers over his knee. Then apply with your palm the two sides of his knee with force. And another attendant should extend his foot until the knee returns to its place.
And the sign of its return is that the leg adheres to the thigh with ease, not with effort.
Then bandage it, and make the leg adhere to the thigh. Then bind them together with a bandage for three or four days. Then loosen them.
And let the sick person not use walking for some days, until he is healed.
If its reduction is refused, then administer a strong extension with bandages, whose description preceded in the treatment of the hip, until it returns.
Concerning the treatment of the dislocation of the Calcaneus.
The calcaneus is sometimes removed with a small displacement, and sometimes dislocated completely. And its dislocation occurs either inwards or outwards.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you see the Calcaneus swollen, protruding towards the side to which it is dislocated.
The treatment of its removal is easy by returning it: and it is that it be extended easily with the hands, and straightened, until it returns.
In the treatment however, when it is dislocated completely, it is necessary that you have the sick person sit upright; and let a very strong assistant hold him from the back of his back, in the middle of it.
Then you hold his foot with your right hand from its upper part, and with your left hand from the lower part of his foot at the ankle joint.
Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand. Then with the left hand twice.
Then extend with the left, and drive the foot's front with the right towards the leg without exertion.
Do that twice, as we have narrated.
Then push the foot's front to the leg for the third time, and you pull the heel.
If therefore it returns in one time, or in two according to this method, and you see the foot levelled, it is good; and if not, then repeat the operation on it, because it returns.
If however its reduction is prohibited to you with what we have narrated, then make the sick person lie on his back on the ground, and fix a firmly planted stake in the ground, so that it falls between his thighs; and you have already wrapped a cloth around it, so that it does not harm the sick person.
Then let the attendant take his thigh; afterwards, let another attendant stretch the foot, either with their hands or with a bandage that ties to the foot's ankle. Then let every attendant extend against the extension of their counterpart; and let the stake stand between the sick person's two thighs, holding him, so that his body is not pulled downwards during the extension.
Then the physician should adjust the dislocation with his hands, and another attendant should hold the healthy leg downwards. For the dislocation returns with speed.
Therefore, when the dislocation returns, and its return's health is evident to you, then apply a plaster and lint, and tightly bind it with bandages, and tie the foot to the leg with a bandage.
And it is necessary to be cautious or fearful of the nerves located above the posterior heel, so that the bandage is not too tight on them and injures them.
Then leave it for two or three days. If the bandage softens, tighten it; then loosen it on the third or fourth day; and prohibit the sick person from walking for forty days. If, however, he attempts to walk before that period, there is no certainty that the dislocation will not be dislodged, and be corrupted, and not receive subsequent healing.
If a hot abscess occurs, then it is necessary to apply to it the treatment described in another place in our previous discussion, and a poultice, until it is removed.
Concerning the dislocation of the toes of the foot, and its treatment.
It is necessary to align what is dislocated from them with a slight extension, without effort; and indeed this is not difficult, but rather easy.
If, however, the dislocation is in some nodes of the foot's back, then it is necessary that the patient sit his foot upon a level place, or upon a board; and that he stand as if walking.
Then you shall stand and place your foot upon that which projects from those joints. Then press it with your foot with force, until it returns, and you see it now aligned, and no projection appears in the place.
Then place under the sole of his foot a board that encompasses the entire foot, which has two ends. Then bind it with a suitable bandage for three days. Then loosen it, and keep him from walking for many days, until it is strengthened and secured from reduction.
On the types of dislocation with wound or fracture.
When something of this sort occurs, and you attempt its cure and restoration, often death results; and on account of this, it is not fitting that anyone should presume to treat such a case unless he is skilled in the art through long experience, compassionate, pious, slow, not bold, nor presumptuous; and that he should use in the beginning medicaments which soothe only hot abscesses; and he should leave the sick person to divine judgment. Unless you see, or hope for, salvation from perdition with the alleviation of the illness, and in which something of hope appears. Therefore, attempt its reduction immediately at the beginning of the matter, before a hot abscess occurs.
If, therefore, the limb returns as we wish, then administer a regimen that will soothe the abscess, and treat the wound with whatever appropriate desiccant ointments.
If the dislocation is with a fracture, and separate bone fragments occur, then attempt the removal of them, and do in that what we have said in simple illnesses from what preceded in their places.
And beware according to your ability, and guard yourself from entering the path of fear, according to what my teaching has preceded for you.
For that is better for you before your Creator, and more enduring for your glory, and more beneficial to your body among those in your time.
